CHAPTER 1


I scarcely know where to begin, though I sometimes facetiously place  — 
cause of it all to Charley Furuseth’s credit.  He kept a summer cottage
in Mill Valley, under  —  shadow of Mount Tamalpais, and never occupied
it except when he loafed through  —  winter months and read Nietzsche and
Schopenhauer to rest his brain.  When summer came on, he elected to sweat
out a hot and dusty existence in  —  city and to toil incessantly.  Had
it not been my custom to run up to see him every Saturday afternoon and
to stop over till Monday morning, this particular January Monday morning
would not have found me afloat on San Francisco Bay.

Not but that I was afloat in a safe craft, for  —  _Martinez_ was a new
ferry-steamer, making her fourth or fifth trip on  —  run between
Sausalito and San Francisco.   —  danger lay in  —  heavy fog which
blanketed  —  bay, and of which, as a landsman, I had little
apprehension.  In fact, I remember  —  placid exaltation with which I
took up my position on  —  forward upper deck, directly beneath  — 
pilot-house, and allowed  —  mystery of  —  fog to lay hold of my
imagination.  A fresh breeze was blowing, and for a time I was alone in
 —  moist obscurity—yet not alone, for I was dimly conscious of  — 
presence of  —  pilot, and of what I took to be  —  captain, in  —  glass
house above my head.

I remember thinking how comfortable it was, this division of labour which
made it unnecessary for me to study fogs, winds, tides, and navigation,
in order to visit my friend who lived across an arm of  —  sea.  It was
good that men should be specialists, I mused.   —  peculiar knowledge of
 —  pilot and captain sufficed for many thousands of people who knew no
more of  —  sea and navigation than I knew.  On  —  o — r hand, instead
of having to devote my energy to  —  learning of a multitude of things, I
concentrated it upon a few particular things, such as, for instance,  — 
analysis of Poe’s place in American literature—an essay of mine, by  — 
way, in  —  current _Atlantic_.  Coming aboard, as I passed through  — 
cabin, I had noticed with greedy eyes a stout gentleman reading  — 
_Atlantic_, which was open at my very essay.  And  — re it was again,  — 
division of labour,  —  special knowledge of  —  pilot and captain which
permitted  —  stout gentleman to read my special knowledge on Poe while
 — y carried him safely from Sausalito to San Francisco.

A red-faced man, slamming  —  cabin door behind him and stumping out on
 —  deck, interrupted my reflections, though I made a mental note of  — 
topic for use in a projected essay which I had thought of calling “ — 
Necessity for Freedom: A Plea for  —  Artist.”   —  red-faced man shot a
glance up at  —  pilot-house, gazed around at  —  fog, stumped across  — 
deck and back (he evidently had artificial legs), and stood still by my
side, legs wide apart, and with an expression of keen enjoyment on his
face.  I was not wrong when I decided that his days had been spent on  — 
sea.

“It’s nasty wea — r like this here that turns heads grey before  — ir
time,” he said, with a nod toward  —  pilot-house.

“I had not thought  — re was any particular strain,” I answered.  “It
seems as simple as A, B, C.   — y know  —  direction by compass,  — 
distance, and  —  speed.  I should not call it anything more than
ma — matical certainty.”

“Strain!” he snorted.  “Simple as A, B, C!  Ma — matical certainty!”

He seemed to brace himself up and lean backward against  —  air as he
stared at me.  “How about this here tide that’s rushin’ out through  — 
Golden Gate?” he demanded, or bellowed, ra — r.  “How fast is she ebbin’?
What’s  —  drift, eh?  Listen to that, will you?  A bell-buoy, and we’re
a-top of it!  See ’em alterin’  —  course!”

From out of  —  fog came  —  mournful tolling of a bell, and I could see
 —  pilot turning  —  wheel with great rapidity.   —  bell, which had
seemed straight ahead, was now sounding from  —  side.  Our own whistle
was blowing hoarsely, and from time to time  —  sound of o — r whistles
came to us from out of  —  fog.

“That’s a ferry-boat of some sort,”  —  new-comer said, indicating a
whistle off to  —  right.  “And  — re!  D’ye hear that?  Blown by mouth.
Some scow schooner, most likely.  Better watch out, Mr. Schooner-man.
Ah, I thought so.  Now hell’s a poppin’ for somebody!”

 —  unseen ferry-boat was blowing blast after blast, and  —  mouth-blown
horn was tooting in terror-stricken fashion.

“And now  — y’re payin’  — ir respects to each o — r and tryin’ to get
clear,”  —  red-faced man went on, as  —  hurried whistling ceased.

His face was shining, his eyes flashing with excitement as he translated
into articulate language  —  speech of  —  horns and sirens.  “That’s a
steam-siren a-goin’ it over  — re to  —  left.  And you hear that fellow
with a frog in his throat—a steam schooner as near as I can judge,
crawlin’ in from  —  Heads against  —  tide.”

A shrill little whistle, piping as if gone mad, came from directly ahead
and from very near at hand.  Gongs sounded on  —  _Martinez_.  Our
paddle-wheels stopped,  — ir pulsing beat died away, and  — n  — y
started again.   —  shrill little whistle, like  —  chirping of a cricket
amid  —  cries of great beasts, shot through  —  fog from more to  — 
side and swiftly grew faint and fainter.  I looked to my companion for
enlightenment.

“One of  — m dare-devil launches,” he said.  “I almost wish we’d sunk
him,  —  little rip!   — y’re  —  cause of more trouble.  And what good
are  — y?  Any jackass gets aboard one and runs it from hell to
breakfast, blowin’ his whistle to beat  —  band and tellin’  —  rest of
 —  world to look out for him, because he’s comin’ and can’t look out for
himself!  Because he’s comin’!  And you’ve got to look out, too!  Right
of way!  Common decency!   — y don’t know  —  meanin’ of it!”

I felt quite amused at his unwarranted choler, and while he stumped
indignantly up and down I fell to dwelling upon  —  romance of  —  fog.
And romantic it certainly was— —  fog, like  —  grey shadow of infinite
mystery, brooding over  —  whirling speck of earth; and men, mere motes
of light and sparkle, cursed with an insane relish for work, riding  — ir
steeds of wood and steel through  —  heart of  —  mystery, groping  — ir
way blindly through  —  Unseen, and clamouring and clanging in confident
speech  —  while  — ir hearts are heavy with incertitude and fear.

 —  voice of my companion brought me back to myself with a laugh.  I too
had been groping and floundering,  —  while I thought I rode clear-eyed
through  —  mystery.

“Hello! somebody comin’ our way,” he was saying.  “And d’ye hear that?
He’s comin’ fast.  Walking right along.  Guess he don’t hear us yet.
Wind’s in wrong direction.”

 —  fresh breeze was blowing right down upon us, and I could hear  — 
whistle plainly, off to one side and a little ahead.

“Ferry-boat?” I asked.

He nodded,  — n added, “Or he wouldn’t be keepin’ up such a clip.”  He
gave a short chuckle.  “ — y’re gettin’ anxious up  — re.”

I glanced up.   —  captain had thrust his head and shoulders out of  — 
pilot-house, and was staring intently into  —  fog as though by sheer
force of will he could penetrate it.  His face was anxious, as was  — 
face of my companion, who had stumped over to  —  rail and was gazing
with a like intentness in  —  direction of  —  invisible danger.

 — n everything happened, and with inconceivable rapidity.   —  fog
seemed to break away as though split by a wedge, and  —  bow of a
steamboat emerged, trailing fog-wreaths on ei — r side like seaweed on
 —  snout of Leviathan.  I could see  —  pilot-house and a white-bearded
man leaning partly out of it, on his elbows.  He was clad in a blue
uniform, and I remember noting how trim and quiet he was.  His quietness,
under  —  circumstances, was terrible.  He accepted Destiny, marched hand
in hand with it, and coolly measured  —  stroke.  As he leaned  — re, he
ran a calm and speculative eye over us, as though to determine  — 
precise point of  —  collision, and took no notice whatever when our
pilot, white with rage, shouted, “Now you’ve done it!”

On looking back, I realize that  —  remark was too obvious to make
rejoinder necessary.

“Grab hold of something and hang on,”  —  red-faced man said to me.  All
his bluster had gone, and he seemed to have caught  —  contagion of
preternatural calm.  “And listen to  —  women scream,” he said
grimly—almost bitterly, I thought, as though he had been through  — 
experience before.

 —  vessels came toge — r before I could follow his advice.  We must have
been struck squarely amidships, for I saw nothing,  —  strange steamboat
having passed beyond my line of vision.   —  _Martinez_ heeled over,
sharply, and  — re was a crashing and rending of timber.  I was thrown
flat on  —  wet deck, and before I could scramble to my feet I heard  — 
scream of  —  women.  This it was, I am certain,— —  most indescribable
of blood-curdling sounds,—that threw me into a panic.  I remembered  — 
life-preservers stored in  —  cabin, but was met at  —  door and swept
backward by a wild rush of men and women.  What happened in  —  next few
minutes I do not recollect, though I have a clear remembrance of pulling
down life-preservers from  —  overhead racks, while  —  red-faced man
fastened  — m about  —  bodies of an hysterical group of women.  This
memory is as distinct and sharp as that of any picture I have seen.  It
is a picture, and I can see it now,— —  jagged edges of  —  hole in  — 
side of  —  cabin, through which  —  grey fog swirled and eddied;  — 
empty upholstered seats, littered with all  —  evidences of sudden
flight, such as packages, hand satchels, umbrellas, and wraps;  —  stout
gentleman who had been reading my essay, encased in cork and canvas,  — 
magazine still in his hand, and asking me with monotonous insistence if I
thought  — re was any danger;  —  red-faced man, stumping gallantly
around on his artificial legs and buckling life-preservers on all comers;
and finally,  —  screaming bedlam of women.

This it was,  —  screaming of  —  women, that most tried my nerves.  It
must have tried, too,  —  nerves of  —  red-faced man, for I have ano — r
picture which will never fade from my mind.   —  stout gentleman is
stuffing  —  magazine into his overcoat pocket and looking on curiously.
A tangled mass of women, with drawn, white faces and open mouths, is
shrieking like a chorus of lost souls; and  —  red-faced man, his face
now purplish with wrath, and with arms extended overhead as in  —  act of
hurling thunderbolts, is shouting, “Shut up!  Oh, shut up!”

I remember  —  scene impelled me to sudden laughter, and in  —  next
instant I realized I was becoming hysterical myself; for  — se were women
of my own kind, like my mo — r and sisters, with  —  fear of death upon
 — m and unwilling to die.  And I remember that  —  sounds  — y made
reminded me of  —  squealing of pigs under  —  knife of  —  butcher, and
I was struck with horror at  —  vividness of  —  analogy.   — se women,
capable of  —  most sublime emotions, of  —  tenderest sympathies, were
open-mou — d and screaming.   — y wanted to live,  — y were helpless,
like rats in a trap, and  — y screamed.

 —  horror of it drove me out on deck.  I was feeling sick and squeamish,
and sat down on a bench.  In a hazy way I saw and heard men rushing and
shouting as  — y strove to lower  —  boats.  It was just as I had read
descriptions of such scenes in books.   —  tackles jammed.  Nothing
worked.  One boat lowered away with  —  plugs out, filled with women and
children and  — n with water, and capsized.  Ano — r boat had been
lowered by one end, and still hung in  —  tackle by  —  o — r end, where
it had been abandoned.  Nothing was to be seen of  —  strange steamboat
which had caused  —  disaster, though I heard men saying that she would
undoubtedly send boats to our assistance.

I descended to  —  lower deck.   —  _Martinez_ was sinking fast, for  — 
water was very near.  Numbers of  —  passengers were leaping overboard.
O — rs, in  —  water, were clamouring to be taken aboard again.  No one
heeded  — m.  A cry arose that we were sinking.  I was seized by  — 
consequent panic, and went over  —  side in a surge of bodies.  How I
went over I do not know, though I did know, and instantly, why those in
 —  water were so desirous of getting back on  —  steamer.   —  water was
cold—so cold that it was painful.   —  pang, as I plunged into it, was as
quick and sharp as that of fire.  It bit to  —  marrow.  It was like  — 
grip of death.  I gasped with  —  anguish and shock of it, filling my
lungs before  —  life-preserver popped me to  —  surface.   —  taste of
 —  salt was strong in my mouth, and I was strangling with  —  acrid
stuff in my throat and lungs.

But it was  —  cold that was most distressing.  I felt that I could
survive but a few minutes.  People were struggling and floundering in  — 
water about me.  I could hear  — m crying out to one ano — r.  And I
heard, also,  —  sound of oars.  Evidently  —  strange steamboat had
lowered its boats.  As  —  time went by I marvelled that I was still
alive.  I had no sensation whatever in my lower limbs, while a chilling
numbness was wrapping about my heart and creeping into it.  Small waves,
with spiteful foaming crests, continually broke over me and into my
mouth, sending me off into more strangling paroxysms.

 —  noises grew indistinct, though I heard a final and despairing chorus
of screams in  —  distance, and knew that  —  _Martinez_ had gone down.
Later,—how much later I have no knowledge,—I came to myself with a start
of fear.  I was alone.  I could hear no calls or cries—only  —  sound of
 —  waves, made weirdly hollow and reverberant by  —  fog.  A panic in a
crowd, which partakes of a sort of community of interest, is not so
terrible as a panic when one is by oneself; and such a panic I now
suffered.  Whi — r was I drifting?   —  red-faced man had said that  — 
tide was ebbing through  —  Golden Gate.  Was I,  — n, being carried out
to sea?  And  —  life-preserver in which I floated?  Was it not liable to
go to pieces at any moment?  I had heard of such things being made of
paper and hollow rushes which quickly became saturated and lost all
buoyancy.  And I could not swim a stroke.  And I was alone, floating,
apparently, in  —  midst of a grey primordial vastness.  I confess that a
madness seized me, that I shrieked aloud as  —  women had shrieked, and
beat  —  water with my numb hands.

How long this lasted I have no conception, for a blankness intervened, of
which I remember no more than one remembers of troubled and painful
sleep.  When I aroused, it was as after centuries of time; and I saw,
almost above me and emerging from  —  fog,  —  bow of a vessel, and three
triangular sails, each shrewdly lapping  —  o — r and filled with wind.
Where  —  bow cut  —  water  — re was a great foaming and gurgling, and I
seemed directly in its path.  I tried to cry out, but was too exhausted.
 —  bow plunged down, just missing me and sending a swash of water clear
over my head.   — n  —  long, black side of  —  vessel began slipping
past, so near that I could have touched it with my hands.  I tried to
reach it, in a mad resolve to claw into  —  wood with my nails, but my
arms were heavy and lifeless.  Again I strove to call out, but made no
sound.

 —  stern of  —  vessel shot by, dropping, as it did so, into a hollow
between  —  waves; and I caught a glimpse of a man standing at  —  wheel,
and of ano — r man who seemed to be doing little else than smoke a cigar.
I saw  —  smoke issuing from his lips as he slowly turned his head and
glanced out over  —  water in my direction.  It was a careless,
unpremeditated glance, one of those haphazard things men do when  — y
have no immediate call to do anything in particular, but act because  — y
are alive and must do something.

But life and death were in that glance.  I could see  —  vessel being
swallowed up in  —  fog; I saw  —  back of  —  man at  —  wheel, and  — 
head of  —  o — r man turning, slowly turning, as his gaze struck  — 
water and casually lifted along it toward me.  His face wore an absent
expression, as of deep thought, and I became afraid that if his eyes did
light upon me he would never — less not see me.  But his eyes did light
upon me, and looked squarely into mine; and he did see me, for he sprang
to  —  wheel, thrusting  —  o — r man aside, and whirled it round and
round, hand over hand, at  —  same time shouting orders of some sort.
 —  vessel seemed to go off at a tangent to its former course and leapt
almost instantly from view into  —  fog.

I felt myself slipping into unconsciousness, and tried with all  —  power
of my will to fight above  —  suffocating blankness and darkness that was
rising around me.  A little later I heard  —  stroke of oars, growing
nearer and nearer, and  —  calls of a man.  When he was very near I heard
him crying, in vexed fashion, “Why in hell don’t you sing out?”  This
meant me, I thought, and  — n  —  blankness and darkness rose over me.




CHAPTER 2


I seemed swinging in a mighty rhythm through orbit vastness.  Sparkling
points of light spluttered and shot past me.   — y were stars, I knew,
and flaring comets, that peopled my flight among  —  suns.  As I reached
 —  limit of my swing and prepared to rush back on  —  counter swing, a
great gong struck and thundered.  For an immeasurable period, lapped in
 —  rippling of placid centuries, I enjoyed and pondered my tremendous
flight.

But a change came over  —  face of  —  dream, for a dream I told myself
it must be.  My rhythm grew shorter and shorter.  I was jerked from swing
to counter swing with irritating haste.  I could scarcely catch my
breath, so fiercely was I impelled through  —  heavens.   —  gong
thundered more frequently and more furiously.  I grew to await it with a
nameless dread.   — n it seemed as though I were being dragged over
rasping sands, white and hot in  —  sun.  This gave place to a sense of
intolerable anguish.  My skin was scorching in  —  torment of fire.   — 
gong clanged and knelled.   —  sparkling points of light flashed past me
in an interminable stream, as though  —  whole sidereal system were
dropping into  —  void.  I gasped, caught my breath painfully, and opened
my eyes.  Two men were kneeling beside me, working over me.  My mighty
rhythm was  —  lift and forward plunge of a ship on  —  sea.   — 
terrific gong was a frying-pan, hanging on  —  wall, that rattled and
clattered with each leap of  —  ship.   —  rasping, scorching sands were
a man’s hard hands chafing my naked chest.  I squirmed under  —  pain of
it, and half lifted my head.  My chest was raw and red, and I could see
tiny blood globules starting through  —  torn and inflamed cuticle.

“That’ll do, Yonson,” one of  —  men said.  “Carn’t yer see you’ve
bloomin’ well rubbed all  —  gent’s skin orf?”

 —  man addressed as Yonson, a man of  —  heavy Scandinavian type, ceased
chafing me, and arose awkwardly to his feet.   —  man who had spoken to
him was clearly a Cockney, with  —  clean lines and weakly pretty, almost
effeminate, face of  —  man who has absorbed  —  sound of Bow Bells with
his mo — r’s milk.  A draggled muslin cap on his head and a dirty
gunny-sack about his slim hips proclaimed him cook of  —  decidedly dirty
ship’s galley in which I found myself.

“An’ ’ow yer feelin’ now, sir?” he asked, with  —  subservient smirk
which comes only of generations of tip-seeking ancestors.

For reply, I twisted weakly into a sitting posture, and was helped by
Yonson to my feet.   —  rattle and bang of  —  frying-pan was grating
horribly on my nerves.  I could not collect my thoughts.  Clutching  — 
woodwork of  —  galley for support,—and I confess  —  grease with which
it was scummed put my teeth on edge,—I reached across a hot cooking-range
to  —  offending utensil, unhooked it, and wedged it securely into  — 
coal-box.

 —  cook grinned at my exhibition of nerves, and thrust into my hand a
steaming mug with an “’Ere, this’ll do yer good.”  It was a nauseous
mess,—ship’s coffee,—but  —  heat of it was revivifying.  Between gulps
of  —  molten stuff I glanced down at my raw and bleeding chest and
turned to  —  Scandinavian.

“Thank you, Mr. Yonson,” I said; “but don’t you think your measures were
ra — r heroic?”

It was because he understood  —  reproof of my action, ra — r than of my
words, that he held up his palm for inspection.  It was remarkably
calloused.  I passed my hand over  —  horny projections, and my teeth
went on edge once more from  —  horrible rasping sensation produced.

“My name is Johnson, not Yonson,” he said, in very good, though slow,
English, with no more than a shade of accent to it.

 — re was mild protest in his pale blue eyes, and withal a timid
frankness and manliness that quite won me to him.

“Thank you, Mr. Johnson,” I corrected, and reached out my hand for his.

He hesitated, awkward and bashful, shifted his weight from one leg to  — 
o — r,  — n blunderingly gripped my hand in a hearty shake.

“Have you any dry clo — s I may put on?” I asked  —  cook.

“Yes, sir,” he answered, with cheerful alacrity.  “I’ll run down an’ tyke
a look over my kit, if you’ve no objections, sir, to wearin’ my things.”

He dived out of  —  galley door, or glided ra — r, with a swiftness and
smoothness of gait that struck me as being not so much cat-like as oily.
In fact, this oiliness, or greasiness, as I was later to learn, was
probably  —  most salient expression of his personality.

“And where am I?” I asked Johnson, whom I took, and rightly, to be one of
 —  sailors.  “What vessel is this, and where is she bound?”

“Off  —  Farallones, heading about sou-west,” he answered, slowly and
methodically, as though groping for his best English, and rigidly
observing  —  order of my queries.  “ —  schooner _Ghost_, bound
seal-hunting to Japan.”

“And who is  —  captain?  I must see him as soon as I am dressed.”

Johnson looked puzzled and embarrassed.  He hesitated while he groped in
his vocabulary and framed a complete answer.  “ —  cap’n is Wolf Larsen,
or so men call him.  I never heard his o — r name.  But you better speak
soft with him.  He is mad this morning.   —  mate—”

But he did not finish.   —  cook had glided in.

“Better sling yer ’ook out of ’ere, Yonson,” he said.  “ —  old man’ll be
wantin’ yer on deck, an’ this ayn’t no d’y to fall foul of ’im.”

Johnson turned obediently to  —  door, at  —  same time, over  —  cook’s
shoulder, favouring me with an amazingly solemn and portentous wink as
though to emphasize his interrupted remark and  —  need for me to be
soft-spoken with  —  captain.

Hanging over  —  cook’s arm was a loose and crumpled array of
evil-looking and sour-smelling garments.

“ — y was put aw’y wet, sir,” he vouchsafed explanation.  “But you’ll
’ave to make  — m do till I dry yours out by  —  fire.”

Clinging to  —  woodwork, staggering with  —  roll of  —  ship, and aided
by  —  cook, I managed to slip into a rough woollen undershirt.  On  — 
instant my flesh was creeping and crawling from  —  harsh contact.  He
noticed my involuntary twitching and grimacing, and smirked:

“I only ’ope yer don’t ever ’ave to get used to such as that in this
life, ’cos you’ve got a bloomin’ soft skin, that you ’ave, more like a
lydy’s than any I know of.  I was bloomin’ well sure you was a gentleman
as soon as I set eyes on yer.”

I had taken a dislike to him at first, and as he helped to dress me this
dislike increased.   — re was something repulsive about his touch.  I
shrank from his hand; my flesh revolted.  And between this and  —  smells
arising from various pots boiling and bubbling on  —  galley fire, I was
in haste to get out into  —  fresh air.  Fur — r,  — re was  —  need of
seeing  —  captain about what arrangements could be made for getting me
ashore.

A cheap cotton shirt, with frayed collar and a bosom discoloured with
what I took to be ancient blood-stains, was put on me amid a running and
apologetic fire of comment.  A pair of workman’s brogans encased my feet,
and for trousers I was furnished with a pair of pale blue, washed-out
overalls, one leg of which was fully ten inches shorter than  —  o — r.
 —  abbreviated leg looked as though  —  devil had  — re clutched for  — 
Cockney’s soul and missed  —  shadow for  —  substance.

“And whom have I to thank for this kindness?” I asked, when I stood
completely arrayed, a tiny boy’s cap on my head, and for coat a dirty,
striped cotton jacket which ended at  —  small of my back and  —  sleeves
of which reached just below my elbows.

 —  cook drew himself up in a smugly humble fashion, a deprecating smirk
on his face.  Out of my experience with stewards on  —  Atlantic liners
at  —  end of  —  voyage, I could have sworn he was waiting for his tip.
From my fuller knowledge of  —  creature I now know that  —  posture was
unconscious.  An hereditary servility, no doubt, was responsible.

“Mugridge, sir,” he fawned, his effeminate features running into a greasy
smile.  “Thomas Mugridge, sir, an’ at yer service.”

“All right, Thomas,” I said.  “I shall not forget you—when my clo — s are
dry.”

A soft light suffused his face and his eyes glistened, as though
somewhere in  —  deeps of his being his ancestors had quickened and
stirred with dim memories of tips received in former lives.

“Thank you, sir,” he said, very gratefully and very humbly indeed.

Precisely in  —  way that  —  door slid back, he slid aside, and I
stepped out on deck.  I was still weak from my prolonged immersion.  A
puff of wind caught me,—and I staggered across  —  moving deck to a
corner of  —  cabin, to which I clung for support.   —  schooner, heeled
over far out from  —  perpendicular, was bowing and plunging into  — 
long Pacific roll.  If she were heading south-west as Johnson had said,
 —  wind,  — n, I calculated, was blowing nearly from  —  south.   —  fog
was gone, and in its place  —  sun sparkled crisply on  —  surface of  — 
water, I turned to  —  east, where I knew California must lie, but could
see nothing save low-lying fog-banks— —  same fog, doubtless, that had
brought about  —  disaster to  —  _Martinez_ and placed me in my present
situation.  To  —  north, and not far away, a group of naked rocks thrust
above  —  sea, on one of which I could distinguish a lighthouse.  In  — 
south-west, and almost in our course, I saw  —  pyramidal loom of some
vessel’s sails.

Having completed my survey of  —  horizon, I turned to my more immediate
surroundings.  My first thought was that a man who had come through a
collision and rubbed shoulders with death merited more attention than I
received.  Beyond a sailor at  —  wheel who stared curiously across  — 
top of  —  cabin, I attracted no notice whatever.

Everybody seemed interested in what was going on amid ships.   — re, on a
hatch, a large man was lying on his back.  He was fully clo — d, though
his shirt was ripped open in front.  Nothing was to be seen of his chest,
however, for it was covered with a mass of black hair, in appearance like
 —  furry coat of a dog.  His face and neck were hidden beneath a black
beard, intershot with grey, which would have been stiff and bushy had it
not been limp and draggled and dripping with water.  His eyes were
closed, and he was apparently unconscious; but his mouth was wide open,
his breast, heaving as though from suffocation as he laboured noisily for
breath.  A sailor, from time to time and quite methodically, as a matter
of routine, dropped a canvas bucket into  —  ocean at  —  end of a rope,
hauled it in hand under hand, and sluiced its contents over  —  prostrate
man.

Pacing back and forth  —  length of  —  hatchways and savagely chewing
 —  end of a cigar, was  —  man whose casual glance had rescued me from
 —  sea.  His height was probably five feet ten inches, or ten and a
half; but my first impression, or feel of  —  man, was not of this, but
of his strength.  And yet, while he was of massive build, with broad
shoulders and deep chest, I could not characterize his strength as
massive.  It was what might be termed a sinewy, knotty strength, of  — 
kind we ascribe to lean and wiry men, but which, in him, because of his
heavy build, partook more of  —  enlarged gorilla order.  Not that in
appearance he seemed in  —  least gorilla-like.  What I am striving to
express is this strength itself, more as a thing apart from his physical
semblance.  It was a strength we are wont to associate with things
primitive, with wild animals, and  —  creatures we imagine our
tree-dwelling prototypes to have been—a strength savage, ferocious, alive
in itself,  —  essence of life in that it is  —  potency of motion,  — 
elemental stuff itself out of which  —  many forms of life have been
moulded; in short, that which wri — s in  —  body of a snake when  — 
head is cut off, and  —  snake, as a snake, is dead, or which lingers in
 —  shapeless lump of turtle-meat and recoils and quivers from  —  prod
of a finger.

Such was  —  impression of strength I ga — red from this man who paced up
and down.  He was firmly planted on his legs; his feet struck  —  deck
squarely and with surety; every movement of a muscle, from  —  heave of
 —  shoulders to  —  tightening of  —  lips about  —  cigar, was
decisive, and seemed to come out of a strength that was excessive and
overwhelming.  In fact, though this strength pervaded every action of
his, it seemed but  —  advertisement of a greater strength that lurked
within, that lay dormant and no more than stirred from time to time, but
which might arouse, at any moment, terrible and compelling, like  —  rage
of a lion or  —  wrath of a storm.

 —  cook stuck his head out of  —  galley door and grinned encouragingly
at me, at  —  same time jerking his thumb in  —  direction of  —  man who
paced up and down by  —  hatchway.  Thus I was given to understand that
he was  —  captain,  —  “Old Man,” in  —  cook’s vernacular,  — 
individual whom I must interview and put to  —  trouble of somehow
getting me ashore.  I had half started forward, to get over with what I
was certain would be a stormy five minutes, when a more violent
suffocating paroxysm seized  —  unfortunate person who was lying on his
back.  He wrenched and wri — d about convulsively.   —  chin, with  — 
damp black beard, pointed higher in  —  air as  —  back muscles stiffened
and  —  chest swelled in an unconscious and instinctive effort to get
more air.  Under  —  whiskers, and all unseen, I knew that  —  skin was
taking on a purplish hue.

 —  captain, or Wolf Larsen, as men called him, ceased pacing and gazed
down at  —  dying man.  So fierce had this final struggle become that  — 
sailor paused in  —  act of flinging more water over him and stared
curiously,  —  canvas bucket partly tilted and dripping its contents to
 —  deck.   —  dying man beat a tattoo on  —  hatch with his heels,
straightened out his legs, and stiffened in one great tense effort, and
rolled his head from side to side.   — n  —  muscles relaxed,  —  head
stopped rolling, and a sigh, as of profound relief, floated upward from
his lips.   —  jaw dropped,  —  upper lip lifted, and two rows of
tobacco-discoloured teeth appeared.  It seemed as though his features had
frozen into a diabolical grin at  —  world he had left and outwitted.

 — n a most surprising thing occurred.   —  captain broke loose upon  — 
dead man like a thunderclap.  Oaths rolled from his lips in a continuous
stream.  And  — y were not namby-pamby oaths, or mere expressions of
indecency.  Each word was a blasphemy, and  — re were many words.   — y
crisped and crackled like electric sparks.  I had never heard anything
like it in my life, nor could I have conceived it possible.  With a turn
for literary expression myself, and a penchant for forcible figures and
phrases, I appreciated, as no o — r listener, I dare say,  —  peculiar
vividness and strength and absolute blasphemy of his metaphors.   — 
cause of it all, as near as I could make out, was that  —  man, who was
mate, had gone on a debauch before leaving San Francisco, and  — n had
 —  poor taste to die at  —  beginning of  —  voyage and leave Wolf
Larsen short-handed.

It should be unnecessary to state, at least to my friends, that I was
shocked.  Oaths and vile language of any sort had always been repellent
to me.  I felt a wilting sensation, a sinking at  —  heart, and, I might
just as well say, a giddiness.  To me, death had always been invested
with solemnity and dignity.  It had been peaceful in its occurrence,
sacred in its ceremonial.  But death in its more sordid and terrible
aspects was a thing with which I had been unacquainted till now.  As I
say, while I appreciated  —  power of  —  terrific denunciation that
swept out of Wolf Larsen’s mouth, I was inexpressibly shocked.   — 
scorching torrent was enough to wi — r  —  face of  —  corpse.  I should
not have been surprised if  —  wet black beard had frizzled and curled
and flared up in smoke and flame.  But  —  dead man was unconcerned.  He
continued to grin with a sardonic humour, with a cynical mockery and
defiance.  He was master of  —  situation.




CHAPTER 3


Wolf Larsen ceased swearing as suddenly as he had begun.  He relighted
his cigar and glanced around.  His eyes chanced upon  —  cook.

“Well, Cooky?” he began, with a suaveness that was cold and of  —  temper
of steel.

“Yes, sir,”  —  cook eagerly interpolated, with appeasing and apologetic
servility.

“Don’t you think you’ve stretched that neck of yours just about enough?
It’s unhealthy, you know.   —  mate’s gone, so I can’t afford to lose you
too.  You must be very, very careful of your health, Cooky.  Understand?”

His last word, in striking contrast with  —  smoothness of his previous
utterance, snapped like  —  lash of a whip.   —  cook quailed under it.

“Yes, sir,” was  —  meek reply, as  —  offending head disappeared into
 —  galley.

At this sweeping rebuke, which  —  cook had only pointed,  —  rest of  — 
crew became uninterested and fell to work at one task or ano — r.  A
number of men, however, who were lounging about a companion-way between
 —  galley and hatch, and who did not seem to be sailors, continued
talking in low tones with one ano — r.   — se, I afterward learned, were
 —  hunters,  —  men who shot  —  seals, and a very superior breed to
common sailor-folk.

“Johansen!” Wolf Larsen called out.  A sailor stepped forward obediently.
“Get your palm and needle and sew  —  beggar up.  You’ll find some old
canvas in  —  sail-locker.  Make it do.”

“What’ll I put on his feet, sir?”  —  man asked, after  —  customary “Ay,
ay, sir.”

“We’ll see to that,” Wolf Larsen answered, and elevated his voice in a
call of “Cooky!”

Thomas Mugridge popped out of his galley like a jack-in- — -box.

“Go below and fill a sack with coal.”

“Any of you fellows got a Bible or Prayer-book?” was  —  captain’s next
demand, this time of  —  hunters lounging about  —  companion-way.

 — y shook  — ir heads, and some one made a jocular remark which I did
not catch, but which raised a general laugh.

Wolf Larsen made  —  same demand of  —  sailors.  Bibles and Prayer-books
seemed scarce articles, but one of  —  men volunteered to pursue  — 
quest amongst  —  watch below, returning in a minute with  —  information
that  — re was none.

 —  captain shrugged his shoulders.  “ — n we’ll drop him over without
any palavering, unless our clerical-looking castaway has  —  burial
service at sea by heart.”

By this time he had swung fully around and was facing me.  “You’re a
preacher, aren’t you?” he asked.

 —  hunters,— — re were six of  — m,—to a man, turned and regarded me.  I
was painfully aware of my likeness to a scarecrow.  A laugh went up at my
appearance,—a laugh that was not lessened or softened by  —  dead man
stretched and grinning on  —  deck before us; a laugh that was as rough
and harsh and frank as  —  sea itself; that arose out of coarse feelings
and blunted sensibilities, from natures that knew nei — r courtesy nor
gentleness.

Wolf Larsen did not laugh, though his grey eyes lighted with a slight
glint of amusement; and in that moment, having stepped forward quite
close to him, I received my first impression of  —  man himself, of  — 
man as apart from his body, and from  —  torrent of blasphemy I had heard
him spew forth.   —  face, with large features and strong lines, of  — 
square order, yet well filled out, was apparently massive at first sight;
but again, as with  —  body,  —  massiveness seemed to vanish, and a
conviction to grow of a tremendous and excessive mental or spiritual
strength that lay behind, sleeping in  —  deeps of his being.   —  jaw,
 —  chin,  —  brow rising to a goodly height and swelling heavily above
 —  eyes,— — se, while strong in  — mselves, unusually strong, seemed to
speak an immense vigour or virility of spirit that lay behind and beyond
and out of sight.   — re was no sounding such a spirit, no measuring, no
determining of metes and bounds, nor neatly classifying in some
pigeon-hole with o — rs of similar type.

 —  eyes—and it was my destiny to know  — m well—were large and handsome,
wide apart as  —  true artist’s are wide, sheltering under a heavy brow
and arched over by thick black eyebrows.   —  eyes  — mselves were of
that baffling protean grey which is never twice  —  same; which runs
through many shades and colourings like intershot silk in sunshine; which
is grey, dark and light, and greenish-grey, and sometimes of  —  clear
azure of  —  deep sea.   — y were eyes that masked  —  soul with a
thousand guises, and that sometimes opened, at rare moments, and allowed
it to rush up as though it were about to fare forth nakedly into  — 
world on some wonderful adventure,—eyes that could brood with  — 
hopeless sombreness of leaden skies; that could snap and crackle points
of fire like those which sparkle from a whirling sword; that could grow
chill as an arctic landscape, and yet again, that could warm and soften
and be all a-dance with love-lights, intense and masculine, luring and
compelling, which at  —  same time fascinate and dominate women till  — y
surrender in a gladness of joy and of relief and sacrifice.

But to return.  I told him that, unhappily for  —  burial service, I was
not a preacher, when he sharply demanded:

“What do you do for a living?”

I confess I had never had such a question asked me before, nor had I ever
canvassed it.  I was quite taken aback, and before I could find myself
had sillily stammered, “I—I am a gentleman.”

His lip curled in a swift sneer.

“I have worked, I do work,” I cried impetuously, as though he were my
judge and I required vindication, and at  —  same time very much aware of
my arrant idiocy in discussing  —  subject at all.

“For your living?”

 — re was something so imperative and masterful about him that I was
quite beside myself—“rattled,” as Furuseth would have termed it, like a
quaking child before a stern school-master.

“Who feeds you?” was his next question.

“I have an income,” I answered stoutly, and could have bitten my tongue
 —  next instant.  “All of which, you will pardon my observing, has
nothing whatsoever to do with what I wish to see you about.”

But he disregarded my protest.

“Who earned it?  Eh?  I thought so.  Your fa — r.  You stand on dead
men’s legs.  You’ve never had any of your own.  You couldn’t walk alone
between two sunrises and hustle  —  meat for your belly for three meals.
Let me see your hand.”

His tremendous, dormant strength must have stirred, swiftly and
accurately, or I must have slept a moment, for before I knew it he had
stepped two paces forward, gripped my right hand in his, and held it up
for inspection.  I tried to withdraw it, but his fingers tightened,
without visible effort, till I thought mine would be crushed.  It is hard
to maintain one’s dignity under such circumstances.  I could not squirm
or struggle like a schoolboy.  Nor could I attack such a creature who had
but to twist my arm to break it.  Nothing remained but to stand still and
accept  —  indignity.  I had time to notice that  —  pockets of  —  dead
man had been emptied on  —  deck, and that his body and his grin had been

sewing toge — r with coarse white twine, shoving  —  needle through with
a lea — r contrivance fitted on  —  palm of his hand.

Wolf Larsen dropped my hand with a flirt of disdain.

“Dead men’s hands have kept it soft.  Good for little else than
dish-washing and scullion work.”

“I wish to be put ashore,” I said firmly, for I now had myself in
control.  “I shall pay you whatever you judge your delay and trouble to
be worth.”

He looked at me curiously.  Mockery shone in his eyes.

“I have a counter proposition to make, and for  —  good of your soul.  My
mate’s gone, and  — re’ll be a lot of promotion.  A sailor comes aft to

dollars per month and found.  Now what do you say?  And mind you, it’s

in time to stand on your own legs, and perhaps to toddle along a bit.”

But I took no notice.   —  sails of  —  vessel I had seen off to  — 
south-west had grown larger and plainer.   — y were of  —  same

smaller.  She was a pretty sight, leaping and flying toward us, and

far — r over.  Once, in a gust,  —  rail dipped under  —  sea, and  —

“That vessel will soon be passing us,” I said, after a moment’s pause.
“As she is going in  —  opposite direction, she is very probably bound

“Very probably,” was Wolf Larsen’s answer, as he turned partly away from
me and cried out, “Cooky!  Oh, Cooky!”

 —  Cockney popped out of  —  galley.

“Where’s that boy?  Tell him I want him.”

“Yes, sir;” and Thomas Mugridge fled swiftly aft and disappeared down
ano — r companion-way near  —  wheel.  A moment later he emerged, a
heavy-set young fellow of eighteen or nineteen, with a glowering,
villainous countenance, trailing at his heels.

“’Ere ’e is, sir,”  —  cook said.

But Wolf Larsen ignored that worthy, turning at once to  —  cabin-boy.

“What’s your name, boy?”

“George Leach, sir,” came  —  sullen answer, and  —  boy’s bearing showed
clearly that he divined  —  reason for which he had been summoned.

“Not an Irish name,”  —  captain snapped sharply.  “O’Toole or McCarthy
would suit your mug a damn sight better.  Unless, very likely,  — re’s an
Irishman in your mo — r’s woodpile.”

I saw  —  young fellow’s hands clench at  —  insult, and  —  blood crawl
scarlet up his neck.

“But let that go,” Wolf Larsen continued.  “You may have very good

twice as nasty.  I know  —  kind.  Well, you can make up your mind to

“McCready and Swanson.”

“Sir!” Wolf Larsen thundered.

“McCready and Swanson, sir,”  —  boy corrected, his eyes burning with a
bitter light.

“Who got  —  advance money?”

“ — y did, sir.”

“I thought as much.  And damned glad you were to let  — m have it.
Couldn’t make yourself scarce too quick, with several gentlemen you may
have heard of looking for you.”

 —  boy metamorphosed into a savage on  —  instant.  His body bunched
toge — r as though for a spring, and his face became as an infuriated

“A what?” Wolf Larsen asked, a peculiar softness in his voice, as though
he were overwhelmingly curious to hear  —  unspoken word.

 —  boy hesitated,  — n mastered his temper.  “Nothin’, sir.  I take it
back.”

“And you have shown me I was right.”  This with a gratified smile.  “How
old are you?”

“Just turned sixteen, sir,”

“A lie.  You’ll never see eighteen again.  Big for your age at that, with
muscles like a horse.  Pack up your kit and go for’ard into  —  fo’c’sle.
You’re a boat-puller now.  You’re promoted; see?”

Without waiting for  —  boy’s acceptance,  —  captain turned to  — 
sailor who had just finished  —  gruesome task of sewing up  —  corpse.
“Johansen, do you know anything about navigation?”

“No, sir,”

“Well, never mind; you’re mate just  —  same.  Get your traps aft into
 —  mate’s berth.”

“Ay, ay, sir,” was  —  cheery response, as Johansen started forward.

In  —  meantime  —  erstwhile cabin-boy had not moved.  “What are you
waiting for?” Wolf Larsen demanded.

“I didn’t sign for boat-puller, sir,” was  —  reply.  “I signed for
cabin-boy.  An’ I don’t want no boat-pullin’ in mine.”

“Pack up and go for’ard.”

This time Wolf Larsen’s command was thrillingly imperative.   —  boy

 — n came ano — r stirring of Wolf Larsen’s tremendous strength.  It was

“Well?” Larsen asked of me.  “Have you made up your mind?”

I had glanced occasionally at  —  approaching schooner, and it was now

It was a very trim and neat little craft.  I could see a large, black
number on one of its sails, and I had seen pictures of pilot-boats.

“What vessel is that?” I asked.

“ —  pilot-boat _Lady Mine_,” Wolf Larsen answered grimly.  “Got rid of

“Will you please signal it,  — n, so that I may be put ashore.”

“Sorry, but I’ve lost  —  signal book overboard,” he remarked, and  —

I debated a moment, looking him squarely in  —  eyes.  I had seen  — 
frightful treatment of  —  cabin-boy, and knew that I should very

“_Lady Mine_ ahoy!  Take me ashore!  A thousand dollars if you take me

I waited, watching two men who stood by  —  wheel, one of  — m steering.

“What is  —  matter?  Anything wrong?”

This was  —  cry from  —  _Lady Mine_.

“Yes!” I shouted, at  —  top of my lungs.  “Life or death!  One thousand
dollars if you take me ashore!”

“Too much ’Frisco tanglefoot for  —  health of my crew!” Wolf Larsen

 —  man on  —  _Lady Mine_ laughed back through  —  megaphone.   — 
pilot-boat plunged past.

“Give him hell for me!” came a final cry, and  —  two men waved  — ir
arms in farewell.

I leaned despairingly over  —  rail, watching  —  trim little schooner

 —  wind puffed strongly, and  —  _Ghost_ heeled far over, burying her

When I turned around, a moment later, I saw  —  cabin-boy staggering to

He looked very sick.

“Well, Leach, are you going for’ard?” Wolf Larsen asked.

“Yes, sir,” came  —  answer of a spirit cowed.

“And you?” I was asked.

“I’ll give you a thousand—” I began, but was interrupted.

“Stow that!  Are you going to take up your duties as cabin-boy?  Or do I

What was I to do?  To be brutally beaten, to be killed perhaps, would not

“Well?”

“Yes,” I said.

“Say ‘yes, sir.’”

“Yes, sir,” I corrected.

“What is your name?”

“Van Weyden, sir.”

“First name?”

“Humphrey, sir; Humphrey Van Weyden.”

“Age?”

“Thirty-five, sir.”

“That’ll do.  Go to  —  cook and learn your duties.”

And thus it was that I passed into a state of involuntary servitude to

It will always be to me a monstrous, inconceivable thing, a horrible

“Hold on, don’t go yet.”

I stopped obediently in my walk toward  —  galley.

“Johansen, call all hands.  Now that we’ve everything cleaned up, we’ll

While Johansen was summoning  —  watch below, a couple of sailors, under

were lashed a number of small boats.  Several men picked up  — 
hatch-cover with its ghastly freight, carried it to  —  lee side, and
rested it on  —  boats,  —  feet pointing overboard.  To  —  feet was
attached  —  sack of coal which  —  cook had fetched.

I had always conceived a burial at sea to be a very solemn and
awe-inspiring event, but I was quickly disillusioned, by this burial at
any rate.  One of  —  hunters, a little dark-eyed man whom his mates
called “Smoke,” was telling stories, liberally intersprinkled with oaths
and obscenities; and every minute or so  —  group of hunters gave mouth
to a laughter that sounded to me like a wolf-chorus or  —  barking of
hell-hounds.   —  sailors trooped noisily aft, some of  —  watch below
rubbing  —  sleep from  — ir eyes, and talked in low tones toge — r.
 — re was an ominous and worried expression on  — ir faces.  It was
evident that  — y did not like  —  outlook of a voyage under such a
captain and begun so inauspiciously.  From time to time  — y stole
glances at Wolf Larsen, and I could see that  — y were apprehensive of
 —  man.

He stepped up to  —  hatch-cover, and all caps came off.  I ran my eyes
over  — m—twenty men all told; twenty-two including  —  man at  —  wheel
and myself.  I was pardonably curious in my survey, for it appeared my
fate to be pent up with  — m on this miniature floating world for I knew
not how many weeks or months.   —  sailors, in  —  main, were English and
Scandinavian, and  — ir faces seemed of  —  heavy, stolid order.   — 
hunters, on  —  o — r hand, had stronger and more diversified faces, with
hard lines and  —  marks of  —  free play of passions.  Strange to say,
and I noted it all once, Wolf Larsen’s features showed no such evil
stamp.   — re seemed nothing vicious in  — m.  True,  — re were lines,
but  — y were  —  lines of decision and firmness.  It seemed, ra — r, a
frank and open countenance, which frankness or openness was enhanced by

next incident occurred—that it was  —  face of a man who could behave as
he had behaved to  —  cabin-boy.

At this moment, as he opened his mouth to speak, puff after puff struck
 —  schooner and pressed her side under.   —  wind shrieked a wild song
through  —  rigging.  Some of  —  hunters glanced anxiously aloft.   — 
lee rail, where  —  dead man lay, was buried in  —  sea, and as  — 
schooner lifted and righted  —  water swept across  —  deck wetting us
above our shoe-tops.  A shower of rain drove down upon us, each drop
stinging like a hailstone.  As it passed, Wolf Larsen began to speak,  — 
bare-headed men swaying in unison, to  —  heave and lunge of  —  deck.

“I only remember one part of  —  service,” he said, “and that is, ‘And
 —  body shall be cast into  —  sea.’  So cast it in.”

He ceased speaking.   —  men holding  —  hatch-cover seemed perplexed,
puzzled no doubt by  —  briefness of  —  ceremony.  He burst upon  — m in
a fury.

“Lift up that end  — re, damn you!  What  —  hell’s  —  matter with you?”

 — y elevated  —  end of  —  hatch-cover with pitiful haste, and, like a
dog flung overside,  —  dead man slid feet first into  —  sea.   —  coal
at his feet dragged him down.  He was gone.

“Johansen,” Wolf Larsen said briskly to  —  new mate, “keep all hands on
deck now  — y’re here.  Get in  —  topsails and jibs and make a good job
of it.  We’re in for a sou’-easter.  Better reef  —  jib and mainsail
too, while you’re about it.”

In a moment  —  decks were in commotion, Johansen bellowing orders and
 —  men pulling or letting go ropes of various sorts—all naturally
confusing to a landsman such as myself.  But it was  —  heartlessness of
it that especially struck me.   —  dead man was an episode that was past,
an incident that was dropped, in a canvas covering with a sack of coal,
while  —  ship sped along and her work went on.  Nobody had been
affected.   —  hunters were laughing at a fresh story of Smoke’s;  —  men
pulling and hauling, and two of  — m climbing aloft; Wolf Larsen was
studying  —  clouding sky to windward; and  —  dead man, dying obscenely,
buried sordidly, and sinking down, down—

 — n it was that  —  cruelty of  —  sea, its relentlessness and
awfulness, rushed upon me.  Life had become cheap and tawdry, a beastly
and inarticulate thing, a soulless stirring of  —  ooze and slime.  I
held on to  —  wea — r rail, close by  —  shrouds, and gazed out across
 —  desolate foaming waves to  —  low-lying fog-banks that hid San
Francisco and  —  California coast.  Rain-squalls were driving in
between, and I could scarcely see  —  fog.  And this strange vessel, with
its terrible men, pressed under by wind and sea and ever leaping up and
out, was heading away into  —  south-west, into  —  great and lonely
Pacific expanse.




CHAPTER 4


What happened to me next on  —  sealing-schooner _Ghost_, as I strove to
fit into my new environment, are matters of humiliation and pain.   — 
cook, who was called “ —  doctor” by  —  crew, “Tommy” by  —  hunters,
and “Cooky” by Wolf Larsen, was a changed person.   —  difference worked
in my status brought about a corresponding difference in treatment from
him.  Servile and fawning as he had been before, he was now as
domineering and bellicose.  In truth, I was no longer  —  fine gentleman
with a skin soft as a “lydy’s,” but only an ordinary and very worthless
cabin-boy.

He absurdly insisted upon my addressing him as Mr. Mugridge, and his
behaviour and carriage were insufferable as he showed me my duties.
Besides my work in  —  cabin, with its four small state-rooms, I was
supposed to be his assistant in  —  galley, and my colossal ignorance
concerning such things as peeling potatoes or washing greasy pots was a
source of unending and sarcastic wonder to him.  He refused to take into
consideration what I was, or, ra — r, what my life and  —  things I was
accustomed to had been.  This was part of  —  attitude he chose to adopt
toward me; and I confess, ere  —  day was done, that I hated him with
more lively feelings than I had ever hated any one in my life before.

This first day was made more difficult for me from  —  fact that  — 
_Ghost_, under close reefs (terms such as  — se I did not learn till
later), was plunging through what Mr. Mugridge called an “’owlin’
sou’-easter.”  At half-past five, under his directions, I set  —  table
in  —  cabin, with rough-wea — r trays in place, and  — n carried  —  tea
and cooked food down from  —  galley.  In this connection I cannot
forbear relating my first experience with a boarding sea.

“Look sharp or you’ll get doused,” was Mr. Mugridge’s parting injunction,
as I left  —  galley with a big tea-pot in one hand, and in  —  hollow of
 —  o — r arm several loaves of fresh-baked bread.  One of  —  hunters, a
tall, loose-jointed chap named Henderson, was going aft at  —  time from
 —  steerage ( —  name  —  hunters facetiously gave  — ir midships
sleeping quarters) to  —  cabin.  Wolf Larsen was on  —  poop, smoking
his everlasting cigar.

“’Ere she comes.  Sling yer ’ook!”  —  cook cried.

I stopped, for I did not know what was coming, and saw  —  galley door
slide shut with a bang.   — n I saw Henderson leaping like a madman for
 —  main rigging, up which he shot, on  —  inside, till he was many feet
higher than my head.  Also I saw a great wave, curling and foaming,
poised far above  —  rail.  I was directly under it.  My mind did not
work quickly, everything was so new and strange.  I grasped that I was in
danger, but that was all.  I stood still, in trepidation.   — n Wolf
Larsen shouted from  —  poop:

“Grab hold something, you—you Hump!”

But it was too late.  I sprang toward  —  rigging, to which I might have
clung, and was met by  —  descending wall of water.  What happened after
that was very confusing.  I was beneath  —  water, suffocating and
drowning.  My feet were out from under me, and I was turning over and
over and being swept along I knew not where.  Several times I collided
against hard objects, once striking my right knee a terrible blow.   — n
 —  flood seemed suddenly to subside and I was breathing  —  good air
again.  I had been swept against  —  galley and around  —  steerage
companion-way from  —  wea — r side into  —  lee scuppers.   —  pain from
my hurt knee was agonizing.  I could not put my weight on it, or, at
least, I thought I could not put my weight on it; and I felt sure  —  leg
was broken.  But  —  cook was after me, shouting through  —  lee galley

“’Ere, you!  Don’t tyke all night about it!  Where’s  —  pot?  Lost
overboard?  Serve you bloody well right if yer neck was broke!”

I managed to struggle to my feet.   —  great tea-pot was still in my
hand.  I limped to  —  galley and handed it to him.  But he was consumed
with indignation, real or feigned.

“Gawd blime me if you ayn’t a slob.  Wot ’re you good for anyw’y, I’d
like to know?  Eh?  Wot ’re you good for any’wy?  Cawn’t even carry a bit
of tea aft without losin’ it.  Now I’ll ’ave to boil some more.

“An’ wot ’re you snifflin’ about?” he burst out at me, with renewed rage.
“’Cos you’ve ’urt yer pore little leg, pore little mamma’s darlin’.”

I was not sniffling, though my face might well have been drawn and
twitching from  —  pain.  But I called up all my resolution, set my
teeth, and hobbled back and forth from galley to cabin and cabin to
galley without fur — r mishap.  Two things I had acquired by my accident:
an injured knee-cap that went undressed and from which I suffered for
weary months, and  —  name of “Hump,” which Wolf Larsen had called me
from  —  poop.   — reafter, fore and aft, I was known by no o — r name,
until  —  term became a part of my thought-processes and I identified it
with myself, thought of myself as Hump, as though Hump were I and had
always been I.

It was no easy task, waiting on  —  cabin table, where sat Wolf Larsen,
Johansen, and  —  six hunters.   —  cabin was small, to begin with, and
to move around, as I was compelled to, was not made easier by  — 
schooner’s violent pitching and wallowing.  But what struck me most
forcibly was  —  total lack of sympathy on  —  part of  —  men whom I
served.  I could feel my knee through my clo — s, swelling, and swelling,

All  —  men must have seen my condition, but not one spoke or took notice

“Don’t let a little thing like that bo — r you.  You’ll get used to such
things in time.  It may cripple you some, but all  —  same you’ll be
learning to walk.

“That’s what you call a paradox, isn’t it?” he added.

He seemed pleased when I nodded my head with  —  customary “Yes, sir.”

“I suppose you know a bit about literary things?  Eh?  Good.  I’ll have
some talks with you some time.”

And  — n, taking no fur — r account of me, he turned his back and went up
on deck.

That night, when I had finished an endless amount of work, I was sent to
sleep in  —  steerage, where I made up a spare bunk.  I was glad to get
out of  —  detestable presence of  —  cook and to be off my feet.  To my
surprise, my clo — s had dried on me and  — re seemed no indications of

from  —  foundering of  —  _Martinez_.  Under ordinary circumstances,
after all that I had undergone, I should have been fit for bed and a
trained nurse.

But my knee was bo — ring me terribly.  As well as I could make out,  — 
kneecap seemed turned up on edge in  —  midst of  —  swelling.  As I sat
in my bunk examining it ( —  six hunters were all in  —  steerage,
smoking and talking in loud voices), Henderson took a passing glance at
it.

“Looks nasty,” he commented.  “Tie a rag around it, and it’ll be all
right.”

That was all; and on  —  land I would have been lying on  —  broad of my
back, with a surgeon attending on me, and with strict injunctions to do
nothing but rest.  But I must do  — se men justice.  Callous as  — y were
to my suffering,  — y were equally callous to  — ir own when anything
befell  — m.  And this was due, I believe, first, to habit; and second,
to  —  fact that  — y were less sensitively organized.  I really believe
that a finely-organized, high-strung man would suffer twice and thrice as
much as  — y from a like injury.

Tired as I was,—exhausted, in fact,—I was prevented from sleeping by  — 
pain in my knee.  It was all I could do to keep from groaning aloud.  At

elemental environment seemed to call for a savage repression.  Like  —

ano — r of  —  hunters, lose a finger by having it smashed to a jelly;
and he did not even murmur or change  —  expression on his face.  Yet I
have seen  —  same man, time and again, fly into  —  most outrageous
passion over a trifle.

He was doing it now, vociferating, bellowing, waving his arms, and
cursing like a fiend, and all because of a disagreement with ano — r

that it did, that it could swim  —  moment it was born.   —  o — r
hunter, Latimer, a lean, Yankee-looking fellow with shrewd,
narrow-slitted eyes, held o — rwise, held that  —  seal pup was born on
 —  land for no o — r reason than that it could not swim, that its mo — r
was compelled to teach it to swim as birds were compelled to teach  — ir
nestlings how to fly.

For  —  most part,  —  remaining four hunters leaned on  —  table or lay
in  — ir bunks and left  —  discussion to  —  two antagonists.  But  — y
were supremely interested, for every little while  — y ardently took
sides, and sometimes all were talking at once, till  — ir voices surged

very little reasoning or none at all.   — ir method was one of assertion,
assumption, and denunciation.   — y proved that a seal pup could swim or
not swim at birth by stating  —  proposition very bellicosely and  — n
following it up with an attack on  —  opposing man’s judgment, common
sense, nationality, or past history.  Rebuttal was precisely similar.  I
have related this in order to show  —  mental calibre of  —  men with
whom I was thrown in contact.  Intellectually  — y were children,
inhabiting  —  physical forms of men.

And  — y smoked, incessantly smoked, using a coarse, cheap, and
offensive-smelling tobacco.   —  air was thick and murky with  —  smoke
of it; and this, combined with  —  violent movement of  —  ship as she
struggled through  —  storm, would surely have made me sea-sick had I
been a victim to that malady.  As it was, it made me quite squeamish,
though this nausea might have been due to  —  pain of my leg and
exhaustion.

As I lay  — re thinking, I naturally dwelt upon myself and my situation.
It was unparalleled, undreamed-of, that I, Humphrey Van Weyden, a scholar
and a dilettante, if you please, in things artistic and literary, should
be lying here on a Bering Sea seal-hunting schooner.  Cabin-boy!  I had
never done any hard manual labour, or scullion labour, in my life.  I had
lived a placid, uneventful, sedentary existence all my days— —  life of a
scholar and a recluse on an assured and comfortable income.  Violent life
and athletic sports had never appealed to me.  I had always been a
book-worm; so my sisters and fa — r had called me during my childhood.  I
had gone camping but once in my life, and  — n I left  —  party almost at
its start and returned to  —  comforts and conveniences of a roof.  And
here I was, with dreary and endless vistas before me of table-setting,
potato-peeling, and dish-washing.  And I was not strong.   —  doctors had
always said that I had a remarkable constitution, but I had never
developed it or my body through exercise.  My muscles were small and
soft, like a woman’s, or so  —  doctors had said time and again in  — 
course of  — ir attempts to persuade me to go in for physical-culture

was, in no fit condition for  —  rough life in prospect.

 — se are merely a few of  —  things that went through my mind, and are
related for  —  sake of vindicating myself in advance in  —  weak and
helpless _rôle_ I was destined to play.  But I thought, also, of my
mo — r and sisters, and pictured  — ir grief.  I was among  —  missing
dead of  —  _Martinez_ disaster, an unrecovered body.  I could see  — 
head-lines in  —  papers;  —  fellows at  —  University Club and  — 
Bibelot shaking  — ir heads and saying, “Poor chap!”  And I could see
Charley Furuseth, as I had said good-bye to him that morning, lounging in
a dressing-gown on  —  be-pillowed window couch and delivering himself of
oracular and pessimistic epigrams.

And all  —  while, rolling, plunging, climbing  —  moving mountains and
falling and wallowing in  —  foaming valleys,  —  schooner _Ghost_ was
fighting her way far — r and far — r into  —  heart of  —  Pacific—and I
was on her.  I could hear  —  wind above.  It came to my ears as a
muffled roar.  Now and again feet stamped overhead.  An endless creaking
was going on all about me,  —  woodwork and  —  fittings groaning and
squeaking and complaining in a thousand keys.   —  hunters were still
arguing and roaring like some semi-human amphibious breed.   —  air was
filled with oaths and indecent expressions.  I could see  — ir faces,
flushed and angry,  —  brutality distorted and emphasized by  —  sickly
yellow of  —  sea-lamps which rocked back and forth with  —  ship.
Through  —  dim smoke-haze  —  bunks looked like  —  sleeping dens of
animals in a menagerie.  Oilskins and sea-boots were hanging from  — 
walls, and here and  — re rifles and shotguns rested securely in  — 
racks.  It was a sea-fitting for  —  buccaneers and pirates of by-gone
years.  My imagination ran riot, and still I could not sleep.  And it was
a long, long night, weary and dreary and long.




CHAPTER 5


But my first night in  —  hunters’ steerage was also my last.  Next day
Johansen,  —  new mate, was routed from  —  cabin by Wolf Larsen, and
sent into  —  steerage to sleep  — reafter, while I took possession of
 —  tiny cabin state-room, which, on  —  first day of  —  voyage, had
already had two occupants.   —  reason for this change was quickly
learned by  —  hunters, and became  —  cause of a deal of grumbling on
 — ir part.  It seemed that Johansen, in his sleep, lived over each night
 —  events of  —  day.  His incessant talking and shouting and bellowing
of orders had been too much for Wolf Larsen, who had accordingly foisted
 —  nuisance upon his hunters.

After a sleepless night, I arose weak and in agony, to hobble through my
second day on  —  _Ghost_.  Thomas Mugridge routed me out at half-past
five, much in  —  fashion that Bill Sykes must have routed out his dog;
but Mr. Mugridge’s brutality to me was paid back in kind and with
interest.   —  unnecessary noise he made (I had lain wide-eyed  —  whole

through  —  semi-darkness, and Mr. Mugridge, with a sharp howl of pain,

normal shape, and was called a “cauliflower ear” by  —  sailors.

 —  day was filled with miserable variety.  I had taken my dried clo — s
down from  —  galley  —  night before, and  —  first thing I did was to
exchange  —  cook’s garments for  — m.  I looked for my purse.  In
addition to some small change (and I have a good memory for such things),
it had contained one hundred and eighty-five dollars in gold and paper.
 —  purse I found, but its contents, with  —  exception of  —  small
silver, had been abstracted.  I spoke to  —  cook about it, when I went
on deck to take up my duties in  —  galley, and though I had looked
forward to a surly answer, I had not expected  —  belligerent harangue
that I received.

“Look ’ere, ’Ump,” he began, a malicious light in his eyes and a snarl in
his throat; “d’ye want yer nose punched?  If you think I’m a thief, just
keep it to yerself, or you’ll find ’ow bloody well mistyken you are.
Strike me blind if this ayn’t gratitude for yer!  ’Ere you come, a pore
mis’rable specimen of ’uman scum, an’ I tykes yer into my galley an’

’ell, say I, an’ I’ve a good mind to give you what-for anyw’y.”

So saying, he put up his fists and started for me.  To my shame be it, I
cowered away from  —  blow and ran out  —  galley door.  What else was I
to do?  Force, nothing but force, obtained on this brute-ship.  Moral
suasion was a thing unknown.  Picture it to yourself: a man of ordinary
stature, slender of build, and with weak, undeveloped muscles, who has
lived a peaceful, placid life, and is unused to violence of any sort—what
could such a man possibly do?   — re was no more reason that I should
stand and face  — se human beasts than that I should stand and face an
infuriated bull.

So I thought it out at  —  time, feeling  —  need for vindication and
desiring to be at peace with my conscience.  But this vindication did not
satisfy.  Nor, to this day can I permit my manhood to look back upon
those events and feel entirely exonerated.   —  situation was something
that really exceeded rational formulas for conduct and demanded more than

shame rises within me at  —  recollection, and in  —  pride of my manhood
I feel that my manhood has in unaccountable ways been smirched and
sullied.

All of which is nei — r here nor  — re.   —  speed with which I ran from
 —  galley caused excruciating pain in my knee, and I sank down
helplessly at  —  break of  —  poop.  But  —  Cockney had not pursued me.

“Look at ’im run!  Look at ’im run!” I could hear him crying.  “An’ with
a gyme leg at that!  Come on back, you pore little mamma’s darling.  I
won’t ’it yer; no, I won’t.”

I came back and went on with my work; and here  —  episode ended for  — 
time, though fur — r developments were yet to take place.  I set  — 
breakfast-table in  —  cabin, and at seven o’clock waited on  —  hunters
and officers.   —  storm had evidently broken during  —  night, though a
huge sea was still running and a stiff wind blowing.  Sail had been made
in  —  early watches, so that  —  _Ghost_ was racing along under
everything except  —  two topsails and  —  flying jib.   — se three

most of  —  storm, which was driving him to  —  south-west into that
portion of  —  sea where he expected to pick up with  —  north-east
trades.  It was before this steady wind that he hoped to make  —  major
portion of  —  run to Japan, curving south into  —  tropics and north
again as he approached  —  coast of Asia.

After breakfast I had ano — r unenviable experience.  When I had finished
washing  —  dishes, I cleaned  —  cabin stove and carried  —  ashes up on

reality, he was attempting to warn me to throw my ashes over  —  lee

and flung  —  ashes over  —  side to windward.   —  wind drove  — m back,
and not only over me, but over Henderson and Wolf Larsen.   —  next

realized  — re could be so much pain in a kick.  I reeled away from him

Later in  —  morning I received a surprise of a totally different sort.
Following  —  cook’s instructions, I had gone into Wolf Larsen’s
state-room to put it to rights and make  —  bed.  Against  —  wall, near
 —  head of  —  bunk, was a rack filled with books.  I glanced over  — m,
noting with astonishment such names as Shakespeare, Tennyson, Poe, and De

English and American Literature_, and Johnson’s _Natural History_ in two
large volumes.   — n  — re were a number of grammars, such as Metcalf’s,
and Reed and Kellogg’s; and I smiled as I saw a copy of _ —  Dean’s
English_.

I could not reconcile  — se books with  —  man from what I had seen of
him, and I wondered if he could possibly read  — m.  But when I came to
make  —  bed I found, between  —  blankets, dropped apparently as he had
sunk off to sleep, a complete Browning,  —  Cambridge Edition.  It was
open at “In a Balcony,” and I noticed, here and  — re, passages
underlined in pencil.  Fur — r, letting drop  —  volume during a lurch of
 —  ship, a sheet of paper fell out.  It was scrawled over with
geometrical diagrams and calculations of some sort.

It was patent that this terrible man was no ignorant clod, such as one
would inevitably suppose him to be from his exhibitions of brutality.  At
once he became an enigma.  One side or  —  o — r of his nature was
perfectly comprehensible; but both sides toge — r were bewildering.  I
had already remarked that his language was excellent, marred with an
occasional slight inaccuracy.  Of course, in common speech with  — 
sailors and hunters, it sometimes fairly bristled with errors, which was
due to  —  vernacular itself; but in  —  few words he had held with me it
had been clear and correct.

This glimpse I had caught of his o — r side must have emboldened me, for
I resolved to speak to him about  —  money I had lost.

“I have been robbed,” I said to him, a little later, when I found him
pacing up and down  —  poop alone.

“Sir,” he corrected, not harshly, but sternly.

“I have been robbed, sir,” I amended.

“How did it happen?” he asked.

 — n I told him  —  whole circumstance, how my clo — s had been left to
dry in  —  galley, and how, later, I was nearly beaten by  —  cook when I
mentioned  —  matter.

He smiled at my recital.  “Pickings,” he concluded; “Cooky’s pickings.
And don’t you think your miserable life worth  —  price?  Besides,
consider it a lesson.  You’ll learn in time how to take care of your
money for yourself.  I suppose, up to now, your lawyer has done it for
you, or your business agent.”

I could feel  —  quiet sneer through his words, but demanded, “How can I
get it back again?”

“That’s your look-out.  You haven’t any lawyer or business agent now, so
you’ll have to depend on yourself.  When you get a dollar, hang on to it.
A man who leaves his money lying around,  —  way you did, deserves to
lose it.  Besides, you have sinned.  You have no right to put temptation
in  —  way of your fellow-creatures.  You tempted Cooky, and he fell.
You have placed his immortal soul in jeopardy.  By  —  way, do you
believe in  —  immortal soul?”

His lids lifted lazily as he asked  —  question, and it seemed that  — 
deeps were opening to me and that I was gazing into his soul.  But it was
an illusion.  Far as it might have seemed, no man has ever seen very far
into Wolf Larsen’s soul, or seen it at all,—of this I am convinced.  It
was a very lonely soul, I was to learn, that never unmasked, though at
rare moments it played at doing so.

“I read immortality in your eyes,” I answered, dropping  —  “sir,”—an
experiment, for I thought  —  intimacy of  —  conversation warranted it.

He took no notice.  “By that, I take it, you see something that is alive,
but that necessarily does not have to live for ever.”

“I read more than that,” I continued boldly.

“ — n you read consciousness.  You read  —  consciousness of life that it
is alive; but still no fur — r away, no endlessness of life.”

How clearly he thought, and how well he expressed what he thought!  From
regarding me curiously, he turned his head and glanced out over  — 
leaden sea to windward.  A bleakness came into his eyes, and  —  lines of
his mouth grew severe and harsh.  He was evidently in a pessimistic mood.

“ — n to what end?” he demanded abruptly, turning back to me.  “If I am
immortal—why?”

I halted.  How could I explain my idealism to this man?  How could I put
into speech a something felt, a something like  —  strains of music heard
in sleep, a something that convinced yet transcended utterance?

“What do you believe,  — n?” I countered.

“I believe that life is a mess,” he answered promptly.  “It is like
yeast, a ferment, a thing that moves and may move for a minute, an hour,
a year, or a hundred years, but that in  —  end will cease to move.   — 
big eat  —  little that  — y may continue to move,  —  strong eat  — 
weak that  — y may retain  — ir strength.   —  lucky eat  —  most and
move  —  longest, that is all.  What do you make of those things?”

He swept his arm in an impatient gesture toward a number of  —  sailors
who were working on some kind of rope stuff amidships.

“ — y move, so does  —  jelly-fish move.   — y move in order to eat in
order that  — y may keep moving.   — re you have it.   — y live for  — ir
belly’s sake, and  —  belly is for  — ir sake.  It’s a circle; you get
nowhere.  Nei — r do  — y.  In  —  end  — y come to a standstill.   — y
move no more.   — y are dead.”

“ — y have dreams,” I interrupted, “radiant, flashing dreams—”

“Of grub,” he concluded sententiously.

“And of more—”

“Grub.  Of a larger appetite and more luck in satisfying it.”  His voice
sounded harsh.   — re was no levity in it.  “For, look you,  — y dream of
making lucky voyages which will bring  — m more money, of becoming  — 
mates of ships, of finding fortunes—in short, of being in a better
position for preying on  — ir fellows, of having all night in, good grub
and somebody else to do  —  dirty work.  You and I are just like  — m.
 — re is no difference, except that we have eaten more and better.  I am
eating  — m now, and you too.  But in  —  past you have eaten more than I
have.  You have slept in soft beds, and worn fine clo — s, and eaten good
meals.  Who made those beds? and those clo — s? and those meals?  Not
you.  You never made anything in your own sweat.  You live on an income
which your fa — r earned.  You are like a frigate bird swooping down upon
 —  boobies and robbing  — m of  —  fish  — y have caught.  You are one
with a crowd of men who have made what  — y call a government, who are
masters of all  —  o — r men, and who eat  —  food  —  o — r men get and
would like to eat  — mselves.  You wear  —  warm clo — s.   — y made  — 
clo — s, but  — y shiver in rags and ask you,  —  lawyer, or business
agent who handles your money, for a job.”

“But that is beside  —  matter,” I cried.

“Not at all.”  He was speaking rapidly now, and his eyes were flashing.
“It is piggishness, and it is life.  Of what use or sense is an
immortality of piggishness?  What is  —  end?  What is it all about?  You
have made no food.  Yet  —  food you have eaten or wasted might have
saved  —  lives of a score of wretches who made  —  food but did not eat
it.  What immortal end did you serve? or did  — y?  Consider yourself and
me.  What does your boasted immortality amount to when your life runs
foul of mine?  You would like to go back to  —  land, which is a
favourable place for your kind of piggishness.  It is a whim of mine to
keep you aboard this ship, where my piggishness flourishes.  And keep you
I will.  I may make or break you.  You may die to-day, this week, or next
month.  I could kill you now, with a blow of my fist, for you are a
miserable weakling.  But if we are immortal, what is  —  reason for this?
To be piggish as you and I have been all our lives does not seem to be
just  —  thing for immortals to be doing.  Again, what’s it all about?
Why have I kept you here?—”

“Because you are stronger,” I managed to blurt out.

“But why stronger?” he went on at once with his perpetual queries.
“Because I am a bigger bit of  —  ferment than you?  Don’t you see?
Don’t you see?”

“But  —  hopelessness of it,” I protested.

“I agree with you,” he answered.  “ — n why move at all, since moving is
living?  Without moving and being part of  —  yeast  — re would be no
hopelessness.  But,—and  — re it is,—we want to live and move, though we
have no reason to, because it happens that it is  —  nature of life to
live and move, to want to live and move.  If it were not for this, life
would be dead.  It is because of this life that is in you that you dream
of your immortality.   —  life that is in you is alive and wants to go on
being alive for ever.  Bah!  An eternity of piggishness!”

He abruptly turned on his heel and started forward.  He stopped at  — 
break of  —  poop and called me to him.

“By  —  way, how much was it that Cooky got away with?” he asked.

“One hundred and eighty-five dollars, sir,” I answered.

He nodded his head.  A moment later, as I started down  —  companion
stairs to lay  —  table for dinner, I heard him loudly cursing some men
amidships.




CHAPTER 6


By  —  following morning  —  storm had blown itself quite out and  — 
 Ghost  was rolling slightly on a calm sea without a breath of wind.
Occasional light airs were felt, however, and Wolf Larsen patrolled  — 
poop constantly, his eyes ever searching  —  sea to  —  north-eastward,
from which direction  —  great trade-wind must blow.

 —  men were all on deck and busy preparing  — ir various boats for  — 
season’s hunting.   — re are seven boats aboard,  —  captain’s dingey,
and  —  six which  —  hunters will use.  Three, a hunter, a boat-puller,
and a boat-steerer, compose a boat’s crew.  On board  —  schooner  — 
boat-pullers and steerers are  —  crew.   —  hunters, too, are supposed
to be in command of  —  watches, subject, always, to  —  orders of Wolf
Larsen.

All this, and more, I have learned.   —  _Ghost_ is considered  — 
fastest schooner in both  —  San Francisco and Victoria fleets.  In fact,
she was once a private yacht, and was built for speed.  Her lines and
fittings—though I know nothing about such things—speak for  — mselves.
Johnson was telling me about her in a short chat I had with him during
yesterday’s second dog-watch.  He spoke enthusiastically, with  —  love
for a fine craft such as some men feel for horses.  He is greatly
disgusted with  —  outlook, and I am given to understand that Wolf Larsen
bears a very unsavoury reputation among  —  sealing captains.  It was  —
