_Ghost_ herself that lured Johnson into signing for  —  voyage, but he is
already beginning to repent.

As he told me,  —  _Ghost_ is an eighty-ton schooner of a remarkably fine
model.  Her beam, or width, is twenty-three feet, and her length a little
over ninety feet.  A lead keel of fabulous but unknown weight makes her
very stable, while she carries an immense spread of canvas.  From  — 
deck to  —  truck of  —  maintopmast is something over a hundred feet,
while  —  foremast with its topmast is eight or ten feet shorter.  I am
giving  — se details so that  —  size of this little floating world which
holds twenty-two men may be appreciated.  It is a very little world, a
mote, a speck, and I marvel that men should dare to venture  —  sea on a
contrivance so small and fragile.

Wolf Larsen has, also, a reputation for reckless carrying on of sail.  I
overheard Henderson and ano — r of  —  hunters, Standish, a Californian,
talking about it.  Two years ago he dismasted  —  _Ghost_ in a gale on
Bering Sea, whereupon  —  present masts were put in, which are stronger
and heavier in every way.  He is said to have remarked, when he put  — m
in, that he preferred turning her over to losing  —  sticks.

Every man aboard, with  —  exception of Johansen, who is ra — r overcome
by his promotion, seems to have an excuse for having sailed on  — 
_Ghost_.  Half  —  men forward are deep-water sailors, and  — ir excuse
is that  — y did not know anything about her or her captain.  And those
who do know, whisper that  —  hunters, while excellent shots, were so
notorious for  — ir quarrelsome and rascally proclivities that  — y could
not sign on any decent schooner.

I have made  —  acquaintance of ano — r one of  —  crew,—Louis he is
called, a rotund and jovial-faced Nova Scotia Irishman, and a very
sociable fellow, prone to talk as long as he can find a listener.  In  — 
afternoon, while  —  cook was below asleep and I was peeling  — 
everlasting potatoes, Louis dropped into  —  galley for a “yarn.”  His
excuse for being aboard was that he was drunk when he signed.  He assured
me again and again that it was  —  last thing in  —  world he would dream
of doing in a sober moment.  It seems that he has been seal-hunting
regularly each season for a dozen years, and is accounted one of  —  two
or three very best boat-steerers in both fleets.

“Ah, my boy,” he shook his head ominously at me, “’tis  —  worst schooner
ye could iv selected, nor were ye drunk at  —  time as was I.  ’Tis
sealin’ is  —  sailor’s paradise—on o — r ships than this.   —  mate was
 —  first, but mark me words,  — re’ll be more dead men before  —  trip
is done with.  Hist, now, between you an’ meself and  —  stanchion  — re,
this Wolf Larsen is a regular devil, an’  —  _Ghost’ll_ be a hell-ship
like she’s always ben since he had hold iv her.  Don’t I know?  Don’t I
know?  Don’t I remember him in Hakodate two years gone, when he had a row
an’ shot four iv his men?  Wasn’t I a-layin’ on  —  _Emma L._, not three
hundred yards away?  An’  — re was a man  —  same year he killed with a
blow iv his fist.  Yes, sir, killed ’im dead-oh.  His head must iv
smashed like an eggshell.  An’ wasn’t  — re  —  Governor of Kura Island,
an’  —  Chief iv Police, Japanese gentlemen, sir, an’ didn’t  — y come
aboard  —  _Ghost_ as his guests, a-bringin’  — ir wives along—wee an’
pretty little bits of things like you see ’em painted on fans.  An’ as he
was a-gettin’ under way, didn’t  —  fond husbands get left astern-like in
 — ir sampan, as it might be by accident?  An’ wasn’t it a week later
that  —  poor little ladies was put ashore on  —  o — r side of  — 
island, with nothin’ before ’em but to walk home acrost  —  mountains on
 — ir weeny-teeny little straw sandals which wouldn’t hang toge — r a
mile?  Don’t I know?  ’Tis  —  beast he is, this Wolf Larsen— —  great
big beast mentioned iv in Revelation; an’ no good end will he ever come
to.  But I’ve said nothin’ to ye, mind ye.  I’ve whispered never a word;
for old fat Louis’ll live  —  voyage out if  —  last mo — r’s son of yez
go to  —  fishes.”

“Wolf Larsen!” he snorted a moment later.  “Listen to  —  word, will ye!
Wolf—’tis what he is.  He’s not black-hearted like some men.  ’Tis no
heart he has at all.  Wolf, just wolf, ’tis what he is.  D’ye wonder he’s
well named?”

“But if he is so well-known for what he is,” I queried, “how is it that
he can get men to ship with him?”

“An’ how is it ye can get men to do anything on God’s earth an’ sea?”
Louis demanded with Celtic fire.  “How d’ye find me aboard if ’twasn’t
that I was drunk as a pig when I put me name down?   — re’s  — m that
can’t sail with better men, like  —  hunters, and  — m that don’t know,
like  —  poor devils of wind-jammers for’ard  — re.  But  — y’ll come to
it,  — y’ll come to it, an’ be sorry  —  day  — y was born.  I could weep
for  —  poor creatures, did I but forget poor old fat Louis and  — 
troubles before him.  But ’tis not a whisper I’ve dropped, mind ye, not a
whisper.”

“ — m hunters is  —  wicked boys,” he broke forth again, for he suffered
from a constitutional plethora of speech.  “But wait till  — y get to
cutting up iv jinks and rowin’ ’round.  He’s  —  boy’ll fix ’em.  ’Tis
him that’ll put  —  fear of God in  — ir rotten black hearts.  Look at
that hunter iv mine, Horner.  ‘Jock’ Horner  — y call him, so quiet-like
an’ easy-goin’, soft-spoken as a girl, till ye’d think butter wouldn’t
melt in  —  mouth iv him.  Didn’t he kill his boat-steerer last year?
’Twas called a sad accident, but I met  —  boat-puller in Yokohama an’
 —  straight iv it was given me.  An’  — re’s Smoke,  —  black little
devil—didn’t  —  Roosians have him for three years in  —  salt mines of
Siberia, for poachin’ on Copper Island, which is a Roosian preserve?
Shackled he was, hand an’ foot, with his mate.  An’ didn’t  — y have
words or a ruction of some kind?—for ’twas  —  o — r fellow Smoke sent up
in  —  buckets to  —  top of  —  mine; an’ a piece at a time he went up,
a leg to-day, an’ to-morrow an arm,  —  next day  —  head, an’ so on.”

“But you can’t mean it!” I cried out, overcome with  —  horror of it.

“Mean what!” he demanded, quick as a flash.  “’Tis nothin’ I’ve said.
Deef I am, and dumb, as ye should be for  —  sake iv your mo — r; an’
never once have I opened me lips but to say fine things iv  — m an’ him,
God curse his soul, an’ may he rot in purgatory ten thousand years, and
 — n go down to  —  last an’ deepest hell iv all!”

Johnson,  —  man who had chafed me raw when I first came aboard, seemed
 —  least equivocal of  —  men forward or aft.  In fact,  — re was
nothing equivocal about him.  One was struck at once by his
straightforwardness and manliness, which, in turn, were tempered by a
modesty which might be mistaken for timidity.  But timid he was not.  He
seemed, ra — r, to have  —  courage of his convictions,  —  certainty of
his manhood.  It was this that made him protest, at  —  commencement of
our acquaintance, against being called Yonson.  And upon this, and him,
Louis passed judgment and prophecy.

“’Tis a fine chap, that squarehead Johnson we’ve for’ard with us,” he
said.  “ —  best sailorman in  —  fo’c’sle.  He’s my boat-puller.  But
it’s to trouble he’ll come with Wolf Larsen, as  —  sparks fly upward.
It’s meself that knows.  I can see it brewin’ an’ comin’ up like a storm
in  —  sky.  I’ve talked to him like a bro — r, but it’s little he sees
in takin’ in his lights or flyin’ false signals.  He grumbles out when
things don’t go to suit him, and  — re’ll be always some tell-tale
carryin’ word iv it aft to  —  Wolf.   —  Wolf is strong, and it’s  — 
way of a wolf to hate strength, an’ strength it is he’ll see in
Johnson—no knucklin’ under, and a ‘Yes, sir, thank ye kindly, sir,’ for a
curse or a blow.  Oh, she’s a-comin’!  She’s a-comin’!  An’ God knows
where I’ll get ano — r boat-puller!  What does  —  fool up an’ say, when
 —  old man calls him Yonson, but ‘Me name is Johnson, sir,’ an’  — n
spells it out, letter for letter.  Ye should iv seen  —  old man’s face!
I thought he’d let drive at him on  —  spot.  He didn’t, but he will, an’
he’ll break that squarehead’s heart, or it’s little I know iv  —  ways iv
men on  —  ships iv  —  sea.”

Thomas Mugridge is becoming unendurable.  I am compelled to Mister him
and to Sir him with every speech.  One reason for this is that Wolf
Larsen seems to have taken a fancy to him.  It is an unprecedented thing,
I take it, for a captain to be chummy with  —  cook; but this is
certainly what Wolf Larsen is doing.  Two or three times he put his head
into  —  galley and chaffed Mugridge good-naturedly, and once, this
afternoon, he stood by  —  break of  —  poop and chatted with him for
fully fifteen minutes.  When it was over, and Mugridge was back in  — 
galley, he became greasily radiant, and went about his work, humming
coster songs in a nerve-racking and discordant falsetto.

“I always get along with  —  officers,” he remarked to me in a
confidential tone.  “I know  —  w’y, I do, to myke myself uppreci-yted.
 — re was my last skipper—w’y I thought nothin’ of droppin’ down in  — 
cabin for a little chat and a friendly glass.  ‘Mugridge,’ sez ’e to me,
‘Mugridge,’ sez ’e, ‘you’ve missed yer vokytion.’  ‘An’ ’ow’s that?’ sez
I.  ‘Yer should ’a been born a gentleman, an’ never ’ad to work for yer
livin’.’  God strike me dead, ’Ump, if that ayn’t wot ’e sez, an’ me
a-sittin’  — re in ’is own cabin, jolly-like an’ comfortable, a-smokin’
’is cigars an’ drinkin’ ’is rum.”

This chitter-chatter drove me to distraction.  I never heard a voice I
hated so.  His oily, insinuating tones, his greasy smile and his
monstrous self-conceit grated on my nerves till sometimes I was all in a
tremble.  Positively, he was  —  most disgusting and loathsome person I
have ever met.   —  filth of his cooking was indescribable; and, as he
cooked everything that was eaten aboard, I was compelled to select what I
ate with great circumspection, choosing from  —  least dirty of his
concoctions.

My hands bo — red me a great deal, unused as  — y were to work.   — 
nails were discoloured and black, while  —  skin was already grained with
dirt which even a scrubbing-brush could not remove.   — n blisters came,
in a painful and never-ending procession, and I had a great burn on my
forearm, acquired by losing my balance in a roll of  —  ship and pitching
against  —  galley stove.  Nor was my knee any better.   —  swelling had
not gone down, and  —  cap was still up on edge.  Hobbling about on it
from morning till night was not helping it any.  What I needed was rest,
if it were ever to get well.

Rest!  I never before knew  —  meaning of  —  word.  I had been resting
all my life and did not know it.  But now, could I sit still for one
half-hour and do nothing, not even think, it would be  —  most
pleasurable thing in  —  world.  But it is a revelation, on  —  o — r
hand.  I shall be able to appreciate  —  lives of  —  working people
hereafter.  I did not dream that work was so terrible a thing.  From
half-past five in  —  morning till ten o’clock at night I am everybody’s
slave, with not one moment to myself, except such as I can steal near  — 
end of  —  second dog-watch.  Let me pause for a minute to look out over
 —  sea sparkling in  —  sun, or to gaze at a sailor going aloft to  — 
gaff-topsails, or running out  —  bowsprit, and I am sure to hear  — 
hateful voice, “’Ere, you, ’Ump, no sodgerin’.  I’ve got my peepers on
yer.”

 — re are signs of rampant bad temper in  —  steerage, and  —  gossip is
going around that Smoke and Henderson have had a fight.  Henderson seems
 —  best of  —  hunters, a slow-going fellow, and hard to rouse; but
roused he must have been, for Smoke had a bruised and discoloured eye,
and looked particularly vicious when he came into  —  cabin for supper.

A cruel thing happened just before supper, indicative of  —  callousness
and brutishness of  — se men.   — re is one green hand in  —  crew,
Harrison by name, a clumsy-looking country boy, mastered, I imagine, by
 —  spirit of adventure, and making his first voyage.  In  —  light
baffling airs  —  schooner had been tacking about a great deal, at which
times  —  sails pass from one side to  —  o — r and a man is sent aloft
to shift over  —  fore-gaff-topsail.  In some way, when Harrison was
aloft,  —  sheet jammed in  —  block through which it runs at  —  end of
 —  gaff.  As I understood it,  — re were two ways of getting it
cleared,—first, by lowering  —  foresail, which was comparatively easy
and without danger; and second, by climbing out  —  peak-halyards to  — 
end of  —  gaff itself, an exceedingly hazardous performance.

Johansen called out to Harrison to go out  —  halyards.  It was patent to
everybody that  —  boy was afraid.  And well he might be, eighty feet
above  —  deck, to trust himself on those thin and jerking ropes.  Had
 — re been a steady breeze it would not have been so bad, but  —  _Ghost_
was rolling emptily in a long sea, and with each roll  —  canvas flapped
and boomed and  —  halyards slacked and jerked taut.   — y were capable
of snapping a man off like a fly from a whip-lash.

Harrison heard  —  order and understood what was demanded of him, but
hesitated.  It was probably  —  first time he had been aloft in his life.
Johansen, who had caught  —  contagion of Wolf Larsen’s masterfulness,
burst out with a volley of abuse and curses.

“That’ll do, Johansen,” Wolf Larsen said brusquely.  “I’ll have you know
that I do  —  swearing on this ship.  If I need your assistance, I’ll
call you in.”

“Yes, sir,”  —  mate acknowledged submissively.

In  —  meantime Harrison had started out on  —  halyards.  I was looking
up from  —  galley door, and I could see him trembling, as if with ague,
in every limb.  He proceeded very slowly and cautiously, an inch at a
time.  Outlined against  —  clear blue of  —  sky, he had  —  appearance
of an enormous spider crawling along  —  tracery of its web.

It was a slight uphill climb, for  —  foresail peaked high; and  — 
halyards, running through various blocks on  —  gaff and mast, gave him
separate holds for hands and feet.  But  —  trouble lay in that  —  wind
was not strong enough nor steady enough to keep  —  sail full.  When he
was half-way out,  —  _Ghost_ took a long roll to windward and back again
into  —  hollow between two seas.  Harrison ceased his progress and held
on tightly.  Eighty feet beneath, I could see  —  agonized strain of his
muscles as he gripped for very life.   —  sail emptied and  —  gaff swung
amid-ships.   —  halyards slackened, and, though it all happened very
quickly, I could see  — m sag beneath  —  weight of his body.   — n  — 
gag swung to  —  side with an abrupt swiftness,  —  great sail boomed
like a cannon, and  —  three rows of reef-points slatted against  — 
canvas like a volley of rifles.  Harrison, clinging on, made  —  giddy
rush through  —  air.  This rush ceased abruptly.   —  halyards became
instantly taut.  It was  —  snap of  —  whip.  His clutch was broken.
One hand was torn loose from its hold.   —  o — r lingered desperately
for a moment, and followed.  His body pitched out and down, but in some
way he managed to save himself with his legs.  He was hanging by  — m,
head downward.  A quick effort brought his hands up to  —  halyards
again; but he was a long time regaining his former position, where he
hung, a pitiable object.

“I’ll bet he has no appetite for supper,” I heard Wolf Larsen’s voice,
which came to me from around  —  corner of  —  galley.  “Stand from
under, you, Johansen!  Watch out!  Here she comes!”

In truth, Harrison was very sick, as a person is sea-sick; and for a long
time he clung to his precarious perch without attempting to move.
Johansen, however, continued violently to urge him on to  —  completion
of his task.

“It is a shame,” I heard Johnson growling in painfully slow and correct
English.  He was standing by  —  main rigging, a few feet away from me.
“ —  boy is willing enough.  He will learn if he has a chance.  But this
is—”  He paused awhile, for  —  word “murder” was his final judgment.

“Hist, will ye!” Louis whispered to him, “For  —  love iv your mo — r
hold your mouth!”

But Johnson, looking on, still continued his grumbling.

“Look here,”  —  hunter Standish spoke to Wolf Larsen, “that’s my
boat-puller, and I don’t want to lose him.”

“That’s all right, Standish,” was  —  reply.  “He’s your boat-puller when
you’ve got him in  —  boat; but he’s my sailor when I have him aboard,
and I’ll do what I damn well please with him.”

“But that’s no reason—” Standish began in a torrent of speech.

“That’ll do, easy as she goes,” Wolf Larsen counselled back.  “I’ve told
you what’s what, and let it stop at that.   —  man’s mine, and I’ll make
soup of him and eat it if I want to.”

 — re was an angry gleam in  —  hunter’s eye, but he turned on his heel
and entered  —  steerage companion-way, where he remained, looking
upward.  All hands were on deck now, and all eyes were aloft, where a
human life was at grapples with death.   —  callousness of  — se men, to
whom industrial organization gave control of  —  lives of o — r men, was
appalling.  I, who had lived out of  —  whirl of  —  world, had never
dreamed that its work was carried on in such fashion.  Life had always
seemed a peculiarly sacred thing, but here it counted for nothing, was a
cipher in  —  arithmetic of commerce.  I must say, however, that  — 
sailors  — mselves were sympa — tic, as instance  —  case of Johnson; but
 —  masters ( —  hunters and  —  captain) were heartlessly indifferent.
Even  —  protest of Standish arose out of  —  fact that he did not wish
to lose his boat-puller.  Had it been some o — r hunter’s boat-puller,
he, like  — m, would have been no more than amused.

But to return to Harrison.  It took Johansen, insulting and reviling  — 
poor wretch, fully ten minutes to get him started again.  A little later
he made  —  end of  —  gaff, where, astride  —  spar itself, he had a
better chance for holding on.  He cleared  —  sheet, and was free to
return, slightly downhill now, along  —  halyards to  —  mast.  But he
had lost his nerve.  Unsafe as was his present position, he was loath to
forsake it for  —  more unsafe position on  —  halyards.

He looked along  —  airy path he must traverse, and  — n down to  — 
deck.  His eyes were wide and staring, and he was trembling violently.  I
had never seen fear so strongly stamped upon a human face.  Johansen
called vainly for him to come down.  At any moment he was liable to be
snapped off  —  gaff, but he was helpless with fright.  Wolf Larsen,
walking up and down with Smoke and in conversation, took no more notice
of him, though he cried sharply, once, to  —  man at  —  wheel:

“You’re off your course, my man!  Be careful, unless you’re looking for
trouble!”

“Ay, ay, sir,”  —  helmsman responded, putting a couple of spokes down.

He had been guilty of running  —  _Ghost_ several points off her course
in order that what little wind  — re was should fill  —  foresail and
hold it steady.  He had striven to help  —  unfortunate Harrison at  — 
risk of incurring Wolf Larsen’s anger.

 —  time went by, and  —  suspense, to me, was terrible.  Thomas
Mugridge, on  —  o — r hand, considered it a laughable affair, and was
continually bobbing his head out  —  galley door to make jocose remarks.
How I hated him!  And how my hatred for him grew and grew, during that
fearful time, to cyclopean dimensions.  For  —  first time in my life I
experienced  —  desire to murder—“saw red,” as some of our picturesque
writers phrase it.  Life in general might still be sacred, but life in
 —  particular case of Thomas Mugridge had become very profane indeed.  I
was frightened when I became conscious that I was seeing red, and  — 
thought flashed through my mind: was I, too, becoming tainted by  — 
brutality of my environment?—I, who even in  —  most flagrant crimes had
denied  —  justice and righteousness of capital punishment?

Fully half-an-hour went by, and  — n I saw Johnson and Louis in some sort
of altercation.  It ended with Johnson flinging off Louis’s detaining arm
and starting forward.  He crossed  —  deck, sprang into  —  fore rigging,
and began to climb.  But  —  quick eye of Wolf Larsen caught him.

“Here, you, what are you up to?” he cried.

Johnson’s ascent was arrested.  He looked his captain in  —  eyes and
replied slowly:

“I am going to get that boy down.”

“You’ll get down out of that rigging, and damn lively about it!  D’ye
hear?  Get down!”

Johnson hesitated, but  —  long years of obedience to  —  masters of
ships overpowered him, and he dropped sullenly to  —  deck and went on
forward.

At half after five I went below to set  —  cabin table, but I hardly knew
what I did, for my eyes and my brain were filled with  —  vision of a
man, white-faced and trembling, comically like a bug, clinging to  — 
thrashing gaff.  At six o’clock, when I served supper, going on deck to
get  —  food from  —  galley, I saw Harrison, still in  —  same position.
 —  conversation at  —  table was of o — r things.  Nobody seemed
interested in  —  wantonly imperilled life.  But making an extra trip to
 —  galley a little later, I was gladdened by  —  sight of Harrison
staggering weakly from  —  rigging to  —  forecastle scuttle.  He had
finally summoned  —  courage to descend.

Before closing this incident, I must give a scrap of conversation I had
with Wolf Larsen in  —  cabin, while I was washing  —  dishes.

“You were looking squeamish this afternoon,” he began.  “What was  — 
matter?”

I could see that he knew what had made me possibly as sick as Harrison,
that he was trying to draw me, and I answered, “It was because of  — 
brutal treatment of that boy.”

He gave a short laugh.  “Like sea-sickness, I suppose.  Some men are
subject to it, and o — rs are not.”

“Not so,” I objected.

“Just so,” he went on.  “ —  earth is as full of brutality as  —  sea is
full of motion.  And some men are made sick by  —  one, and some by  — 
o — r.  That’s  —  only reason.”

“But you, who make a mock of human life, don’t you place any value upon
it whatever?” I demanded.

“Value?  What value?”  He looked at me, and though his eyes were steady
and motionless,  — re seemed a cynical smile in  — m.  “What kind of
value?  How do you measure it?  Who values it?”

“I do,” I made answer.

“ — n what is it worth to you?  Ano — r man’s life, I mean.  Come now,
what is it worth?”

 —  value of life?  How could I put a tangible value upon it?  Somehow,
I, who have always had expression, lacked expression when with Wolf
Larsen.  I have since determined that a part of it was due to  —  man’s
personality, but that  —  greater part was due to his totally different
outlook.  Unlike o — r materialists I had met and with whom I had
something in common to start on, I had nothing in common with him.
Perhaps, also, it was  —  elemental simplicity of his mind that baffled
me.  He drove so directly to  —  core of  —  matter, divesting a question
always of all superfluous details, and with such an air of finality, that
I seemed to find myself struggling in deep water, with no footing under
me.  Value of life?  How could I answer  —  question on  —  spur of  — 
moment?   —  sacredness of life I had accepted as axiomatic.  That it was
intrinsically valuable was a truism I had never questioned.  But when he
challenged  —  truism I was speechless.

“We were talking about this yesterday,” he said.  “I held that life was a
ferment, a yeasty something which devoured life that it might live, and
that living was merely successful piggishness.  Why, if  — re is anything
in supply and demand, life is  —  cheapest thing in  —  world.   — re is
only so much water, so much earth, so much air; but  —  life that is
demanding to be born is limitless.  Nature is a spendthrift.  Look at  — 
fish and  — ir millions of eggs.  For that matter, look at you and me.
In our loins are  —  possibilities of millions of lives.  Could we but
find time and opportunity and utilize  —  last bit and every bit of  — 
unborn life that is in us, we could become  —  fa — rs of nations and
populate continents.  Life?  Bah!  It has no value.  Of cheap things it
is  —  cheapest.  Everywhere it goes begging.  Nature spills it out with
a lavish hand.  Where  — re is room for one life, she sows a thousand
lives, and it’s life eats life till  —  strongest and most piggish life
is left.”

“You have read Darwin,” I said.  “But you read him misunderstandingly
when you conclude that  —  struggle for existence sanctions your wanton
destruction of life.”

He shrugged his shoulders.  “You know you only mean that in relation to
human life, for of  —  flesh and  —  fowl and  —  fish you destroy as
much as I or any o — r man.  And human life is in no wise different,
though you feel it is and think that you reason why it is.  Why should I
be parsimonious with this life which is cheap and without value?   — re
are more sailors than  — re are ships on  —  sea for  — m, more workers
than  — re are factories or machines for  — m.  Why, you who live on  — 
land know that you house your poor people in  —  slums of cities and
loose famine and pestilence upon  — m, and that  — re still remain more
poor people, dying for want of a crust of bread and a bit of meat (which
is life destroyed), than you know what to do with.  Have you ever seen
 —  London dockers fighting like wild beasts for a chance to work?”

He started for  —  companion stairs, but turned his head for a final
word.  “Do you know  —  only value life has is what life puts upon
itself?  And it is of course over-estimated since it is of necessity
prejudiced in its own favour.  Take that man I had aloft.  He held on as
if he were a precious thing, a treasure beyond diamonds or rubies.  To
you?  No.  To me?  Not at all.  To himself?  Yes.  But I do not accept
his estimate.  He sadly overrates himself.   — re is plenty more life
demanding to be born.  Had he fallen and dripped his brains upon  —  deck
like honey from  —  comb,  — re would have been no loss to  —  world.  He
was worth nothing to  —  world.   —  supply is too large.  To himself
only was he of value, and to show how fictitious even this value was,
being dead he is unconscious that he has lost himself.  He alone rated
himself beyond diamonds and rubies.  Diamonds and rubies are gone, spread
out on  —  deck to be washed away by a bucket of sea-water, and he does
not even know that  —  diamonds and rubies are gone.  He does not lose
anything, for with  —  loss of himself he loses  —  knowledge of loss.
Don’t you see?  And what have you to say?”

“That you are at least consistent,” was all I could say, and I went on
washing  —  dishes.




CHAPTER 7


At last, after three days of variable winds, we have caught  — 
north-east trades.  I came on deck, after a good night’s rest in spite of
my poor knee, to find  —  _Ghost_ foaming along, wing-and-wing, and every
sail drawing except  —  jibs, with a fresh breeze astern.  Oh,  —  wonder
of  —  great trade-wind!  All day we sailed, and all night, and  —  next
day, and  —  next, day after day,  —  wind always astern and blowing
steadily and strong.   —  schooner sailed herself.   — re was no pulling
and hauling on sheets and tackles, no shifting of topsails, no work at
all for  —  sailors to do except to steer.  At night when  —  sun went
down,  —  sheets were slackened; in  —  morning, when  — y yielded up  — 
damp of  —  dew and relaxed,  — y were pulled tight again—and that was
all.

Ten knots, twelve knots, eleven knots, varying from time to time, is  — 
speed we are making.  And ever out of  —  north-east  —  brave wind
blows, driving us on our course two hundred and fifty miles between  — 
dawns.  It saddens me and gladdens me,  —  gait with which we are leaving
San Francisco behind and with which we are foaming down upon  —  tropics.
Each day grows perceptibly warmer.  In  —  second dog-watch  —  sailors
come on deck, stripped, and heave buckets of water upon one ano — r from
overside.  Flying-fish are beginning to be seen, and during  —  night  — 
watch above scrambles over  —  deck in pursuit of those that fall aboard.
In  —  morning, Thomas Mugridge being duly bribed,  —  galley is
pleasantly areek with  —  odour of  — ir frying; while dolphin meat is
served fore and aft on such occasions as Johnson catches  —  blazing
beauties from  —  bowsprit end.

Johnson seems to spend all his spare time  — re or aloft at  — 
crosstrees, watching  —  _Ghost_ cleaving  —  water under press of sail.
 — re is passion, adoration, in his eyes, and he goes about in a sort of
trance, gazing in ecstasy at  —  swelling sails,  —  foaming wake, and
 —  heave and  —  run of her over  —  liquid mountains that are moving
with us in stately procession.

 —  days and nights are “all a wonder and a wild delight,” and though I
have little time from my dreary work, I steal odd moments to gaze and
gaze at  —  unending glory of what I never dreamed  —  world possessed.
Above,  —  sky is stainless blue—blue as  —  sea itself, which under  — 
forefoot is of  —  colour and sheen of azure satin.  All around  — 
horizon are pale, fleecy clouds, never changing, never moving, like a
silver setting for  —  flawless turquoise sky.

I do not forget one night, when I should have been asleep, of lying on
 —  forecastle-head and gazing down at  —  spectral ripple of foam thrust
aside by  —  _Ghost’s_ forefoot.  It sounded like  —  gurgling of a brook
over mossy stones in some quiet dell, and  —  crooning song of it lured
me away and out of myself till I was no longer Hump  —  cabin-boy, nor
Van Weyden,  —  man who had dreamed away thirty-five years among books.
But a voice behind me,  —  unmistakable voice of Wolf Larsen, strong with
 —  invincible certitude of  —  man and mellow with appreciation of  — 
words he was quoting, aroused me.

   “‘O  —  blazing tropic night, when  —  wake’s a welt of light
      That holds  —  hot sky tame,
   And  —  steady forefoot snores through  —  planet-powdered floors
      Where  —  scared whale flukes in flame.
   Her plates are scarred by  —  sun, dear lass,
      And her ropes are taut with  —  dew,
   For we’re booming down on  —  old trail, our own trail,  —  out trail,
      We’re sagging south on  —  Long Trail— —  trail that is always
   new.’”

“Eh, Hump?  How’s it strike you?” he asked, after  —  due pause which
words and setting demanded.

I looked into his face.  It was aglow with light, as  —  sea itself, and
 —  eyes were flashing in  —  starshine.

“It strikes me as remarkable, to say  —  least, that you should show
enthusiasm,” I answered coldly.

“Why, man, it’s living! it’s life!” he cried.

“Which is a cheap thing and without value.”  I flung his words at him.

He laughed, and it was  —  first time I had heard honest mirth in his
voice.

“Ah, I cannot get you to understand, cannot drive it into your head, what
a thing this life is.  Of course life is valueless, except to itself.
And I can tell you that my life is pretty valuable just now—to myself.
It is beyond price, which you will acknowledge is a terrific overrating,
but which I cannot help, for it is  —  life that is in me that makes  — 
rating.”

He appeared waiting for  —  words with which to express  —  thought that
was in him, and finally went on.

“Do you know, I am filled with a strange uplift; I feel as if all time
were echoing through me, as though all powers were mine.  I know truth,
divine good from evil, right from wrong.  My vision is clear and far.  I
could almost believe in God.  But,” and his voice changed and  —  light
went out of his face,—“what is this condition in which I find myself?
this joy of living? this exultation of life? this inspiration, I may well
call it?  It is what comes when  — re is nothing wrong with one’s
digestion, when his stomach is in trim and his appetite has an edge, and
all goes well.  It is  —  bribe for living,  —  champagne of  —  blood,
 —  effervescence of  —  ferment—that makes some men think holy thoughts,
and o — r men to see God or to create him when  — y cannot see him.  That
is all,  —  drunkenness of life,  —  stirring and crawling of  —  yeast,
 —  babbling of  —  life that is insane with consciousness that it is
alive.  And—bah!  To-morrow I shall pay for it as  —  drunkard pays.  And
I shall know that I must die, at sea most likely, cease crawling of
myself to be all a-crawl with  —  corruption of  —  sea; to be fed upon,
to be carrion, to yield up all  —  strength and movement of my muscles
that it may become strength and movement in fin and scale and  —  guts of
fishes.  Bah!  And bah! again.   —  champagne is already flat.   — 
sparkle and bubble has gone out and it is a tasteless drink.”

He left me as suddenly as he had come, springing to  —  deck with  — 
weight and softness of a tiger.   —  _Ghost_ ploughed on her way.  I
noted  —  gurgling forefoot was very like a snore, and as I listened to
it  —  effect of Wolf Larsen’s swift rush from sublime exultation to
despair slowly left me.   — n some deep-water sailor, from  —  waist of
 —  ship, lifted a rich tenor voice in  —  “Song of  —  Trade Wind”:

   “Oh, I am  —  wind  —  seamen love—
      I am steady, and strong, and true;
    — y follow my track by  —  clouds above,
      O’er  —  fathomless tropic blue.

                                  * * * * *

   Through daylight and dark I follow  —  bark
      I keep like a hound on her trail;
   I’m strongest at noon, yet under  —  moon,
      I stiffen  —  bunt of her sail.”




CHAPTER 8


Sometimes I think Wolf Larsen mad, or half-mad at least, what of his
strange moods and vagaries.  At o — r times I take him for a great man, a
genius who has never arrived.  And, finally, I am convinced that he is
 —  perfect type of  —  primitive man, born a thousand years or
generations too late and an anachronism in this culminating century of
civilization.  He is certainly an individualist of  —  most pronounced
type.  Not only that, but he is very lonely.   — re is no congeniality
between him and  —  rest of  —  men aboard ship.  His tremendous virility
and mental strength wall him apart.   — y are more like children to him,
even  —  hunters, and as children he treats  — m, descending perforce to
 — ir level and playing with  — m as a man plays with puppies.  Or else
he probes  — m with  —  cruel hand of a vivisectionist, groping about in
 — ir mental processes and examining  — ir souls as though to see of what
soul-stuff is made.

I have seen him a score of times, at table, insulting this hunter or
that, with cool and level eyes and, withal, a certain air of interest,
pondering  — ir actions or replies or petty rages with a curiosity almost
laughable to me who stood onlooker and who understood.  Concerning his
own rages, I am convinced that  — y are not real, that  — y are sometimes
experiments, but that in  —  main  — y are  —  habits of a pose or
attitude he has seen fit to take toward his fellow-men.  I know, with  — 
possible exception of  —  incident of  —  dead mate, that I have not seen
him really angry; nor do I wish ever to see him in a genuine rage, when
all  —  force of him is called into play.

While on  —  question of vagaries, I shall tell what befell Thomas
Mugridge in  —  cabin, and at  —  same time complete an incident upon
which I have already touched once or twice.   —  twelve o’clock dinner
was over, one day, and I had just finished putting  —  cabin in order,
when Wolf Larsen and Thomas Mugridge descended  —  companion stairs.
Though  —  cook had a cubby-hole of a state-room opening off from  — 
cabin, in  —  cabin itself he had never dared to linger or to be seen,
and he flitted to and fro, once or twice a day, a timid spectre.

“So you know how to play ‘Nap,’” Wolf Larsen was saying in a pleased sort
of voice.  “I might have guessed an Englishman would know.  I learned it
myself in English ships.”

Thomas Mugridge was beside himself, a bli — ring imbecile, so pleased was
he at chumming thus with  —  captain.   —  little airs he put on and  — 
painful striving to assume  —  easy carriage of a man born to a dignified
place in life would have been sickening had  — y not been ludicrous.  He
quite ignored my presence, though I credited him with being simply unable
to see me.  His pale, wishy-washy eyes were swimming like lazy summer
seas, though what blissful visions  — y beheld were beyond my
imagination.

“Get  —  cards, Hump,” Wolf Larsen ordered, as  — y took seats at  — 
table.  “And bring out  —  cigars and  —  whisky you’ll find in my
berth.”

I returned with  —  articles in time to hear  —  Cockney hinting broadly
that  — re was a mystery about him, that he might be a gentleman’s son
gone wrong or something or o — r; also, that he was a remittance man and
was paid to keep away from England—“p’yed ’ansomely, sir,” was  —  way he
put it; “p’yed ’ansomely to sling my ’ook an’ keep slingin’ it.”

I had brought  —  customary liquor glasses, but Wolf Larsen frowned,
shook his head, and signalled with his hands for me to bring  — 
tumblers.   — se he filled two-thirds full with undiluted whisky—“a
gentleman’s drink?” quoth Thomas Mugridge,—and  — y clinked  — ir glasses
to  —  glorious game of “Nap,” lighted cigars, and fell to shuffling and
dealing  —  cards.

 — y played for money.   — y increased  —  amounts of  —  bets.   — y
drank whisky,  — y drank it neat, and I fetched more.  I do not know
whe — r Wolf Larsen cheated or not,—a thing he was thoroughly capable of
doing,—but he won steadily.   —  cook made repeated journeys to his bunk
for money.  Each time he performed  —  journey with greater swagger, but
he never brought more than a few dollars at a time.  He grew maudlin,
familiar, could hardly see  —  cards or sit upright.  As a preliminary to
ano — r journey to his bunk, he hooked Wolf Larsen’s buttonhole with a
greasy forefinger and vacuously proclaimed and reiterated, “I got money,
I got money, I tell yer, an’ I’m a gentleman’s son.”

Wolf Larsen was unaffected by  —  drink, yet he drank glass for glass,
and if anything his glasses were fuller.   — re was no change in him.  He
did not appear even amused at  —  o — r’s antics.

In  —  end, with loud protestations that he could lose like a gentleman,
 —  cook’s last money was staked on  —  game—and lost.  Whereupon he
leaned his head on his hands and wept.  Wolf Larsen looked curiously at
him, as though about to probe and vivisect him,  — n changed his mind, as
from  —  foregone conclusion that  — re was nothing  — re to probe.

“Hump,” he said to me, elaborately polite, “kindly take Mr. Mugridge’s
arm and help him up on deck.  He is not feeling very well.”

“And tell Johnson to douse him with a few buckets of salt water,” he
added, in a lower tone for my ear alone.

I left Mr. Mugridge on deck, in  —  hands of a couple of grinning sailors
who had been told off for  —  purpose.  Mr. Mugridge was sleepily
spluttering that he was a gentleman’s son.  But as I descended  — 
companion stairs to clear  —  table I heard him shriek as  —  first
bucket of water struck him.

Wolf Larsen was counting his winnings.

“One hundred and eighty-five dollars even,” he said aloud.  “Just as I
thought.   —  beggar came aboard without a cent.”

“And what you have won is mine, sir,” I said boldly.

He favoured me with a quizzical smile.  “Hump, I have studied some
grammar in my time, and I think your tenses are tangled.  ‘Was mine,’ you
should have said, not ’is mine.’”

“It is a question, not of grammar, but of ethics,” I answered.

It was possibly a minute before he spoke.

“D’ye know, Hump,” he said, with a slow seriousness which had in it an
indefinable strain of sadness, “that this is  —  first time I have heard
 —  word ‘ethics’ in  —  mouth of a man.  You and I are  —  only men on
this ship who know its meaning.”

“At one time in my life,” he continued, after ano — r pause, “I dreamed
that I might some day talk with men who used such language, that I might
lift myself out of  —  place in life in which I had been born, and hold
conversation and mingle with men who talked about just such things as
ethics.  And this is  —  first time I have ever heard  —  word
pronounced.  Which is all by  —  way, for you are wrong.  It is a
question nei — r of grammar nor ethics, but of fact.”

“I understand,” I said.  “ —  fact is that you have  —  money.”

His face brightened.  He seemed pleased at my perspicacity.  “But it is
avoiding  —  real question,” I continued, “which is one of right.”

“Ah,” he remarked, with a wry pucker of his mouth, “I see you still
believe in such things as right and wrong.”

“But don’t you?—at all?” I demanded.

“Not  —  least bit.  Might is right, and that is all  — re is to it.
Weakness is wrong.  Which is a very poor way of saying that it is good
for oneself to be strong, and evil for oneself to be weak—or better yet,
it is pleasurable to be strong, because of  —  profits; painful to be
weak, because of  —  penalties.  Just now  —  possession of this money is
a pleasurable thing.  It is good for one to possess it.  Being able to
possess it, I wrong myself and  —  life that is in me if I give it to you
and forego  —  pleasure of possessing it.”

“But you wrong me by withholding it,” I objected.

“Not at all.  One man cannot wrong ano — r man.  He can only wrong
himself.  As I see it, I do wrong always when I consider  —  interests of
o — rs.  Don’t you see?  How can two particles of  —  yeast wrong each
o — r by striving to devour each o — r?  It is  — ir inborn heritage to
strive to devour, and to strive not to be devoured.  When  — y depart
from this  — y sin.”

“ — n you don’t believe in altruism?” I asked.

He received  —  word as if it had a familiar ring, though he pondered it
thoughtfully.  “Let me see, it means something about coöperation, doesn’t
it?”

“Well, in a way  — re has come to be a sort of connection,” I answered
unsurprised by this time at such gaps in his vocabulary, which, like his
knowledge, was  —  acquirement of a self-read, self-educated man, whom no
one had directed in his studies, and who had thought much and talked
little or not at all.  “An altruistic act is an act performed for  — 
welfare of o — rs.  It is unselfish, as opposed to an act performed for
self, which is selfish.”

He nodded his head.  “Oh, yes, I remember it now.  I ran across it in
Spencer.”

“Spencer!” I cried.  “Have you read him?”

“Not very much,” was his confession.  “I understood quite a good deal of
_First Principles_, but his _Biology_ took  —  wind out of my sails, and
his _Psychology_ left me butting around in  —  doldrums for many a day.
I honestly could not understand what he was driving at.  I put it down to
mental deficiency on my part, but since  — n I have decided that it was
for want of preparation.  I had no proper basis.  Only Spencer and myself
know how hard I hammered.  But I did get something out of his _Data of
Ethics_.   — re’s where I ran across ‘altruism,’ and I remember now how
it was used.”

I wondered what this man could have got from such a work.  Spencer I
remembered enough to know that altruism was imperative to his ideal of
highest conduct.  Wolf Larsen, evidently, had sifted  —  great
philosopher’s teachings, rejecting and selecting according to his needs
and desires.

“What else did you run across?” I asked.

His brows drew in slightly with  —  mental effort of suitably phrasing
thoughts which he had never before put into speech.  I felt an elation of
spirit.  I was groping into his soul-stuff as he made a practice of
groping in  —  soul-stuff of o — rs.  I was exploring virgin territory.
A strange, a terribly strange, region was unrolling itself before my
eyes.

“In as few words as possible,” he began, “Spencer puts it something like
this: First, a man must act for his own benefit—to do this is to be moral
and good.  Next, he must act for  —  benefit of his children.  And third,
he must act for  —  benefit of his race.”

“And  —  highest, finest, right conduct,” I interjected, “is that act
which benefits at  —  same time  —  man, his children, and his race.”

“I wouldn’t stand for that,” he replied.  “Couldn’t see  —  necessity for
it, nor  —  common sense.  I cut out  —  race and  —  children.  I would
sacrifice nothing for  — m.  It’s just so much slush and sentiment, and
you must see it yourself, at least for one who does not believe in
eternal life.  With immortality before me, altruism would be a paying
business proposition.  I might elevate my soul to all kinds of altitudes.
But with nothing eternal before me but death, given for a brief spell
this yeasty crawling and squirming which is called life, why, it would be
immoral for me to perform any act that was a sacrifice.  Any sacrifice
that makes me lose one crawl or squirm is foolish,—and not only foolish,
for it is a wrong against myself and a wicked thing.  I must not lose one
crawl or squirm if I am to get  —  most out of  —  ferment.  Nor will  — 
eternal movelessness that is coming to me be made easier or harder by  — 
sacrifices or selfishnesses of  —  time when I was yeasty and acrawl.”

“ — n you are an individualist, a materialist, and, logically, a
hedonist.”

“Big words,” he smiled.  “But what is a hedonist?”

He nodded agreement when I had given  —  definition.  “And you are also,”
I continued, “a man one could not trust in  —  least thing where it was
possible for a selfish interest to intervene?”

“Now you’re beginning to understand,” he said, brightening.

“You are a man utterly without what  —  world calls morals?”

“That’s it.”

“A man of whom to be always afraid—”

“That’s  —  way to put it.”

“As one is afraid of a snake, or a tiger, or a shark?”

“Now you know me,” he said.  “And you know me as I am generally known.
O — r men call me ‘Wolf.’”

“You are a sort of monster,” I added audaciously, “a Caliban who has
pondered Setebos, and who acts as you act, in idle moments, by whim and
fancy.”

His brow clouded at  —  allusion.  He did not understand, and I quickly
learned that he did not know  —  poem.

“I’m just reading Browning,” he confessed, “and it’s pretty tough.  I
haven’t got very far along, and as it is I’ve about lost my bearings.”

Not to be tiresome, I shall say that I fetched  —  book from his
state-room and read “Caliban” aloud.  He was delighted.  It was a
primitive mode of reasoning and of looking at things that he understood
thoroughly.  He interrupted again and again with comment and criticism.
When I finished, he had me read it over a second time, and a third.  We
fell into discussion—philosophy, science, evolution, religion.  He
betrayed  —  inaccuracies of  —  self-read man, and, it must be granted,
 —  sureness and directness of  —  primitive mind.   —  very simplicity
of his reasoning was its strength, and his materialism was far more
compelling than  —  subtly complex materialism of Charley Furuseth.  Not
that I—a confirmed and, as Furuseth phrased it, a temperamental
idealist—was to be compelled; but that Wolf Larsen stormed  —  last
strongholds of my faith with a vigour that received respect, while not
accorded conviction.

Time passed.  Supper was at hand and  —  table not laid.  I became
restless and anxious, and when Thomas Mugridge glared down  — 
companion-way, sick and angry of countenance, I prepared to go about my
duties.  But Wolf Larsen cried out to him:

“Cooky, you’ve got to hustle to-night.  I’m busy with Hump, and you’ll do
 —  best you can without him.”

And again  —  unprecedented was established.  That night I sat at table
with  —  captain and  —  hunters, while Thomas Mugridge waited on us and
washed  —  dishes afterward—a whim, a Caliban-mood of Wolf Larsen’s, and
one I foresaw would bring me trouble.  In  —  meantime we talked and
talked, much to  —  disgust of  —  hunters, who could not understand a
word.




CHAPTER 9


Three days of rest, three blessed days of rest, are what I had with Wolf
Larsen, eating at  —  cabin table and doing nothing but discuss life,
literature, and  —  universe,  —  while Thomas Mugridge fumed and raged
and did my work as well as his own.

“Watch out for squalls, is all I can say to you,” was Louis’s warning,
given during a spare half-hour on deck while Wolf Larsen was engaged in
straightening out a row among  —  hunters.

“Ye can’t tell what’ll be happenin’,” Louis went on, in response to my
query for more definite information.  “ —  man’s as contrary as air
currents or water currents.  You can never guess  —  ways iv him.  ’Tis
just as you’re thinkin’ you know him and are makin’ a favourable slant
along him, that he whirls around, dead ahead and comes howlin’ down upon
you and a-rippin’ all iv your fine-wea — r sails to rags.”

So I was not altoge — r surprised when  —  squall foretold by Louis smote
me.  We had been having a heated discussion,—upon life, of course,—and,
grown over-bold, I was passing stiff strictures upon Wolf Larsen and  — 
life of Wolf Larsen.  In fact, I was vivisecting him and turning over his
soul-stuff as keenly and thoroughly as it was his custom to do it to
o — rs.  It may be a weakness of mine that I have an incisive way of
speech; but I threw all restraint to  —  winds and cut and slashed until
 —  whole man of him was snarling.   —  dark sun-bronze of his face went
black with wrath, his eyes were ablaze.   — re was no clearness or sanity
in  — m—nothing but  —  terrific rage of a madman.  It was  —  wolf in
him that I saw, and a mad wolf at that.

He sprang for me with a half-roar, gripping my arm.  I had steeled myself
to brazen it out, though I was trembling inwardly; but  —  enormous
strength of  —  man was too much for my fortitude.  He had gripped me by
 —  biceps with his single hand, and when that grip tightened I wilted
and shrieked aloud.  My feet went out from under me.  I simply could not
stand upright and endure  —  agony.   —  muscles refused  — ir duty.   — 
pain was too great.  My biceps was being crushed to a pulp.

He seemed to recover himself, for a lucid gleam came into his eyes, and
he relaxed his hold with a short laugh that was more like a growl.  I
fell to  —  floor, feeling very faint, while he sat down, lighted a
cigar, and watched me as a cat watches a mouse.  As I wri — d about I
could see in his eyes that curiosity I had so often noted, that wonder
and perplexity, that questing, that everlasting query of his as to what
it was all about.

I finally crawled to my feet and ascended  —  companion stairs.  Fair
wea — r was over, and  — re was nothing left but to return to  —  galley.
My left arm was numb, as though paralysed, and days passed before I could
use it, while weeks went by before  —  last stiffness and pain went out
of it.  And he had done nothing but put his hand upon my arm and squeeze.
 — re had been no wrenching or jerking.  He had just closed his hand with
a steady pressure.  What he might have done I did not fully realize till
next day, when he put his head into  —  galley, and, as a sign of renewed
friendliness, asked me how my arm was getting on.

“It might have been worse,” he smiled.

I was peeling potatoes.  He picked one up from  —  pan.  It was
fair-sized, firm, and unpeeled.  He closed his hand upon it, squeezed,
and  —  potato squirted out between his fingers in mushy streams.   — 
pulpy remnant he dropped back into  —  pan and turned away, and I had a
sharp vision of how it might have fared with me had  —  monster put his
real strength upon me.

But  —  three days’ rest was good in spite of it all, for it had given my
knee  —  very chance it needed.  It felt much better,  —  swelling had
materially decreased, and  —  cap seemed descending into its proper
place.  Also,  —  three days’ rest brought  —  trouble I had foreseen.
It was plainly Thomas Mugridge’s intention to make me pay for those three
days.  He treated me vilely, cursed me continually, and heaped his own
work upon me.  He even ventured to raise his fist to me, but I was
becoming animal-like myself, and I snarled in his face so terribly that
it must have frightened him back.  It is no pleasant picture I can
conjure up of myself, Humphrey Van Weyden, in that noisome ship’s galley,
crouched in a corner over my task, my face raised to  —  face of  — 
creature about to strike me, my lips lifted and snarling like a dog’s, my
eyes gleaming with fear and helplessness and  —  courage that comes of
fear and helplessness.  I do not like  —  picture.  It reminds me too
strongly of a rat in a trap.  I do not care to think of it; but it was
elective, for  —  threatened blow did not descend.

Thomas Mugridge backed away, glaring as hatefully and viciously as I
glared.  A pair of beasts is what we were, penned toge — r and showing
our teeth.  He was a coward, afraid to strike me because I had not
quailed sufficiently in advance; so he chose a new way to intimidate me.
 — re was only one galley knife that, as a knife, amounted to anything.
This, through many years of service and wear, had acquired a long, lean
blade.  It was unusually cruel-looking, and at first I had shuddered
every time I used it.   —  cook borrowed a stone from Johansen and
proceeded to sharpen  —  knife.  He did it with great ostentation,
glancing significantly at me  —  while.  He whetted it up and down all
day long.  Every odd moment he could find he had  —  knife and stone out
and was whetting away.   —  steel acquired a razor edge.  He tried it
with  —  ball of his thumb or across  —  nail.  He shaved hairs from  — 
back of his hand, glanced along  —  edge with microscopic acuteness, and
found, or feigned that he found, always, a slight inequality in its edge
somewhere.   — n he would put it on  —  stone again and whet, whet, whet,
till I could have laughed aloud, it was so very ludicrous.

It was also serious, for I learned that he was capable of using it, that
under all his cowardice  — re was a courage of cowardice, like mine, that
would impel him to do  —  very thing his whole nature protested against
doing and was afraid of doing.  “Cooky’s sharpening his knife for Hump,”
was being whispered about among  —  sailors, and some of  — m twitted him
about it.  This he took in good part, and was really pleased, nodding his
head with direful foreknowledge and mystery, until George Leach,  — 
erstwhile cabin-boy, ventured some rough pleasantry on  —  subject.

Now it happened that Leach was one of  —  sailors told off to douse
Mugridge after his game of cards with  —  captain.  Leach had evidently
done his task with a thoroughness that Mugridge had not forgiven, for
words followed and evil names involving smirched ancestries.  Mugridge
menaced with  —  knife he was sharpening for me.  Leach laughed and
hurled more of his Telegraph Hill Billingsgate, and before ei — r he or I
knew what had happened, his right arm had been ripped open from elbow to
wrist by a quick slash of  —  knife.   —  cook backed away, a fiendish
expression on his face,  —  knife held before him in a position of
defence.  But Leach took it quite calmly, though blood was spouting upon
 —  deck as generously as water from a fountain.

“I’m goin’ to get you, Cooky,” he said, “and I’ll get you hard.  And I
won’t be in no hurry about it.  You’ll be without that knife when I come
for you.”

So saying, he turned and walked quietly forward.  Mugridge’s face was
livid with fear at what he had done and at what he might expect sooner or
later from  —  man he had stabbed.  But his demeanour toward me was more
ferocious than ever.  In spite of his fear at  —  reckoning he must
expect to pay for what he had done, he could see that it had been an
object-lesson to me, and he became more domineering and exultant.  Also
 — re was a lust in him, akin to madness, which had come with sight of
 —  blood he had drawn.  He was beginning to see red in whatever
direction he looked.   —  psychology of it is sadly tangled, and yet I
could read  —  workings of his mind as clearly as though it were a
printed book.

Several days went by,  —  _Ghost_ still foaming down  —  trades, and I
could swear I saw madness growing in Thomas Mugridge’s eyes.  And I
confess that I became afraid, very much afraid.  Whet, whet, whet, it
went all day long.   —  look in his eyes as he felt  —  keen edge and
glared at me was positively carnivorous.  I was afraid to turn my
shoulder to him, and when I left  —  galley I went out backwards—to  — 
amusement of  —  sailors and hunters, who made a point of ga — ring in
groups to witness my exit.   —  strain was too great.  I sometimes
thought my mind would give way under it—a meet thing on this ship of
madmen and brutes.  Every hour, every minute of my existence was in
jeopardy.  I was a human soul in distress, and yet no soul, fore or aft,
betrayed sufficient sympathy to come to my aid.  At times I thought of
throwing myself on  —  mercy of Wolf Larsen, but  —  vision of  — 
mocking devil in his eyes that questioned life and sneered at it would
come strong upon me and compel me to refrain.  At o — r times I seriously
contemplated suicide, and  —  whole force of my hopeful philosophy was
required to keep me from going over  —  side in  —  darkness of night.

Several times Wolf Larsen tried to inveigle me into discussion, but I
gave him short answers and eluded him.  Finally, he commanded me to
resume my seat at  —  cabin table for a time and let  —  cook do my work.
 — n I spoke frankly, telling him what I was enduring from Thomas
Mugridge because of  —  three days of favouritism which had been shown
me.  Wolf Larsen regarded me with smiling eyes.

“So you’re afraid, eh?” he sneered.

“Yes,” I said defiantly and honestly, “I am afraid.”

“That’s  —  way with you fellows,” he cried, half angrily,
“sentimentalizing about your immortal souls and afraid to die.  At sight
of a sharp knife and a cowardly Cockney  —  clinging of life to life
overcomes all your fond foolishness.  Why, my dear fellow, you will live
for ever.  You are a god, and God cannot be killed.  Cooky cannot hurt
you.  You are sure of your resurrection.  What’s  — re to be afraid of?

“You have eternal life before you.  You are a millionaire in immortality,
and a millionaire whose fortune cannot be lost, whose fortune is less
perishable than  —  stars and as lasting as space or time.  It is
impossible for you to diminish your principal.  Immortality is a thing
without beginning or end.  Eternity is eternity, and though you die here
and now you will go on living somewhere else and hereafter.  And it is
all very beautiful, this shaking off of  —  flesh and soaring of  — 
imprisoned spirit.  Cooky cannot hurt you.  He can only give you a boost
on  —  path you eternally must tread.

“Or, if you do not wish to be boosted just yet, why not boost Cooky?
According to your ideas, he, too, must be an immortal millionaire.  You
cannot bankrupt him.  His paper will always circulate at par.  You cannot
diminish  —  length of his living by killing him, for he is without
beginning or end.  He’s bound to go on living, somewhere, somehow.   — n
boost him.  Stick a knife in him and let his spirit free.  As it is, it’s
in a nasty prison, and you’ll do him only a kindness by breaking down  — 
door.  And who knows?—it may be a very beautiful spirit that will go
soaring up into  —  blue from that ugly carcass.  Boost him along, and
I’ll promote you to his place, and he’s getting forty-five dollars a
month.”

It was plain that I could look for no help or mercy from Wolf Larsen.
Whatever was to be done I must do for myself; and out of  —  courage of
fear I evolved  —  plan of fighting Thomas Mugridge with his own weapons.
I borrowed a whetstone from Johansen.  Louis,  —  boat-steerer, had
already begged me for condensed milk and sugar.   —  lazarette, where
such delicacies were stored, was situated beneath  —  cabin floor.
Watching my chance, I stole five cans of  —  milk, and that night, when
it was Louis’s watch on deck, I traded  — m with him for a dirk as lean
and cruel-looking as Thomas Mugridge’s vegetable knife.  It was rusty and
dull, but I turned  —  grindstone while Louis gave it an edge.  I slept
more soundly than usual that night.

Next morning, after breakfast, Thomas Mugridge began his whet, whet,
whet.  I glanced warily at him, for I was on my knees taking  —  ashes
from  —  stove.  When I returned from throwing  — m overside, he was
talking to Harrison, whose honest yokel’s face was filled with
fascination and wonder.

“Yes,” Mugridge was saying, “an’ wot does ’is worship do but give me two
years in Reading.  But blimey if I cared.   —  o — r mug was fixed
plenty.  Should ’a seen ’im.  Knife just like this.  I stuck it in, like
into soft butter, an’  —  w’y ’e squealed was better’n a tu-penny gaff.”
He shot a glance in my direction to see if I was taking it in, and went
on.  “‘I didn’t mean it Tommy,’ ’e was snifflin’; ‘so ’elp me Gawd, I
didn’t mean it!’  ‘I’ll fix yer bloody well right,’ I sez, an’ kept right
after ’im.  I cut ’im in ribbons, that’s wot I did, an’ ’e a-squealin’
all  —  time.  Once ’e got ’is ’and on  —  knife an’ tried to ’old it.
‘Ad ’is fingers around it, but I pulled it through, cuttin’ to  —  bone.
O, ’e was a sight, I can tell yer.”

A call from  —  mate interrupted  —  gory narrative, and Harrison went
aft.  Mugridge sat down on  —  raised threshold to  —  galley and went on
with his knife-sharpening.  I put  —  shovel away and calmly sat down on
 —  coal-box facing him.  He favoured me with a vicious stare.  Still
calmly, though my heart was going pitapat, I pulled out Louis’s dirk and
began to whet it on  —  stone.  I had looked for almost any sort of
explosion on  —  Cockney’s part, but to my surprise he did not appear
aware of what I was doing.  He went on whetting his knife.  So did I.
And for two hours we sat  — re, face to face, whet, whet, whet, till  — 
news of it spread abroad and half  —  ship’s company was crowding  — 
galley doors to see  —  sight.

Encouragement and advice were freely tendered, and Jock Horner,  — 
quiet, self-spoken hunter who looked as though he would not harm a mouse,
advised me to leave  —  ribs alone and to thrust upward for  —  abdomen,
at  —  same time giving what he called  —  “Spanish twist” to  —  blade.
Leach, his bandaged arm prominently to  —  fore, begged me to leave a few
remnants of  —  cook for him; and Wolf Larsen paused once or twice at  — 
break of  —  poop to glance curiously at what must have been to him a
stirring and crawling of  —  yeasty thing he knew as life.

And I make free to say that for  —  time being life assumed  —  same
sordid values to me.   — re was nothing pretty about it, nothing
divine—only two cowardly moving things that sat whetting steel upon
stone, and a group of o — r moving things, cowardly and o — rwise, that
looked on.  Half of  — m, I am sure, were anxious to see us shedding each
o — r’s blood.  It would have been entertainment.  And I do not think
 — re was one who would have interfered had we closed in a
death-struggle.

On  —  o — r hand,  —  whole thing was laughable and childish.  Whet,
whet, whet,—Humphrey Van Weyden sharpening his knife in a ship’s galley
and trying its edge with his thumb!  Of all situations this was  —  most
inconceivable.  I know that my own kind could not have believed it
possible.  I had not been called “Sissy” Van Weyden all my days without
reason, and that “Sissy” Van Weyden should be capable of doing this thing
was a revelation to Humphrey Van Weyden, who knew not whe — r to be
exultant or ashamed.

But nothing happened.  At  —  end of two hours Thomas Mugridge put away
knife and stone and held out his hand.

“Wot’s  —  good of mykin’ a ’oly show of ourselves for  — m mugs?” he
demanded.  “ — y don’t love us, an’ bloody well glad  — y’d be a-seein’
us cuttin’ our throats.  Yer not ’arf bad, ’Ump!  You’ve got spunk, as
you Yanks s’y, an’ I like yer in a w’y.  So come on an’ shyke.”

Coward that I might be, I was less a coward than he.  It was a distinct
victory I had gained, and I refused to forego any of it by shaking his
detestable hand.

“All right,” he said pridelessly, “tyke it or leave it, I’ll like yer
none  —  less for it.”  And to save his face he turned fiercely upon  — 
onlookers.  “Get outa my galley-doors, you bloomin’ swabs!”

This command was reinforced by a steaming kettle of water, and at sight
of it  —  sailors scrambled out of  —  way.  This was a sort of victory
for Thomas Mugridge, and enabled him to accept more gracefully  —  defeat
I had given him, though, of course, he was too discreet to attempt to
drive  —  hunters away.

“I see Cooky’s finish,” I heard Smoke say to Horner.

“You bet,” was  —  reply.  “Hump runs  —  galley from now on, and Cooky
pulls in his horns.”

Mugridge heard and shot a swift glance at me, but I gave no sign that  — 
conversation had reached me.  I had not thought my victory was so
far-reaching and complete, but I resolved to let go nothing I had gained.
As  —  days went by, Smoke’s prophecy was verified.   —  Cockney became
more humble and slavish to me than even to Wolf Larsen.  I mistered him
and sirred him no longer, washed no more greasy pots, and peeled no more
potatoes.  I did my own work, and my own work only, and when and in what
fashion I saw fit.  Also I carried  —  dirk in a sheath at my hip,
sailor-fashion, and maintained toward Thomas Mugridge a constant attitude
which was composed of equal parts of domineering, insult, and contempt.




CHAPTER 10


My intimacy with Wolf Larsen increases—if by intimacy may be denoted
those relations which exist between master and man, or, better yet,
between king and jester.  I am to him no more than a toy, and he values
me no more than a child values a toy.  My function is to amuse, and so
long as I amuse all goes well; but let him become bored, or let him have
one of his black moods come upon him, and at once I am relegated from
cabin table to galley, while, at  —  same time, I am fortunate to escape
with my life and a whole body.

 —  loneliness of  —  man is slowly being borne in upon me.   — re is not
a man aboard but hates or fears him, nor is  — re a man whom he does not
despise.  He seems consuming with  —  tremendous power that is in him and
that seems never to have found adequate expression in works.  He is as
Lucifer would be, were that proud spirit banished to a society of
soulless, Tomlinsonian ghosts.

This loneliness is bad enough in itself, but, to make it worse, he is
oppressed by  —  primal melancholy of  —  race.  Knowing him, I review
 —  old Scandinavian myths with clearer understanding.   — 
white-skinned, fair-haired savages who created that terrible pan — on
were of  —  same fibre as he.   —  frivolity of  —  laughter-loving
Latins is no part of him.  When he laughs it is from a humour that is
nothing else than ferocious.  But he laughs rarely; he is too often sad.
And it is a sadness as deep-reaching as  —  roots of  —  race.  It is  — 
race heritage,  —  sadness which has made  —  race sober-minded,
clean-lived and fanatically moral, and which, in this latter connection,
has culminated among  —  English in  —  Reformed Church and Mrs. Grundy.

In point of fact,  —  chief vent to this primal melancholy has been
religion in its more agonizing forms.  But  —  compensations of such
religion are denied Wolf Larsen.  His brutal materialism will not permit
it.  So, when his blue moods come on, nothing remains for him, but to be
devilish.  Were he not so terrible a man, I could sometimes feel sorry
for him, as instance three mornings ago, when I went into his stateroom
to fill his water-bottle and came unexpectedly upon him.  He did not see
me.  His head was buried in his hands, and his shoulders were heaving
convulsively as with sobs.  He seemed torn by some mighty grief.  As I
softly withdrew I could hear him groaning, “God!  God!  God!”  Not that
he was calling upon God; it was a mere expletive, but it came from his
soul.

At dinner he asked  —  hunters for a remedy for headache, and by evening,
strong man that he was, he was half-blind and reeling about  —  cabin.

“I’ve never been sick in my life, Hump,” he said, as I guided him to his
room.  “Nor did I ever have a headache except  —  time my head was
healing after having been laid open for six inches by a capstan-bar.”

For three days this blinding headache lasted, and he suffered as wild
animals suffer, as it seemed  —  way on ship to suffer, without plaint,
without sympathy, utterly alone.

This morning, however, on entering his state-room to make  —  bed and put
things in order, I found him well and hard at work.  Table and bunk were
littered with designs and calculations.  On a large transparent sheet,
compass and square in hand, he was copying what appeared to be a scale of
some sort or o — r.

“Hello, Hump,” he greeted me genially.  “I’m just finishing  —  finishing
touches.  Want to see it work?”

“But what is it?” I asked.

“A labour-saving device for mariners, navigation reduced to kindergarten
simplicity,” he answered gaily.  “From to-day a child will be able to
navigate a ship.  No more long-winded calculations.  All you need is one
star in  —  sky on a dirty night to know instantly where you are.  Look.
I place  —  transparent scale on this star-map, revolving  —  scale on
 —  North Pole.  On  —  scale I’ve worked out  —  circles of altitude and
 —  lines of bearing.  All I do is to put it on a star, revolve  —  scale
till it is opposite those figures on  —  map underneath, and presto!
 — re you are,  —  ship’s precise location!”

 — re was a ring of triumph in his voice, and his eyes, clear blue this
morning as  —  sea, were sparkling with light.

“You must be well up in ma — matics,” I said.  “Where did you go to
school?”

“Never saw  —  inside of one, worse luck,” was  —  answer.  “I had to dig
it out for myself.”

“And why do you think I have made this thing?” he demanded, abruptly.
“Dreaming to leave footprints on  —  sands of time?”  He laughed one of
his horrible mocking laughs.  “Not at all.  To get it patented, to make
money from it, to revel in piggishness with all night in while o — r men
do  —  work.  That’s my purpose.  Also, I have enjoyed working it out.”

“ —  creative joy,” I murmured.

“I guess that’s what it ought to be called.  Which is ano — r way of
expressing  —  joy of life in that it is alive,  —  triumph of movement
over matter, of  —  quick over  —  dead,  —  pride of  —  yeast because
it is yeast and crawls.”

I threw up my hands with helpless disapproval of his inveterate
materialism and went about making  —  bed.  He continued copying lines
and figures upon  —  transparent scale.  It was a task requiring  — 
utmost nicety and precision, and I could not but admire  —  way he
tempered his strength to  —  fineness and delicacy of  —  need.

When I had finished  —  bed, I caught myself looking at him in a
fascinated sort of way.  He was certainly a handsome man—beautiful in  — 
masculine sense.  And again, with never-failing wonder, I remarked  — 
total lack of viciousness, or wickedness, or sinfulness in his face.  It
was  —  face, I am convinced, of a man who did no wrong.  And by this I
do not wish to be misunderstood.  What I mean is that it was  —  face of
a man who ei — r did nothing contrary to  —  dictates of his conscience,
or who had no conscience.  I am inclined to  —  latter way of accounting
for it.  He was a magnificent atavism, a man so purely primitive that he
was of  —  type that came into  —  world before  —  development of  — 
moral nature.  He was not immoral, but merely unmoral.

As I have said, in  —  masculine sense his was a beautiful face.
Smooth-shaven, every line was distinct, and it was cut as clear and sharp
as a cameo; while sea and sun had tanned  —  naturally fair skin to a
dark bronze which bespoke struggle and battle and added both to his
savagery and his beauty.   —  lips were full, yet possessed of  — 
firmness, almost harshness, which is characteristic of thin lips.   — 
set of his mouth, his chin, his jaw, was likewise firm or harsh, with all
 —  fierceness and indomitableness of  —  male— —  nose also.  It was  — 
nose of a being born to conquer and command.  It just hinted of  —  eagle
beak.  It might have been Grecian, it might have been Roman, only it was
a shade too massive for  —  one, a shade too delicate for  —  o — r.  And
while  —  whole face was  —  incarnation of fierceness and strength,  — 
primal melancholy from which he suffered seemed to greaten  —  lines of
mouth and eye and brow, seemed to give a largeness and completeness which
o — rwise  —  face would have lacked.

And so I caught myself standing idly and studying him.  I cannot say how
greatly  —  man had come to interest me.  Who was he?  What was he?  How
had he happened to be?  All powers seemed his, all potentialities—why,
 — n, was he no more than  —  obscure master of a seal-hunting schooner
with a reputation for frightful brutality amongst  —  men who hunted
seals?

My curiosity burst from me in a flood of speech.

“Why is it that you have not done great things in this world?  With  — 
power that is yours you might have risen to any height.  Unpossessed of
conscience or moral instinct, you might have mastered  —  world, broken
it to your hand.  And yet here you are, at  —  top of your life, where
diminishing and dying begin, living an obscure and sordid existence,
hunting sea animals for  —  satisfaction of woman’s vanity and love of
decoration, revelling in a piggishness, to use your own words, which is
anything and everything except splendid.  Why, with all that wonderful
strength, have you not done something?   — re was nothing to stop you,
nothing that could stop you.  What was wrong?  Did you lack ambition?
Did you fall under temptation?  What was  —  matter?  What was  — 
matter?”

He had lifted his eyes to me at  —  commencement of my outburst, and
followed me complacently until I had done and stood before him breathless
and dismayed.  He waited a moment, as though seeking where to begin, and
 — n said:

“Hump, do you know  —  parable of  —  sower who went forth to sow?  If
you will remember, some of  —  seed fell upon stony places, where  — re
was not much earth, and forthwith  — y sprung up because  — y had no
deepness of earth.  And when  —  sun was up  — y were scorched, and
because  — y had no root  — y wi — red away.  And some fell among thorns,
and  —  thorns sprung up and choked  — m.”

“Well?” I said.

“Well?” he queried, half petulantly.  “It was not well.  I was one of
those seeds.”

He dropped his head to  —  scale and resumed  —  copying.  I finished my
work and had opened  —  door to leave, when he spoke to me.

“Hump, if you will look on  —  west coast of  —  map of Norway you will
see an indentation called Romsdal Fiord.  I was born within a hundred
miles of that stretch of water.  But I was not born Norwegian.  I am a
Dane.  My fa — r and mo — r were Danes, and how  — y ever came to that
bleak bight of land on  —  west coast I do not know.  I never heard.
Outside of that  — re is nothing mysterious.   — y were poor people and
unlettered.   — y came of generations of poor unlettered people—peasants
of  —  sea who sowed  — ir sons on  —  waves as has been  — ir custom
since time began.   — re is no more to tell.”

“But  — re is,” I objected.  “It is still obscure to me.”

“What can I tell you?” he demanded, with a recrudescence of fierceness.
“Of  —  meagreness of a child’s life? of fish diet and coarse living? of
going out with  —  boats from  —  time I could crawl? of my bro — rs, who
went away one by one to  —  deep-sea farming and never came back? of
myself, unable to read or write, cabin-boy at  —  mature age of ten on
 —  coastwise, old-country ships? of  —  rough fare and rougher usage,
where kicks and blows were bed and breakfast and took  —  place of
speech, and fear and hatred and pain were my only soul-experiences?  I do
not care to remember.  A madness comes up in my brain even now as I think
of it.  But  — re were coastwise skippers I would have returned and
killed when a man’s strength came to me, only  —  lines of my life were
cast at  —  time in o — r places.  I did return, not long ago, but
unfortunately  —  skippers were dead, all but one, a mate in  —  old
days, a skipper when I met him, and when I left him a cripple who would
never walk again.”

“But you who read Spencer and Darwin and have never seen  —  inside of a
school, how did you learn to read and write?” I queried.

“In  —  English merchant service.  Cabin-boy at twelve, ship’s boy at
fourteen, ordinary seamen at sixteen, able seaman at seventeen, and cock
of  —  fo’c’sle, infinite ambition and infinite loneliness, receiving
nei — r help nor sympathy, I did it all for myself—navigation,
ma — matics, science, literature, and what not.  And of what use has it
been?  Master and owner of a ship at  —  top of my life, as you say, when
I am beginning to diminish and die.  Paltry, isn’t it?  And when  —  sun
was up I was scorched, and because I had no root I wi — red away.”

“But history tells of slaves who rose to  —  purple,” I chided.

“And history tells of opportunities that came to  —  slaves who rose to
 —  purple,” he answered grimly.  “No man makes opportunity.  All  — 
great men ever did was to know it when it came to  — m.   —  Corsican
knew.  I have dreamed as greatly as  —  Corsican.  I should have known
 —  opportunity, but it never came.   —  thorns sprung up and choked me.
And, Hump, I can tell you that you know more about me than any living
man, except my own bro — r.”

“And what is he?  And where is he?”

“Master of  —  steamship _Macedonia_, seal-hunter,” was  —  answer.  “We
will meet him most probably on  —  Japan coast.  Men call him ‘Death’
Larsen.”

“Death Larsen!” I involuntarily cried.  “Is he like you?”

“Hardly.  He is a lump of an animal without any head.  He has all my—my—”

“Brutishness,” I suggested.

“Yes,—thank you for  —  word,—all my brutishness, but he can scarcely
read or write.”

“And he has never philosophized on life,” I added.

“No,” Wolf Larsen answered, with an indescribable air of sadness.  “And
he is all  —  happier for leaving life alone.  He is too busy living it
to think about it.  My mistake was in ever opening  —  books.”




CHAPTER 11


 —  Ghost has attained  —  sou — rnmost point of  —  arc she is
describing across  —  Pacific, and is already beginning to edge away to
 —  west and north toward some lone island, it is rumoured, where she
will fill her water-casks before proceeding to  —  season’s hunt along
 —  coast of Japan.   —  hunters have experimented and practised with
 — ir rifles and shotguns till  — y are satisfied, and  —  boat-pullers
and steerers have made  — ir spritsails, bound  —  oars and rowlocks in
lea — r and sennit so that  — y will make no noise when creeping on  — 
seals, and put  — ir boats in apple-pie order—to use Leach’s homely
phrase.

His arm, by  —  way, has healed nicely, though  —  scar will remain all
his life.  Thomas Mugridge lives in mortal fear of him, and is afraid to
venture on deck after dark.   — re are two or three standing quarrels in
 —  forecastle.  Louis tells me that  —  gossip of  —  sailors finds its
way aft, and that two of  —  telltales have been badly beaten by  — ir
mates.  He shakes his head dubiously over  —  outlook for  —  man
Johnson, who is boat-puller in  —  same boat with him.  Johnson has been
guilty of speaking his mind too freely, and has collided two or three
times with Wolf Larsen over  —  pronunciation of his name.  Johansen he
thrashed on  —  amidships deck  —  o — r night, since which time  —  mate
has called him by his proper name.  But of course it is out of  — 
question that Johnson should thrash Wolf Larsen.
