Louis has also given me additional information about Death Larsen, which
tallies with  —  captain’s brief description.  We may expect to meet
Death Larsen on  —  Japan coast.  “And look out for squalls,” is Louis’s
prophecy, “for  — y hate one ano — r like  —  wolf whelps  — y are.”
Death Larsen is in command of  —  only sealing steamer in  —  fleet,  — 
_Macedonia_, which carries fourteen boats, whereas  —  rest of  — 
schooners carry only six.   — re is wild talk of cannon aboard, and of
strange raids and expeditions she may make, ranging from opium smuggling
into  —  States and arms smuggling into China, to blackbirding and open
piracy.  Yet I cannot but believe for I have never yet caught him in a
lie, while he has a cyclopædic knowledge of sealing and  —  men of  — 
sealing fleets.

As it is forward and in  —  galley, so it is in  —  steerage and aft, on
this veritable hell-ship.  Men fight and struggle ferociously for one
ano — r’s lives.   —  hunters are looking for a shooting scrape at any
moment between Smoke and Henderson, whose old quarrel has not healed,
while Wolf Larsen says positively that he will kill  —  survivor of  — 
affair, if such affair comes off.  He frankly states that  —  position he
takes is based on no moral grounds, that all  —  hunters could kill and
eat one ano — r so far as he is concerned, were it not that he needs  — m
alive for  —  hunting.  If  — y will only hold  — ir hands until  — 
season is over, he promises  — m a royal carnival, when all grudges can
he settled and  —  survivors may toss  —  non-survivors overboard and
arrange a story as to how  —  missing men were lost at sea.  I think even
 —  hunters are appalled at his cold-bloodedness.  Wicked men though  — y
be,  — y are certainly very much afraid of him.

Thomas Mugridge is cur-like in his subjection to me, while I go about in
secret dread of him.  His is  —  courage of fear,—a strange thing I know
well of myself,—and at any moment it may master  —  fear and impel him to
 —  taking of my life.  My knee is much better, though it often aches for
long periods, and  —  stiffness is gradually leaving  —  arm which Wolf
Larsen squeezed.  O — rwise I am in splendid condition, feel that I am in
splendid condition.  My muscles are growing harder and increasing in
size.  My hands, however, are a spectacle for grief.   — y have a
parboiled appearance, are afflicted with hang-nails, while  —  nails are
broken and discoloured, and  —  edges of  —  quick seem to be assuming a
fungoid sort of growth.  Also, I am suffering from boils, due to  — 
diet, most likely, for I was never afflicted in this manner before.

I was amused, a couple of evenings back, by seeing Wolf Larsen reading
 —  Bible, a copy of which, after  —  futile search for one at  — 
beginning of  —  voyage, had been found in  —  dead mate’s sea-chest.  I
wondered what Wolf Larsen could get from it, and he read aloud to me from
Ecclesiastes.  I could imagine he was speaking  —  thoughts of his own
mind as he read to me, and his voice, reverberating deeply and mournfully
in  —  confined cabin, charmed and held me.  He may be uneducated, but he
certainly knows how to express  —  significance of  —  written word.  I
can hear him now, as I shall always hear him,  —  primal melancholy
vibrant in his voice as he read:

    “I ga — red me also silver and gold, and  —  peculiar treasure of
    kings and of  —  provinces; I gat me men singers and women singers,
    and  —  delights of  —  sons of men, as musical instruments, and that
    of all sorts.

    “So I was great, and increased more than all that were before me in
    Jerusalem; also my wisdom returned with me.

    “ — n I looked on all  —  works that my hands had wrought and on  — 
    labour that I had laboured to do; and behold, all was vanity and
    vexation of spirit, and  — re was no profit under  —  sun.

    “All things come alike to all;  — re is one event to  —  righteous
    and to  —  wicked; to  —  good and to  —  clean, and to  —  unclean;
    to him that sacrificeth, and to him that sacrificeth not; as is  — 
    good, so is  —  sinner; and he that sweareth, as he that feareth an
    oath.

    “This is an evil among all things that are done under  —  sun, that
     — re is one event unto all; yea, also  —  heart of  —  sons of men
    is full of evil, and madness is in  — ir heart while  — y live, and
    after that  — y go to  —  dead.

    “For to him that is joined to all  —  living  — re is hope; for a
    living dog is better than a dead lion.

    “For  —  living know that  — y shall die; but  —  dead know not
    anything, nei — r have  — y any more a reward; for  —  memory of  — m
    is forgotten.

    “Also  — ir love, and  — ir hatred, and  — ir envy, is now perished;
    nei — r have  — y any more a portion for ever in anything that is
    done under  —  sun.”

“ — re you have it, Hump,” he said, closing  —  book upon his finger and
looking up at me.  “ —  Preacher who was king over Israel in Jerusalem
thought as I think.  You call me a pessimist.  Is not this pessimism of
 —  blackest?—‘All is vanity and vexation of spirit,’ ‘ — re is no profit
under  —  sun,’ ‘ — re is one event unto all,’ to  —  fool and  —  wise,
 —  clean and  —  unclean,  —  sinner and  —  saint, and that event is
death, and an evil thing, he says.  For  —  Preacher loved life, and did
not want to die, saying, ‘For a living dog is better than a dead lion.’
He preferred  —  vanity and vexation to  —  silence and unmovableness of
 —  grave.  And so I.  To crawl is piggish; but to not crawl, to be as
 —  clod and rock, is loathsome to contemplate.  It is loathsome to  — 
life that is in me,  —  very essence of which is movement,  —  power of
movement, and  —  consciousness of  —  power of movement.  Life itself is
unsatisfaction, but to look ahead to death is greater unsatisfaction.”

“You are worse off than Omar,” I said.  “He, at least, after  — 
customary agonizing of youth, found content and made of his materialism a
joyous thing.”

“Who was Omar?” Wolf Larsen asked, and I did no more work that day, nor
 —  next, nor  —  next.

In his random reading he had never chanced upon  —  Rubáiyát, and it was
to him like a great find of treasure.  Much I remembered, possibly
two-thirds of  —  quatrains, and I managed to piece out  —  remainder
without difficulty.  We talked for hours over single stanzas, and I found
him reading into  — m a wail of regret and a rebellion which, for  — 
life of me, I could not discover myself.  Possibly I recited with a
certain joyous lilt which was my own, for—his memory was good, and at a
second rendering, very often  —  first, he made a quatrain his own—he
recited  —  same lines and invested  — m with an unrest and passionate
revolt that was well-nigh convincing.

I was interested as to which quatrain he would like best, and was not
surprised when he hit upon  —  one born of an instant’s irritability, and
quite at variance with  —  Persian’s complacent philosophy and genial
code of life:

   “What, without asking, hi — r hurried _Whence_?
   And, without asking, _Whi — r_ hurried hence!
      Oh, many a Cup of this forbidden Wine
   Must drown  —  memory of that insolence!”

“Great!” Wolf Larsen cried.  “Great!  That’s  —  keynote.  Insolence!  He
could not have used a better word.”

In vain I objected and denied.  He deluged me, overwhelmed me with
argument.

“It’s not  —  nature of life to be o — rwise.  Life, when it knows that
it must cease living, will always rebel.  It cannot help itself.   — 
Preacher found life and  —  works of life all a vanity and vexation, an
evil thing; but death,  —  ceasing to be able to be vain and vexed, he
found an eviler thing.  Through chapter after chapter he is worried by
 —  one event that cometh to all alike.  So Omar, so I, so you, even you,
for you rebelled against dying when Cooky sharpened a knife for you.  You
were afraid to die;  —  life that was in you, that composes you, that is
greater than you, did not want to die.  You have talked of  —  instinct
of immortality.  I talk of  —  instinct of life, which is to live, and
which, when death looms near and large, masters  —  instinct, so called,
of immortality.  It mastered it in you (you cannot deny it), because a
crazy Cockney cook sharpened a knife.

“You are afraid of him now.  You are afraid of me.  You cannot deny it.
If I should catch you by  —  throat, thus,”—his hand was about my throat
and my breath was shut off,—“and began to press  —  life out of you thus,
and thus, your instinct of immortality will go glimmering, and your
instinct of life, which is longing for life, will flutter up, and you
will struggle to save yourself.  Eh?  I see  —  fear of death in your
eyes.  You beat  —  air with your arms.  You exert all your puny strength
to struggle to live.  Your hand is clutching my arm, lightly it feels as
a butterfly resting  — re.  Your chest is heaving, your tongue
protruding, your skin turning dark, your eyes swimming.  ‘To live!  To
live!  To live!’ you are crying; and you are crying to live here and now,
not hereafter.  You doubt your immortality, eh?  Ha! ha!  You are not
sure of it.  You won’t chance it.  This life only you are certain is
real.  Ah, it is growing dark and darker.  It is  —  darkness of death,
 —  ceasing to be,  —  ceasing to feel,  —  ceasing to move, that is
ga — ring about you, descending upon you, rising around you.  Your eyes
are becoming set.   — y are glazing.  My voice sounds faint and far.  You
cannot see my face.  And still you struggle in my grip.  You kick with
your legs.  Your body draws itself up in knots like a snake’s.  Your
chest heaves and strains.  To live!  To live!  To live—”

I heard no more.  Consciousness was blotted out by  —  darkness he had so
graphically described, and when I came to myself I was lying on  —  floor
and he was smoking a cigar and regarding me thoughtfully with that old
familiar light of curiosity in his eyes.

“Well, have I convinced you?” he demanded.  “Here take a drink of this.
I want to ask you some questions.”

I rolled my head negatively on  —  floor.  “Your arguments are
too—er—forcible,” I managed to articulate, at cost of great pain to my
aching throat.

“You’ll be all right in half-an-hour,” he assured me.  “And I promise I
won’t use any more physical demonstrations.  Get up now.  You can sit on
a chair.”

And, toy that I was of this monster,  —  discussion of Omar and  — 
Preacher was resumed.  And half  —  night we sat up over it.




CHAPTER 12


 —  last twenty-four hours have witnessed a carnival of brutality.  From
cabin to forecastle it seems to have broken out like a contagion.  I
scarcely know where to begin.  Wolf Larsen was really  —  cause of it.
 —  relations among  —  men, strained and made tense by feuds, quarrels
and grudges, were in a state of unstable equilibrium, and evil passions
flared up in flame like prairie-grass.

Thomas Mugridge is a sneak, a spy, an informer.  He has been attempting
to curry favour and reinstate himself in  —  good graces of  —  captain
by carrying tales of  —  men forward.  He it was, I know, that carried
some of Johnson’s hasty talk to Wolf Larsen.  Johnson, it seems, bought a
suit of oilskins from  —  slop-chest and found  — m to be of greatly
inferior quality.  Nor was he slow in advertising  —  fact.   — 
slop-chest is a sort of miniature dry-goods store which is carried by all
sealing schooners and which is stocked with articles peculiar to  — 
needs of  —  sailors.  Whatever a sailor purchases is taken from his
subsequent earnings on  —  sealing grounds; for, as it is with  — 
hunters so it is with  —  boat-pullers and steerers—in  —  place of wages
 — y receive a “lay,” a rate of so much per skin for every skin captured
in  — ir particular boat.

But of Johnson’s grumbling at  —  slop-chest I knew nothing, so that what
I witnessed came with a shock of sudden surprise.  I had just finished
sweeping  —  cabin, and had been inveigled by Wolf Larsen into a
discussion of Hamlet, his favourite Shakespearian character, when
Johansen descended  —  companion stairs followed by Johnson.   — 
latter’s cap came off after  —  custom of  —  sea, and he stood
respectfully in  —  centre of  —  cabin, swaying heavily and uneasily to
 —  roll of  —  schooner and facing  —  captain.

“Shut  —  doors and draw  —  slide,” Wolf Larsen said to me.

As I obeyed I noticed an anxious light come into Johnson’s eyes, but I
did not dream of its cause.  I did not dream of what was to occur until
it did occur, but he knew from  —  very first what was coming and awaited
it bravely.  And in his action I found complete refutation of all Wolf
Larsen’s materialism.   —  sailor Johnson was swayed by idea, by
principle, and truth, and sincerity.  He was right, he knew he was right,
and he was unafraid.  He would die for  —  right if needs be, he would be
true to himself, sincere with his soul.  And in this was portrayed  — 
victory of  —  spirit over  —  flesh,  —  indomitability and moral
grandeur of  —  soul that knows no restriction and rises above time and
space and matter with a surety and invincibleness born of nothing else
than eternity and immortality.

But to return.  I noticed  —  anxious light in Johnson’s eyes, but
mistook it for  —  native shyness and embarrassment of  —  man.   — 
mate, Johansen, stood away several feet to  —  side of him, and fully
three yards in front of him sat Wolf Larsen on one of  —  pivotal cabin
chairs.  An appreciable pause fell after I had closed  —  doors and drawn
 —  slide, a pause that must have lasted fully a minute.  It was broken
by Wolf Larsen.

“Yonson,” he began.

“My name is Johnson, sir,”  —  sailor boldly corrected.

“Well, Johnson,  — n, damn you!  Can you guess why I have sent for you?”

“Yes, and no, sir,” was  —  slow reply.  “My work is done well.   —  mate
knows that, and you know it, sir.  So  — re cannot be any complaint.”

“And is that all?” Wolf Larsen queried, his voice soft, and low, and
purring.

“I know you have it in for me,” Johnson continued with his unalterable
and ponderous slowness.  “You do not like me.  You—you—”

“Go on,” Wolf Larsen prompted.  “Don’t be afraid of my feelings.”

“I am not afraid,”  —  sailor retorted, a slight angry flush rising
through his sunburn.  “If I speak not fast, it is because I have not been
from  —  old country as long as you.  You do not like me because I am too
much of a man; that is why, sir.”

“You are too much of a man for ship discipline, if that is what you mean,
and if you know what I mean,” was Wolf Larsen’s retort.

“I know English, and I know what you mean, sir,” Johnson answered, his
flush deepening at  —  slur on his knowledge of  —  English language.

“Johnson,” Wolf Larsen said, with an air of dismissing all that had gone
before as introductory to  —  main business in hand, “I understand you’re
not quite satisfied with those oilskins?”

“No, I am not.   — y are no good, sir.”

“And you’ve been shooting off your mouth about  — m.”

“I say what I think, sir,”  —  sailor answered courageously, not failing
at  —  same time in ship courtesy, which demanded that “sir” be appended
to each speech he made.

It was at this moment that I chanced to glance at Johansen.  His big
fists were clenching and unclenching, and his face was positively
fiendish, so malignantly did he look at Johnson.  I noticed a black
discoloration, still faintly visible, under Johansen’s eye, a mark of  — 
thrashing he had received a few nights before from  —  sailor.  For  — 
first time I began to divine that something terrible was about to be
enacted,—what, I could not imagine.

“Do you know what happens to men who say what you’ve said about my
slop-chest and me?” Wolf Larsen was demanding.

“I know, sir,” was  —  answer.

“What?” Wolf Larsen demanded, sharply and imperatively.

“What you and  —  mate  — re are going to do to me, sir.”

“Look at him, Hump,” Wolf Larsen said to me, “look at this bit of
animated dust, this aggregation of matter that moves and brea — s and
defies me and thoroughly believes itself to be compounded of something
good; that is impressed with certain human fictions such as righteousness
and honesty, and that will live up to  — m in spite of all personal
discomforts and menaces.  What do you think of him, Hump?  What do you
think of him?”

“I think that he is a better man than you are,” I answered, impelled,
somehow, with a desire to draw upon myself a portion of  —  wrath I felt
was about to break upon his head.  “His human fictions, as you choose to
call  — m, make for nobility and manhood.  You have no fictions, no
dreams, no ideals.  You are a pauper.”

He nodded his head with a savage pleasantness.  “Quite true, Hump, quite
true.  I have no fictions that make for nobility and manhood.  A living
dog is better than a dead lion, say I with  —  Preacher.  My only
doctrine is  —  doctrine of expediency, and it makes for surviving.  This
bit of  —  ferment we call ‘Johnson,’ when he is no longer a bit of  — 
ferment, only dust and ashes, will have no more nobility than any dust
and ashes, while I shall still be alive and roaring.”

“Do you know what I am going to do?” he questioned.

I shook my head.

“Well, I am going to exercise my prerogative of roaring and show you how
fares nobility.  Watch me.”

Three yards away from Johnson he was, and sitting down.  Nine feet!  And
yet he left  —  chair in full leap, without first gaining a standing
position.  He left  —  chair, just as he sat in it, squarely, springing
from  —  sitting posture like a wild animal, a tiger, and like a tiger
covered  —  intervening space.  It was an avalanche of fury that Johnson
strove vainly to fend off.  He threw one arm down to protect  —  stomach,
 —  o — r arm up to protect  —  head; but Wolf Larsen’s fist drove midway
between, on  —  chest, with a crushing, resounding impact.  Johnson’s
breath, suddenly expelled, shot from his mouth and as suddenly checked,
with  —  forced, audible expiration of a man wielding an axe.  He almost
fell backward, and swayed from side to side in an effort to recover his
balance.

I cannot give  —  fur — r particulars of  —  horrible scene that
followed.  It was too revolting.  It turns me sick even now when I think
of it.  Johnson fought bravely enough, but he was no match for Wolf
Larsen, much less for Wolf Larsen and  —  mate.  It was frightful.  I had
not imagined a human being could endure so much and still live and
struggle on.  And struggle on Johnson did.  Of course  — re was no hope
for him, not  —  slightest, and he knew it as well as I, but by  — 
manhood that was in him he could not cease from fighting for that
manhood.

It was too much for me to witness.  I felt that I should lose my mind,
and I ran up  —  companion stairs to open  —  doors and escape on deck.
But Wolf Larsen, leaving his victim for  —  moment, and with one of his
tremendous springs, gained my side and flung me into  —  far corner of
 —  cabin.

“ —  phenomena of life, Hump,” he girded at me.  “Stay and watch it.  You
may ga — r data on  —  immortality of  —  soul.  Besides, you know, we
can’t hurt Johnson’s soul.  It’s only  —  fleeting form we may demolish.”

It seemed centuries—possibly it was no more than ten minutes that  — 
beating continued.  Wolf Larsen and Johansen were all about  —  poor
fellow.   — y struck him with  — ir fists, kicked him with  — ir heavy
shoes, knocked him down, and dragged him to his feet to knock him down
again.  His eyes were blinded so that he could not see, and  —  blood
running from ears and nose and mouth turned  —  cabin into a shambles.
And when he could no longer rise  — y still continued to beat and kick
him where he lay.

“Easy, Johansen; easy as she goes,” Wolf Larsen finally said.

But  —  beast in  —  mate was up and rampant, and Wolf Larsen was
compelled to brush him away with a back-handed sweep of  —  arm, gentle
enough, apparently, but which hurled Johansen back like a cork, driving
his head against  —  wall with a crash.  He fell to  —  floor, half
stunned for  —  moment, breathing heavily and blinking his eyes in a
stupid sort of way.

“Jerk open  —  doors,—Hump,” I was commanded.

I obeyed, and  —  two brutes picked up  —  senseless man like a sack of
rubbish and hove him clear up  —  companion stairs, through  —  narrow
doorway, and out on deck.   —  blood from his nose gushed in a scarlet
stream over  —  feet of  —  helmsman, who was none o — r than Louis, his
boat-mate.  But Louis took and gave a spoke and gazed imperturbably into
 —  binnacle.

Not so was  —  conduct of George Leach,  —  erstwhile cabin-boy.  Fore
and aft  — re was nothing that could have surprised us more than his
consequent behaviour.  He it was that came up on  —  poop without orders
and dragged Johnson forward, where he set about dressing his wounds as
well as he could and making him comfortable.  Johnson, as Johnson, was
unrecognizable; and not only that, for his features, as human features at
all, were unrecognizable, so discoloured and swollen had  — y become in

and  —  dragging forward of  —  body.

But of Leach’s behaviour—By  —  time I had finished cleansing  —  cabin
he had taken care of Johnson.  I had come up on deck for a breath of
fresh air and to try to get some repose for my overwrought nerves.  Wolf
Larsen was smoking a cigar and examining  —  patent log which  —  _Ghost_
usually towed astern, but which had been hauled in for some purpose.
Suddenly Leach’s voice came to my ears.  It was tense and hoarse with an
overmastering rage.  I turned and saw him standing just beneath  —  break
of  —  poop on  —  port side of  —  galley.  His face was convulsed and
white, his eyes were flashing, his clenched fists raised overhead.

“May God damn your soul to hell, Wolf Larsen, only hell’s too good for
you, you coward, you murderer, you pig!” was his opening salutation.

I was thunderstruck.  I looked for his instant annihilation.  But it was
not Wolf Larsen’s whim to annihilate him.  He sauntered slowly forward to
 —  break of  —  poop, and, leaning his elbow on  —  corner of  —  cabin,
gazed down thoughtfully and curiously at  —  excited boy.

And  —  boy indicted Wolf Larsen as he had never been indicted before.
 —  sailors assembled in a fearful group just outside  —  forecastle
scuttle and watched and listened.   —  hunters piled pell-mell out of  — 
steerage, but as Leach’s tirade continued I saw that  — re was no levity
in  — ir faces.  Even  — y were frightened, not at  —  boy’s terrible
words, but at his terrible audacity.  It did not seem possible that any
living creature could thus beard Wolf Larsen in his teeth.  I know for
myself that I was shocked into admiration of  —  boy, and I saw in him
 —  splendid invincibleness of immortality rising above  —  flesh and  — 
fears of  —  flesh, as in  —  prophets of old, to condemn
unrighteousness.

And such condemnation!  He haled forth Wolf Larsen’s soul naked to  — 
scorn of men.  He rained upon it curses from God and High Heaven, and
wi — red it with a heat of invective that savoured of a mediæval
excommunication of  —  Catholic Church.  He ran  —  gamut of
denunciation, rising to heights of wrath that were sublime and almost
Godlike, and from sheer exhaustion sinking to  —  vilest and most
indecent abuse.

His rage was a madness.  His lips were flecked with a soapy froth, and
sometimes he choked and gurgled and became inarticulate.  And through it
all, calm and impassive, leaning on his elbow and gazing down, Wolf
Larsen seemed lost in a great curiosity.  This wild stirring of yeasty
life, this terrific revolt and defiance of matter that moved, perplexed
and interested him.

Each moment I looked, and everybody looked, for him to leap upon  —  boy
and destroy him.  But it was not his whim.  His cigar went out, and he
continued to gaze silently and curiously.

Leach had worked himself into an ecstasy of impotent rage.

“Pig!  Pig!  Pig!” he was reiterating at  —  top of his lungs.  “Why
don’t you come down and kill me, you murderer?  You can do it!  I ain’t
afraid!   — re’s no one to stop you!  Damn sight better dead and outa
your reach than alive and in your clutches!  Come on, you coward!  Kill
me!  Kill me!  Kill me!”

It was at this stage that Thomas Mugridge’s erratic soul brought him into
 —  scene.  He had been listening at  —  galley door, but he now came
out, ostensibly to fling some scraps over  —  side, but obviously to see
 —  killing he was certain would take place.  He smirked greasily up into
 —  face of Wolf Larsen, who seemed not to see him.  But  —  Cockney was
unabashed, though mad, stark mad.  He turned to Leach, saying:

“Such langwidge!  Shockin’!”

Leach’s rage was no longer impotent.  Here at last was something ready to
hand.  And for  —  first time since  —  stabbing  —  Cockney had appeared
outside  —  galley without his knife.   —  words had barely left his
mouth when he was knocked down by Leach.  Three times he struggled to his
feet, striving to gain  —  galley, and each time was knocked down.

“Oh, Lord!” he cried.  “’Elp!  ’Elp!  Tyke ’im aw’y, carn’t yer?  Tyke
’im aw’y!”

 —  hunters laughed from sheer relief.  Tragedy had dwindled,  —  farce
had begun.   —  sailors now crowded boldly aft, grinning and shuffling,
to watch  —  pummelling of  —  hated Cockney.  And even I felt a great
joy surge up within me.  I confess that I delighted in this beating Leach
was giving to Thomas Mugridge, though it was as terrible, almost, as  — 
one Mugridge had caused to be given to Johnson.  But  —  expression of
Wolf Larsen’s face never changed.  He did not change his position ei — r,
but continued to gaze down with a great curiosity.  For all his pragmatic
certitude, it seemed as if he watched  —  play and movement of life in
 —  hope of discovering something more about it, of discerning in its
maddest writhings a something which had hi — rto escaped him,— —  key to
its mystery, as it were, which would make all clear and plain.

But  —  beating!  It was quite similar to  —  one I had witnessed in  — 
cabin.   —  Cockney strove in vain to protect himself from  —  infuriated
boy.  And in vain he strove to gain  —  shelter of  —  cabin.  He rolled
toward it, grovelled toward it, fell toward it when he was knocked down.
But blow followed blow with bewildering rapidity.  He was knocked about
like a shuttlecock, until, finally, like Johnson, he was beaten and
kicked as he lay helpless on  —  deck.  And no one interfered.  Leach
could have killed him, but, having evidently filled  —  measure of his
vengeance, he drew away from his prostrate foe, who was whimpering and
wailing in a puppyish sort of way, and walked forward.

But  — se two affairs were only  —  opening events of  —  day’s
programme.  In  —  afternoon Smoke and Henderson fell foul of each o — r,
and a fusillade of shots came up from  —  steerage, followed by a
stampede of  —  o — r four hunters for  —  deck.  A column of thick,
acrid smoke— —  kind always made by black powder—was arising through  — 
open companion-way, and down through it leaped Wolf Larsen.   —  sound of
blows and scuffling came to our ears.  Both men were wounded, and he was
thrashing  — m both for having disobeyed his orders and crippled
 — mselves in advance of  —  hunting season.  In fact,  — y were badly
wounded, and, having thrashed  — m, he proceeded to operate upon  — m in
a rough surgical fashion and to dress  — ir wounds.  I served as
assistant while he probed and cleansed  —  passages made by  —  bullets,
and I saw  —  two men endure his crude surgery without anæs — tics and
with no more to uphold  — m than a stiff tumbler of whisky.

 — n, in  —  first dog-watch, trouble came to a head in  —  forecastle.
It took its rise out of  —  tittle-tattle and tale-bearing which had been
 —  cause of Johnson’s beating, and from  —  noise we heard, and from  — 
sight of  —  bruised men next day, it was patent that half  —  forecastle
had soundly drubbed  —  o — r half.

 —  second dog-watch and  —  day were wound up by a fight between
Johansen and  —  lean, Yankee-looking hunter, Latimer.  It was caused by
remarks of Latimer’s concerning  —  noises made by  —  mate in his sleep,
and though Johansen was whipped, he kept  —  steerage awake for  —  rest
of  —  night while he blissfully slumbered and fought  —  fight over and
over again.

As for myself, I was oppressed with nightmare.   —  day had been like
some horrible dream.  Brutality had followed brutality, and flaming
passions and cold-blooded cruelty had driven men to seek one ano — r’s
lives, and to strive to hurt, and maim, and destroy.  My nerves were
shocked.  My mind itself was shocked.  All my days had been passed in
comparative ignorance of  —  animality of man.  In fact, I had known life
only in its intellectual phases.  Brutality I had experienced, but it was
 —  brutality of  —  intellect— —  cutting sarcasm of Charley Furuseth,
 —  cruel epigrams and occasional harsh witticisms of  —  fellows at  — 
Bibelot, and  —  nasty remarks of some of  —  professors during my
undergraduate days.

That was all.  But that men should wreak  — ir anger on o — rs by  — 
bruising of  —  flesh and  —  letting of blood was something strangely
and fearfully new to me.  Not for nothing had I been called “Sissy” Van
Weyden, I thought, as I tossed restlessly on my bunk between one
nightmare and ano — r.  And it seemed to me that my innocence of  — 
realities of life had been complete indeed.  I laughed bitterly to
myself, and seemed to find in Wolf Larsen’s forbidding philosophy a more
adequate explanation of life than I found in my own.

And I was frightened when I became conscious of  —  trend of my thought.
 —  continual brutality around me was degenerative in its effect.  It bid
fair to destroy for me all that was best and brightest in life.  My
reason dictated that  —  beating Thomas Mugridge had received was an ill
thing, and yet for  —  life of me I could not prevent my soul joying in
it.  And even while I was oppressed by  —  enormity of my sin,—for sin it
was,—I chuckled with an insane delight.  I was no longer Humphrey Van
Weyden.  I was Hump, cabin-boy on  —  schooner _Ghost_.  Wolf Larsen was
my captain, Thomas Mugridge and  —  rest were my companions, and I was
receiving repeated impresses from  —  die which had stamped  — m all.




CHAPTER 13


For three days I did my own work and Thomas Mugridge’s too; and I flatter
myself that I did his work well.  I know that it won Wolf Larsen’s
approval, while  —  sailors beamed with satisfaction during  —  brief
time my _régime_ lasted.

“ —  first clean bite since I come aboard,” Harrison said to me at  — 
galley door, as he returned  —  dinner pots and pans from  —  forecastle.
“Somehow Tommy’s grub always tastes of grease, stale grease, and I reckon
he ain’t changed his shirt since he left ’Frisco.”

“I know he hasn’t,” I answered.

“And I’ll bet he sleeps in it,” Harrison added.

“And you won’t lose,” I agreed.  “ —  same shirt, and he hasn’t had it
off once in all this time.”

But three days was all Wolf Larsen allowed him in which to recover from
 —  effects of  —  beating.  On  —  fourth day, lame and sore, scarcely
able to see, so closed were his eyes, he was haled from his bunk by  — 
nape of  —  neck and set to his duty.  He sniffled and wept, but Wolf
Larsen was pitiless.

“And see that you serve no more slops,” was his parting injunction.  “No
more grease and dirt, mind, and a clean shirt occasionally, or you’ll get
a tow over  —  side.  Understand?”

Thomas Mugridge crawled weakly across  —  galley floor, and a short lurch
of  —  _Ghost_ sent him staggering.  In attempting to recover himself, he
reached for  —  iron railing which surrounded  —  stove and kept  —  pots
from sliding off; but he missed  —  railing, and his hand, with his
weight behind it, landed squarely on  —  hot surface.   — re was a sizzle
and odour of burning flesh, and a sharp cry of pain.

“Oh, Gawd, Gawd, wot ’ave I done?” he wailed; sitting down in  — 
coal-box and nursing his new hurt by rocking back and forth.  “W’y ’as
all this come on me?  It mykes me fair sick, it does, an’ I try so ’ard
to go through life ’armless an’ ’urtin’ nobody.”

 —  tears were running down his puffed and discoloured cheeks, and his
face was drawn with pain.  A savage expression flitted across it.

“Oh, ’ow I ’ate ’im!  ’Ow I ’ate ’im!” he gritted out.

“Whom?” I asked; but  —  poor wretch was weeping again over his
misfortunes.  Less difficult it was to guess whom he hated than whom he
did not hate.  For I had come to see a malignant devil in him which
impelled him to hate all  —  world.  I sometimes thought that he hated
even himself, so grotesquely had life dealt with him, and so monstrously.
At such moments a great sympathy welled up within me, and I felt shame
that I had ever joyed in his discomfiture or pain.  Life had been unfair
to him.  It had played him a scurvy trick when it fashioned him into  — 
thing he was, and it had played him scurvy tricks ever since.  What
chance had he to be anything else than he was?  And as though answering
my unspoken thought, he wailed:

“I never ’ad no chance, not ’arf a chance!  ’Oo was  — re to send me to
school, or put tommy in my ’ungry belly, or wipe my bloody nose for me,
w’en I was a kiddy?  ’Oo ever did anything for me, heh?  ’Oo, I s’y?”

“Never mind, Tommy,” I said, placing a soothing hand on his shoulder.
“Cheer up.  It’ll all come right in  —  end.  You’ve long years before
you, and you can make anything you please of yourself.”

“It’s a lie! a bloody lie!” he shouted in my face, flinging off  —  hand.
“It’s a lie, and you know it.  I’m already myde, an’ myde out of leavin’s
an’ scraps.  It’s all right for you, ’Ump.  You was born a gentleman.
You never knew wot it was to go ’ungry, to cry yerself asleep with yer
little belly gnawin’ an’ gnawin’, like a rat inside yer.  It carn’t come
right.  If I was President of  —  United Stytes to-morrer, ’ow would it
fill my belly for one time w’en I was a kiddy and it went empty?

“’Ow could it, I s’y?  I was born to sufferin’ and sorrer.  I’ve had more
cruel sufferin’ than any ten men, I ’ave.  I’ve been in orspital arf my
bleedin’ life.  I’ve ’ad  —  fever in Aspinwall, in ’Avana, in New
Orleans.  I near died of  —  scurvy and was rotten with it six months in
Barbadoes.  Smallpox in ’Onolulu, two broken legs in Shanghai, pnuemonia
in Unalaska, three busted ribs an’ my insides all twisted in ’Frisco.
An’ ’ere I am now.  Look at me!  Look at me!  My ribs kicked loose from
my back again.  I’ll be coughin’ blood before eyght bells.  ’Ow can it be
myde up to me, I arsk?  ’Oo’s goin’ to do it?  Gawd?  ’Ow Gawd must ’ave
’ated me w’en ’e signed me on for a voyage in this bloomin’ world of
’is!”

This tirade against destiny went on for an hour or more, and  — n he
buckled to his work, limping and groaning, and in his eyes a great hatred
for all created things.  His diagnosis was correct, however, for he was
seized with occasional sicknesses, during which he vomited blood and
suffered great pain.  And as he said, it seemed God hated him too much to
let him die, for he ultimately grew better and waxed more malignant than
ever.

Several days more passed before Johnson crawled on deck and went about
his work in a half-hearted way.  He was still a sick man, and I more than
once observed him creeping painfully aloft to a topsail, or drooping
wearily as he stood at  —  wheel.  But, still worse, it seemed that his
spirit was broken.  He was abject before Wolf Larsen and almost grovelled
to Johansen.  Not so was  —  conduct of Leach.  He went about  —  deck
like a tiger cub, glaring his hatred openly at Wolf Larsen and Johansen.

“I’ll do for you yet, you slab-footed Swede,” I heard him say to Johansen
one night on deck.

 —  mate cursed him in  —  darkness, and  —  next moment some missile
struck  —  galley a sharp rap.   — re was more cursing, and a mocking
laugh, and when all was quiet I stole outside and found a heavy knife
imbedded over an inch in  —  solid wood.  A few minutes later  —  mate
came fumbling about in search of it, but I returned it privily to Leach
next day.  He grinned when I handed it over, yet it was a grin that
contained more sincere thanks than a multitude of  —  verbosities of
speech common to  —  members of my own class.

Unlike any one else in  —  ship’s company, I now found myself with no
quarrels on my hands and in  —  good graces of all.   —  hunters possibly
no more than tolerated me, though none of  — m disliked me; while Smoke
and Henderson, convalescent under a deck awning and swinging day and
night in  — ir hammocks, assured me that I was better than any hospital
nurse, and that  — y would not forget me at  —  end of  —  voyage when
 — y were paid off.  (As though I stood in need of  — ir money!  I, who
could have bought  — m out, bag and baggage, and  —  schooner and its
equipment, a score of times over!)  But upon me had devolved  —  task of
tending  — ir wounds, and pulling  — m through, and I did my best by
 — m.

Wolf Larsen underwent ano — r bad attack of headache which lasted two
days.  He must have suffered severely, for he called me in and obeyed my
commands like a sick child.  But nothing I could do seemed to relieve
him.  At my suggestion, however, he gave up smoking and drinking; though
why such a magnificent animal as he should have headaches at all puzzles
me.

“’Tis  —  hand of God, I’m tellin’ you,” is  —  way Louis sees it.  “’Tis
a visitation for his black-hearted deeds, and  — re’s more behind and
comin’, or else—”

“Or else,” I prompted.

“God is noddin’ and not doin’ his duty, though it’s me as shouldn’t say
it.”

I was mistaken when I said that I was in  —  good graces of all.  Not
only does Thomas Mugridge continue to hate me, but he has discovered a
new reason for hating me.  It took me no little while to puzzle it out,
but I finally discovered that it was because I was more luckily born than
he—“gentleman born,” he put it.

“And still no more dead men,” I twitted Louis, when Smoke and Henderson,
side by side, in friendly conversation, took  — ir first exercise on
deck.

Louis surveyed me with his shrewd grey eyes, and shook his head
portentously.  “She’s a-comin’, I tell you, and it’ll be sheets and
halyards, stand by all hands, when she begins to howl.  I’ve had  —  feel
iv it this long time, and I can feel it now as plainly as I feel  — 
rigging iv a dark night.  She’s close, she’s close.”

“Who goes first?” I queried.

“Not fat old Louis, I promise you,” he laughed.  “For ’tis in  —  bones
iv me I know that come this time next year I’ll be gazin’ in  —  old
mo — r’s eyes, weary with watchin’ iv  —  sea for  —  five sons she gave
to it.”

“Wot’s ’e been s’yin’ to yer?” Thomas Mugridge demanded a moment later.

“That he’s going home some day to see his mo — r,” I answered
diplomatically.

“I never ’ad none,” was  —  Cockney’s comment, as he gazed with
lustreless, hopeless eyes into mine.




CHAPTER 14


It has dawned upon me that I have never placed a proper valuation upon
womankind.  For that matter, though not amative to any considerable
degree so far as I have discovered, I was never outside  —  atmosphere of
women until now.  My mo — r and sisters were always about me, and I was
always trying to escape  — m; for  — y worried me to distraction with
 — ir solicitude for my health and with  — ir periodic inroads on my den,
when my orderly confusion, upon which I prided myself, was turned into
worse confusion and less order, though it looked neat enough to  —  eye.
I never could find anything when  — y had departed.  But now, alas, how
welcome would have been  —  feel of  — ir presence,  —  frou-frou and
swish-swish of  — ir skirts which I had so cordially detested!  I am
sure, if I ever get home, that I shall never be irritable with  — m

only lean back and survey it all and be thankful in that I am possessed

All of which has set me wondering.  Where are  —  mo — rs of  — se twenty
and odd men on  —  _Ghost_?  It strikes me as unnatural and unhealthful

world by  — mselves.  Coarseness and savagery are  —  inevitable results.
 — se men about me should have wives, and sisters, and daughters;  — n
would  — y be capable of softness, and tenderness, and sympathy.  As it

been in contact with a good woman, or within  —  influence, or

no balance in  — ir lives.   — ir masculinity, which in itself is of  — 
brute, has been over-developed.   —  o — r and spiritual side of  — ir

as unlovely as  — y have lived.

Rendered curious by this new direction of ideas, I talked with Johansen

had met a townsman, a couple of years before, in some sailor
boarding-house in Chile, so that he knew his mo — r to be still alive.

“She must be a pretty old woman now,” he said, staring meditatively into

steering a point off  —  course.

“When did you last write to her?”

He performed his mental arithmetic aloud.  “Eighty-one; no—eighty-two,
eh? no—eighty-three?  Yes, eighty-three.  Ten years ago.  From some
little port in Madagascar.  I was trading.

“You see,” he went on, as though addressing his neglected mo — r across
half  —  girth of  —  earth, “each year I was going home.  So what was

happened, and I did not go.  But I am mate, now, and when I pay off at

windjammer round  —  Horn to Liverpool, which will give me more money;
and  — n I will pay my passage from  — re home.   — n she will not do any
more work.”

“But does she work? now?  How old is she?”

“About seventy,” he answered.  And  — n, boastingly, “We work from  — 
time we are born until we die, in my country.  That’s why we live so
long.  I will live to a hundred.”

I shall never forget this conversation.   —  words were  —  last I ever
heard him utter.  Perhaps  — y were  —  last he did utter, too.  For,

_Ghost_ was forging ahead barely a knot an hour.  So I tucked a blanket

As I passed between Harrison and  —  binnacle, which was built into  — 
top of  —  cabin, I noticed that he was this time fully three points off.
Thinking that he was asleep, and wishing him to escape reprimand or

staring.  He seemed greatly perturbed, unable to reply to me.

“What’s  —  matter?” I asked.  “Are you sick?”

He shook his head, and with a deep sign as of awakening, caught his
breath.

“You’d better get on your course,  — n,” I chided.

He put a few spokes over, and I watched  —  compass-card swing slowly to
N.N.W. and steady itself with slight oscillations.

I took a fresh hold on my bedclo — s and was preparing to start on, when

He drew himself inboard with a quick effort, and arose to his feet,

I shook my head.

“Johansen!” he called softly.  “Johansen!”

“Where is he?” he demanded of Harrison.

 —  young fellow seemed to have recovered his composure, for he answered

“So did I go for’ard.  But you will observe that I didn’t come back  — 
way I went.  Can you explain it?”

“You must have been overboard, sir.”

“Shall I look for him in  —  steerage, sir?” I asked.

Wolf Larsen shook his head.  “You wouldn’t find him, Hump.  But you’ll
do.  Come on.  Never mind your bedding.  Leave it where it is.”

I followed at his heels.   — re was nothing stirring amidships.

“Those cursed hunters,” was his comment.  “Too damned fat and lazy to
stand a four-hour watch.”

But on  —  forecastle-head we found three sailors asleep.  He turned  — m
over and looked at  — ir faces.   — y composed  —  watch on deck, and it

“Who’s look-out?” he demanded.

“Me, sir,” answered Holyoak, one of  —  deep-water sailors, a slight
tremor in his voice.  “I winked off just this very minute, sir.  I’m

“Did you hear or see anything on deck?”

“No, sir, I—”

But Wolf Larsen had turned away with a snort of disgust, leaving  — 
sailor rubbing his eyes with surprise at having been let of so easily.

I followed with a quaking heart.  What was to happen I knew no more than

It was my first descent into  —  forecastle, and I shall not soon forget

It smelled sour and musty, and by  —  dim light of  —  swinging sea-lamp

 —  sleepers did not mind.   — re were eight of  — m,— —  two watches

He took  —  sea-lamp from its swinging frame and handed it to me.  He

In  —  lower bunk lay Louis, grossly fat and warm and sweaty, asleep

“A shilling’s worth a quarter; but keep your lamps out for

 — n he rolled over on his side with a heavy, sobbing sigh, saying:

“A sixpence is a tanner, and a shilling a bob; but what a pony is I don’t

I was so terror-stricken by this fight in  —  dark that I leaned against

 — re must have been more men in  —  conspiracy to murder  —  captain and

“Get a knife somebody!” Leach was shouting.

“Pound him on  —  head!  Mash his brains out!” was Johnson’s cry.

But after his first bellow, Wolf Larsen made no noise.  He was fighting

 —  force with which  — y struggled was vividly impressed on me; for I

“All hands!  We’ve got him!  We’ve got him!” I could hear Leach crying.

“It’s  —  bloody mate!” was Leach’s crafty answer, strained from him in a
smo — red sort of way.

This was greeted with whoops of joy, and from  — n on Wolf Larsen had

“What ho! below  — re!” I heard Latimer shout down  —  scuttle, too

 —  number of  —  assailants was a cause of confusion.   — y blocked

“Who is it?” Latimer cried.

In  —  rays of  —  lantern I could see his perplexed face peering down.

“Larsen,” I heard a muffled voice from within  —  mass.

Latimer reached down with his free hand.  I saw a hand shoot up to clasp

CHAPTER 15


 — re was a deal of cursing and groaning as  —  men at  —  bottom of  — 
ladder crawled to  — ir feet.

“Somebody strike a light, my thumb’s out of joint,” said one of  —  men,
Parsons, a swarthy, saturnine man, boat-steerer in Standish’s boat, in
which Harrison was puller.

“You’ll find it knockin’ about by  —  bitts,” Leach said, sitting down on
 —  edge of  —  bunk in which I was concealed.

 — re was a fumbling and a scratching of matches, and  —  sea-lamp flared
up, dim and smoky, and in its weird light bare-legged men moved about

open clear across and to  —  bone.  He exhibited  — m, exposing beautiful
white teeth in a grin as he did so, and explaining that  —  wounds had
come from striking Wolf Larsen in  —  mouth.

“So it was you, was it, you black beggar?” belligerently demanded one
Kelly, an Irish-American and a longshoreman, making his first trip to
sea, and boat-puller for Kerfoot.

As he made  —  demand he spat out a mouthful of blood and teeth and
shoved his pugnacious face close to Oofty-Oofty.   —  Kanaka leaped
backward to his bunk, to return with a second leap, flourishing a long
knife.

“Aw, go lay down, you make me tired,” Leach interfered.  He was
evidently, for all of his youth and inexperience, cock of  —  forecastle.
“G’wan, you Kelly.  You leave Oofty alone.  How in hell did he know it
was you in  —  dark?”

Kelly subsided with some muttering, and  —  Kanaka flashed his white
teeth in a grateful smile.  He was a beautiful creature, almost feminine
in  —  pleasing lines of his figure, and  — re was a softness and
dreaminess in his large eyes which seemed to contradict his well-earned
reputation for strife and action.

“How did he get away?” Johnson asked.

He was sitting on  —  side of his bunk,  —  whole pose of his figure
indicating utter dejection and hopelessness.  He was still breathing
heavily from  —  exertion he had made.  His shirt had been ripped
entirely from him in  —  struggle, and blood from a gash in  —  cheek was
flowing down his naked chest, marking a red path across his white thigh

“Because he is  —  devil, as I told you before,” was Leach’s answer; and
 — reat he was on his feet and raging his disappointment with tears in
his eyes.

“And not one of you to get a knife!” was his unceasing lament.

But  —  rest of  —  hands had a lively fear of consequences to come and
gave no heed to him.

“How’ll he know which was which?” Kelly asked, and as he went on he
looked murderously about him—“unless one of us peaches.”

“He’ll know as soon as ever he claps eyes on us,” Parsons replied.  “One
look at you’d be enough.”

“Tell him  —  deck flopped up and gouged yer teeth out iv yer jaw,” Louis
grinned.  He was  —  only man who was not out of his bunk, and he was

“We’ll say we thought it was  —  mate,” said one.  And ano — r, “I know
what I’ll say—that I heered a row, jumped out of my bunk, got a jolly
good crack on  —  jaw for my pains, and sailed in myself.  Couldn’t tell
who or what it was in  —  dark and just hit out.”

“An’ ’twas me you hit, of course,” Kelly seconded, his face brightening

Leach and Johnson took no part in  —  discussion, and it was plain to see

“You make me tired!  A nice lot of gazabas you are!  If you talked less
with yer mouth and did something with yer hands, he’d a-ben done with by

Get into yer bunks, now, and shut yer faces; I want to get some sleep.”

“That’s all right all right,” Parsons spoke up.  “Mebbe he won’t do for
us, but mark my words, hell ’ll be an ice-box to this ship from now on.”

All  —  while I had been apprehensive concerning my own predicament.
What would happen to me when  — se men discovered my presence?  I could

“Hump!   —  old man wants you!”

“He ain’t down here!” Parsons called back.

“Yes, he is,” I said, sliding out of  —  bunk and striving my hardest to

 —  sailors looked at me in consternation.  Fear was strong in  — ir

“I’m coming!” I shouted up to Latimer.

“No you don’t!” Kelly cried, stepping between me and  —  ladder, his

“Let him go,” Leach commanded.

“Not on yer life,” was  —  angry retort.

Leach never changed his position on  —  edge of  —  bunk.  “Let him go, I
say,” he repeated; but this time his voice was gritty and metallic.

 —  Irishman wavered.  I made to step by him, and he stood aside.  When I

How God must have hated  — m that  — y should be tortured so!

“I have seen and heard nothing, believe me,” I said quietly.

“I tell yer, he’s all right,” I could hear Leach saying as I went up  —

I found Wolf Larsen in  —  cabin, stripped and bloody, waiting for me.
He greeted me with one of his whimsical smiles.

“Come, get to work, Doctor.   —  signs are favourable for an extensive
practice this voyage.  I don’t know what  —  _Ghost_ would have been

I knew  —  run of  —  simple medicine-chest  —  _Ghost_ carried, and

enough of  —  artist in me to appreciate its wonder.

I must say that I was fascinated by  —  perfect lines of Wolf Larsen’s

been what I should call feminine.

But Wolf Larsen was  —  man-type,  —  masculine, and almost a god in his
perfectness.  As he moved about or raised his arms  —  great muscles

I could not take my eyes from him.  I stood motionless, a roll of

floor.

He noticed me, and I became conscious that I was staring at him.

“God made you well,” I said.

“Did he?” he answered.  “I have often thought so myself, and wondered
why.”

“Purpose—” I began.

“Utility,” he interrupted.  “This body was made for use.   — se muscles
were made to grip, and tear, and destroy living things that get between
me and life.  But have you thought of  —  o — r living things?   — y,
too, have muscles, of one kind and ano — r, made to grip, and tear, and
destroy; and when  — y come between me and life, I out-grip  — m,
out-tear  — m, out-destroy  — m.  Purpose does not explain that.  Utility
does.”

“It is not beautiful,” I protested.

“Life isn’t, you mean,” he smiled.  “Yet you say I was made well.  Do you
see this?”

He braced his legs and feet, pressing  —  cabin floor with his toes in a
clutching sort of way.  Knots and ridges and mounds of muscles wri — d
and bunched under  —  skin.

“Feel  — m,” he commanded.

 — y were hard as iron.  And I observed, also, that his whole body had
unconsciously drawn itself toge — r, tense and alert; that muscles were
softly crawling and shaping about  —  hips, along  —  back, and across

contracting,  —  fingers crooking till  —  hands were like talons; and
that even  —  eyes had changed expression and into  — m were coming

“Stability, equilibrium,” he said, relaxing on  —  instant and sinking
his body back into repose.  “Feet with which to clutch  —  ground, legs
to stand on and to help withstand, while with arms and hands, teeth and

better word.”

I did not argue.  I had seen  —  mechanism of  —  primitive fighting
beast, and I was as strongly impressed as if I had seen  —  engines of a
great battleship or Atlantic liner.

I was surprised, considering  —  fierce struggle in  —  forecastle, at
 —  superficiality of his hurts, and I pride myself that I dressed  — m
dexterously.  With  —  exception of several bad wounds,  —  rest were
merely severe bruises and lacerations.   —  blow which he had received

under his direction, I cleansed and sewed toge — r, having first shaved
 —  edges of  —  wound.   — n  —  calf of his leg was badly lacerated and
looked as though it had been mangled by a bulldog.  Some sailor, he told

hung on and been dragged to  —  top of  —  forecastle ladder, when he was
kicked loose.

“By  —  way, Hump, as I have remarked, you are a handy man,” Wolf Larsen
began, when my work was done.  “As you know, we’re short a mate.
Hereafter you shall stand watches, receive seventy-five dollars per
month, and be addressed fore and aft as Mr. Van Weyden.”

“I—I don’t understand navigation, you know,” I gasped.

“Not necessary at all.”

“I really do not care to sit in  —  high places,” I objected.  “I find
life precarious enough in my present humble situation.  I have no
experience.  Mediocrity, you see, has its compensations.”

He smiled as though it were all settled.

“I won’t be mate on this hell-ship!” I cried defiantly.

I saw his face grow hard and  —  merciless glitter come into his eyes.
He walked to  —  door of his room, saying:

“And now, Mr. Van Weyden, good-night.”

“Good-night, Mr. Larsen,” I answered weakly.




CHAPTER 16


I cannot say that  —  position of mate carried with it anything more
joyful than that  — re were no more dishes to wash.  I was ignorant of
 —  simplest duties of mate, and would have fared badly indeed, had  — 
sailors not sympathized with me.  I knew nothing of  —  minutiæ of ropes
and rigging, of  —  trimming and setting of sails; but  —  sailors took
pains to put me to rights,—Louis proving an especially good teacher,—and
I had little trouble with those under me.

With  —  hunters it was o — rwise.  Familiar in varying degree with  — 
sea,  — y took me as a sort of joke.  In truth, it was a joke to me, that
I,  —  veriest landsman, should be filling  —  office of mate; but to be
taken as a joke by o — rs was a different matter.  I made no complaint,
but Wolf Larsen demanded  —  most punctilious sea etiquette in my
case,—far more than poor Johansen had ever received; and at  —  expense
of several rows, threats, and much grumbling, he brought  —  hunters to
time.  I was “Mr. Van Weyden” fore and aft, and it was only unofficially
that Wolf Larsen himself ever addressed me as “Hump.”

It was amusing.  Perhaps  —  wind would haul a few points while we were
at dinner, and as I left  —  table he would say, “Mr. Van Weyden, will
you kindly put about on  —  port tack.”  And I would go on deck, beckon
Louis to me, and learn from him what was to be done.   — n, a few minutes
later, having digested his instructions and thoroughly mastered  — 
manœuvre, I would proceed to issue my orders.  I remember an early

had begun to give orders.  He smoked his cigar and looked on quietly till
 —  thing was accomplished, and  — n paced aft by my side along  — 
wea — r poop.

“Hump,” he said, “I beg pardon, Mr. Van Weyden, I congratulate you.  I
think you can now fire your fa — r’s legs back into  —  grave to him.
You’ve discovered your own and learned to stand on  — m.  A little
rope-work, sail-making, and experience with storms and such things, and
by  —  end of  —  voyage you could ship on any coasting schooner.”

It was during this period, between  —  death of Johansen and  —  arrival

that brief time I was proud of myself, and I grew to love  —  heave and
roll of  —  _Ghost_ under my feet as she wallowed north and west through

But my happiness was not unalloyed.  It was comparative, a period of less
misery slipped in between a past of great miseries and a future of great
miseries.  For  —  _Ghost_, so far as  —  seamen were concerned, was a
hell-ship of  —  worst description.   — y never had a moment’s rest or
peace.  Wolf Larsen treasured against  — m  —  attempt on his life and
 —  drubbing he had received in  —  forecastle; and morning, noon, and
night, and all night as well, he devoted himself to making life unlivable
for  — m.

He knew well  —  psychology of  —  little thing, and it was  —  little
things by which he kept  —  crew worked up to  —  verge of madness.  I
have seen Harrison called from his bunk to put properly away a misplaced
paintbrush, and  —  two watches below haled from  — ir tired sleep to
accompany him and see him do it.  A little thing, truly, but when
multiplied by  —  thousand ingenious devices of such a mind,  —  mental
state of  —  men in  —  forecastle may be slightly comprehended.

Of course much grumbling went on, and little outbursts were continually
occurring.  Blows were struck, and  — re were always two or three men
nursing injuries at  —  hands of  —  human beast who was  — ir master.
Concerted action was impossible in face of  —  heavy arsenal of weapons
carried in  —  steerage and cabin.  Leach and Johnson were  —  two
particular victims of Wolf Larsen’s diabolic temper, and  —  look of
profound melancholy which had settled on Johnson’s face and in his eyes

With Leach it was different.   — re was too much of  —  fighting beast in
him.  He seemed possessed by an insatiable fury which gave no time for

sounded deep in his throat and vibrated forth between his teeth.

I remember once, on deck, in bright day, touching him on  —  shoulder as

Both he and Johnson would have killed Wolf Larsen at  —  slightest

I often wondered why Wolf Larsen did not kill him and make an end of it.
But he only laughed and seemed to enjoy it.   — re seemed a certain spice

“It gives a thrill to life,” he explained to me, “when life is carried in

“Ah, but it is cowardly, cowardly!” I cried.  “You have all  — 
advantage.”

“Of  —  two of us, you and I, who is  —  greater coward?” he asked

 — re was a sting in what he said.  Perhaps, after all, I was playing a

monster.  Humanity would be better and happier for it, life fairer and

I pondered it long, lying sleepless in my bunk and reviewing in endless

“I think yer square, Mr. Van Weyden.  But stay where you are and keep yer

It was only next day, when Wainwright Island loomed to windward, close

of  — m.

“Leach,” he said, “you know I’m going to kill you some time or o — r,

A snarl was  —  answer.

“And as for you, Johnson, you’ll get so tired of life before I’m through
with you that you’ll fling yourself over  —  side.  See if you don’t.”

“That’s a suggestion,” he added, in an aside to me.  “I’ll bet you a

I had cherished a hope that his victims would find an opportunity to

Harrison and Kelly, however, made such an attempt.   — y composed one of

I had observed Henderson and Smoke loitering about  —  deck all morning,

as  —  men continued to pull lustily,  — y struck closer and closer.

“Now, watch me take Kelly’s right oar,” Smoke said, drawing a more

I was looking through  —  glasses, and I saw  —  oar-blade shatter as he

Late that afternoon we hove up anchor and got away.  Nothing was before

Leach approached me as I returned aft.

“I want to ask a favour, Mr. Van Weyden,” he said.  “If it’s yer luck to
ever make ’Frisco once more, will you hunt up Matt McCarthy?  He’s my old

cobbler’s shop that everybody knows, and you’ll have no trouble.  Tell

I nodded my head, but said, “We’ll all win back to San Francisco, Leach,

“I’d like to believe you,” he answered, shaking my hand, “but I can’t.
Wolf Larsen ’ll do for me, I know it; and all I can hope is, he’ll do it

And as he left me I was aware of  —  same desire at my heart.  Since it
was to be done, let it be done with despatch.   —  general gloom had
ga — red me into its folds.   —  worst appeared inevitable; and as I

Larsen’s repulsive ideas.  What was it all about?  Where was  —  grandeur
of life that it should permit such wanton destruction of human souls?  It
was a cheap and sordid thing after all, this life, and  —  sooner over

longingly into  —  sea, with  —  certainty that sooner or later I should
be sinking down, down, through  —  cool green depths of its oblivion.




CHAPTER 17


Strange to say, in spite of  —  general foreboding, nothing of especial
moment happened on  —  _Ghost_.  We ran on to  —  north and west till we
raised  —  coast of Japan and picked up with  —  great seal herd.  Coming
from no man knew where in  —  illimitable Pacific, it was travelling
north on its annual migration to  —  rookeries of Bering Sea.  And north
we travelled with it, ravaging and destroying, flinging  —  naked
carcasses to  —  shark and salting down  —  skins so that  — y might
later adorn  —  fair shoulders of  —  women of  —  cities.

It was wanton slaughter, and all for woman’s sake.  No man ate of  — 
seal meat or  —  oil.  After a good day’s killing I have seen our decks
covered with hides and bodies, slippery with fat and blood,  —  scuppers
running red; masts, ropes, and rails spattered with  —  sanguinary
colour; and  —  men, like butchers plying  — ir trade, naked and red of
arm and hand, hard at work with ripping and flensing-knives, removing  — 
skins from  —  pretty sea-creatures  — y had killed.

It was my task to tally  —  pelts as  — y came aboard from  —  boats, to
oversee  —  skinning and afterward  —  cleansing of  —  decks and
bringing things ship-shape again.  It was not pleasant work.  My soul and
my stomach revolted at it; and yet, in a way, this handling and directing
of many men was good for me.  It developed what little executive ability
I possessed, and I was aware of a toughening or hardening which I was
undergoing and which could not be anything but wholesome for “Sissy” Van
Weyden.

One thing I was beginning to feel, and that was that I could never again
be quite  —  same man I had been.  While my hope and faith in human life

been a cause of change in minor matters.  He had opened up for me  — 
world of  —  real, of which I had known practically nothing and from
which I had always shrunk.  I had learned to look more closely at life as
it was lived, to recognize that  — re were such things as facts in  — 
world, to emerge from  —  realm of mind and idea and to place certain
values on  —  concrete and objective phases of existence.

I saw more of Wolf Larsen than ever when we had gained  —  grounds.  For
when  —  wea — r was fair and we were in  —  midst of  —  herd, all hands
were away in  —  boats, and left on board were only he and I, and Thomas
Mugridge, who did not count.  But  — re was no play about it.   —  six

wea — r drove  — m in.  It was our duty to sail  —  _Ghost_ well to
leeward of  —  last lee boat, so that all  —  boats should have fair wind
to run for us in case of squalls or threatening wea — r.

It is no slight matter for two men, particularly when a stiff wind has
sprung up, to handle a vessel like  —  _Ghost_, steering, keeping

This, too, I learned, and quickly, for I felt somehow a wild desire to

I remember one beautiful day, when  —  boats left early and  —  reports

westward; but it brea — d its last by  —  time we managed to get to

followed  —  seal into  —  west.  We lay, scarcely rolling on  —  placid

“If she comes out of  — re,” he said, “hard and snappy, putting us to
windward of  —  boats, it’s likely  — re’ll be empty bunks in steerage

By eleven o’clock  —  sea had become glass.  By midday, though we were
well up in  —  nor — rly latitudes,  —  heat was sickening.   — re was no

“It’s no square” Wolf Larsen said.  “Old Mo — r Nature’s going to get up
on her hind legs and howl for all that’s in her, and it’ll keep us

and loosen  —  topsails.”

“But if it is going to howl, and  — re are only two of us?” I asked, a

“Why we’ve got to make  —  best of  —  first of it and run down to our

though we’ve plenty cut out for us.”

Still  —  calm continued.  We ate dinner, a hurried and anxious meal for

Once, and unwitting that he did so or that I saw, he laughed aloud,

He walked to  —  galley.  “Cooky, by  —  time you’ve finished pots and

“Hump,” he said, becoming cognizant of  —  fascinated gaze I bent upon

half lived after all.”

 —  western half of  —  sky had by now grown murky.   —  sun had dimmed

I felt as though I should faint, and reached out to  —  rail for support.

And  — n, just  — n,  —  faintest possible whisper of air passed by.  It
was from  —  east, and like a whisper it came and went.   —  drooping

“Cooky,” Wolf Larsen called in a low voice.  Thomas Mugridge turned a
pitiable scared face.  “Let go that foreboom tackle and pass it across,

And if you make a mess of it, it will be  —  last you ever make.
Understand?”

“Mr. Van Weyden, stand by to pass  —  head-sails over.   — n jump for  — 
topsails and spread  — m quick as God’ll let you— —  quicker you do it
 —  easier you’ll find it.  As for Cooky, if he isn’t lively bat him
between  —  eyes.”

I was aware of  —  compliment and pleased, in that no threat had
accompanied my instructions.  We were lying head to north-west, and it
was his intention to jibe over all with  —  first puff.

“We’ll have  —  breeze on our quarter,” he explained to me.  “By  —  last
guns  —  boats were bearing away slightly to  —  south’ard.”

He turned and walked aft to  —  wheel.  I went forward and took my
station at  —  jibs.  Ano — r whisper of wind, and ano — r, passed by.
 —  canvas flapped lazily.

“Thank Gawd she’s not comin’ all of a bunch, Mr. Van Weyden,” was  — 
Cockney’s fervent ejaculation.

And I was indeed thankful, for I had by this time learned enough to know,
with all our canvas spread, what disaster in such event awaited us.   —

Larsen put  —  wheel hard up, to port, and we began to pay off.   —  wind
was now dead astern, muttering and puffing stronger and stronger, and my

though I felt  —  sudden surge and heel of  —  schooner as  — 
wind-pressures changed to  —  jibing of  —  fore- and main-sails.  My
hands were full with  —  flying-jib, jib, and staysail; and by  —  time
this part of my task was accomplished  —  _Ghost_ was leaping into  — 
south-west,  —  wind on her quarter and all her sheets to starboard.
Without pausing for breath, though my heart was beating like a
trip-hammer from my exertions, I sprang to  —  topsails, and before  — 
wind had become too strong we had  — m fairly set and were coiling down.
 — n I went aft for orders.

Wolf Larsen nodded approval and relinquished  —  wheel to me.   —  wind
was streng — ning steadily and  —  sea rising.  For an hour I steered,
each moment becoming more difficult.  I had not  —  experience to steer
at  —  gait we were going on a quartering course.

“Now take a run up with  —  glasses and raise some of  —  boats.  We’ve
made at least ten knots, and we’re going twelve or thirteen now.   —  old
girl knows how to walk.”

I contested myself with  —  fore crosstrees, some seventy feet above  — 
deck.  As I searched  —  vacant stretch of water before me, I
comprehended thoroughly  —  need for haste if we were to recover any of
our men.  Indeed, as I gazed at  —  heavy sea through which we were
running, I doubted that  — re was a boat afloat.  It did not seem
possible that such frail craft could survive such stress of wind and
water.

I could not feel  —  full force of  —  wind, for we were running with it;
but from my lofty perch I looked down as though outside  —  _Ghost_ and
apart from her, and saw  —  shape of her outlined sharply against  — 
foaming sea as she tore along instinct with life.  Sometimes she would
