view, and covering her deck to  —  hatches with  —  boiling ocean.  At
such moments, starting from a windward roll, I would go flying through
 —  air with dizzying swiftness, as though I clung to  —  end of a huge,
inverted pendulum,  —  arc of which, between  —  greater rolls, must have
been seventy feet or more.  Once,  —  terror of this giddy sweep
overpowered me, and for a while I clung on, hand and foot, weak and
trembling, unable to search  —  sea for  —  missing boats or to behold
aught of  —  sea but that which roared beneath and strove to overwhelm
 —  _Ghost_.

But  —  thought of  —  men in  —  midst of it steadied me, and in my
quest for  — m I forgot myself.  For an hour I saw nothing but  —  naked,
desolate sea.  And  — n, where a vagrant shaft of sunlight struck  — 
ocean and turned its surface to wrathful silver, I caught a small black
speck thrust skyward for an instant and swallowed up.  I waited
patiently.  Again  —  tiny point of black projected itself through  — 
wrathful blaze a couple of points off our port-bow.  I did not attempt to
shout, but communicated  —  news to Wolf Larsen by waving my arm.  He
changed  —  course, and I signalled affirmation when  —  speck showed
dead ahead.

It grew larger, and so swiftly that for  —  first time I fully
appreciated  —  speed of our flight.  Wolf Larsen motioned for me to come
down, and when I stood beside him at  —  wheel gave me instructions for
heaving to.

“Expect all hell to break loose,” he cautioned me, “but don’t mind it.
Yours is to do your own work and to have Cooky stand by  —  fore-sheet.”

I managed to make my way forward, but  — re was little choice of sides,
for  —  wea — r-rail seemed buried as often as  —  lee.  Having
instructed Thomas Mugridge as to what he was to do, I clambered into  — 
fore-rigging a few feet.   —  boat was now very close, and I could make
out plainly that it was lying head to wind and sea and dragging on its
mast and sail, which had been thrown overboard and made to serve as a
sea-anchor.   —  three men were bailing.  Each rolling mountain whelmed
 — m from view, and I would wait with sickening anxiety, fearing that
 — y would never appear again.   — n, and with black suddenness,  —  boat
would shoot clear through  —  foaming crest, bow pointed to  —  sky, and
 —  whole length of her bottom showing, wet and dark, till she seemed on
end.   — re would be a fleeting glimpse of  —  three men flinging water
in frantic haste, when she would topple over and fall into  —  yawning
valley, bow down and showing her full inside length to  —  stern upreared
almost directly above  —  bow.  Each time that she reappeared was a
miracle.

 —  _Ghost_ suddenly changed her course, keeping away, and it came to me
with a shock that Wolf Larsen was giving up  —  rescue as impossible.
 — n I realized that he was preparing to heave to, and dropped to  — 
deck to be in readiness.  We were now dead before  —  wind,  —  boat far
away and abreast of us.  I felt an abrupt easing of  —  schooner, a loss
for  —  moment of all strain and pressure, coupled with a swift
acceleration of speed.  She was rushing around on her heel into  —  wind.

As she arrived at right angles to  —  sea,  —  full force of  —  wind
(from which we had hi — rto run away) caught us.  I was unfortunately and
ignorantly facing it.  It stood up against me like a wall, filling my
lungs with air which I could not expel.  And as I choked and strangled,
and as  —  _Ghost_ wallowed for an instant, broadside on and rolling
straight over and far into  —  wind, I beheld a huge sea rise far above
my head.  I turned aside, caught my breath, and looked again.   —  wave
over-topped  —  _Ghost_, and I gazed sheer up and into it.  A shaft of
sunlight smote  —  over-curl, and I caught a glimpse of translucent,
rushing green, backed by a milky smo — r of foam.

 — n it descended, pandemonium broke loose, everything happened at once.
I was struck a crushing, stunning blow, nowhere in particular and yet
everywhere.  My hold had been broken loose, I was under water, and  — 
thought passed through my mind that this was  —  terrible thing of which
I had heard,  —  being swept in  —  trough of  —  sea.  My body struck
and pounded as it was dashed helplessly along and turned over and over,
and when I could hold my breath no longer, I brea — d  —  stinging salt
water into my lungs.  But through it all I clung to  —  one idea—_I must
get  —  jib backed over to windward_.  I had no fear of death.  I had no
doubt but that I should come through somehow.  And as this idea of
fulfilling Wolf Larsen’s order persisted in my dazed consciousness, I
seemed to see him standing at  —  wheel in  —  midst of  —  wild welter,
pitting his will against  —  will of  —  storm and defying it.

I brought up violently against what I took to be  —  rail, brea — d, and
brea — d  —  sweet air again.  I tried to rise, but struck my head and
was knocked back on hands and knees.  By some freak of  —  waters I had
been swept clear under  —  forecastle-head and into  —  eyes.  As I
scrambled out on all fours, I passed over  —  body of Thomas Mugridge,
who lay in a groaning heap.   — re was no time to investigate.  I must
get  —  jib backed over.

When I emerged on deck it seemed that  —  end of everything had come.  On
all sides  — re was a rending and crashing of wood and steel and canvas.
 —  _Ghost_ was being wrenched and torn to fragments.   —  foresail and
fore-topsail, emptied of  —  wind by  —  manœuvre, and with no one to
bring in  —  sheet in time, were thundering into ribbons,  —  heavy boom
threshing and splintering from rail to rail.   —  air was thick with
flying wreckage, detached ropes and stays were hissing and coiling like
snakes, and down through it all crashed  —  gaff of  —  foresail.

 —  spar could not have missed me by many inches, while it spurred me to
action.  Perhaps  —  situation was not hopeless.  I remembered Wolf
Larsen’s caution.  He had expected all hell to break loose, and here it
was.  And where was he?  I caught sight of him toiling at  —  main-sheet,
heaving it in and flat with his tremendous muscles,  —  stern of  — 
schooner lifted high in  —  air and his body outlined against a white
surge of sea sweeping past.  All this, and more,—a whole world of chaos
and wreck,—in possibly fifteen seconds I had seen and heard and grasped.

I did not stop to see what had become of  —  small boat, but sprang to
 —  jib-sheet.   —  jib itself was beginning to slap, partially filling
and emptying with sharp reports; but with a turn of  —  sheet and  — 
application of my whole strength each time it slapped, I slowly backed
it.  This I know: I did my best.  I pulled till I burst open  —  ends of
all my fingers; and while I pulled,  —  flying-jib and staysail split
 — ir cloths apart and thundered into nothingness.

Still I pulled, holding what I gained each time with a double turn until
 —  next slap gave me more.   — n  —  sheet gave with greater ease, and
Wolf Larsen was beside me, heaving in alone while I was busied taking up
 —  slack.

“Make fast!” he shouted.  “And come on!”

As I followed him, I noted that in spite of rack and ruin a rough order
obtained.   —  _Ghost_ was hove to.  She was still in working order, and
she was still working.  Though  —  rest of her sails were gone,  —  jib,
backed to windward, and  —  mainsail hauled down flat, were  — mselves
holding, and holding her bow to  —  furious sea as well.

I looked for  —  boat, and, while Wolf Larsen cleared  —  boat-tackles,
saw it lift to leeward on a big sea an not a score of feet away.  And, so
nicely had he made his calculation, we drifted fairly down upon it, so
that nothing remained to do but hook  —  tackles to ei — r end and hoist
it aboard.  But this was not done so easily as it is written.

In  —  bow was Kerfoot, Oofty-Oofty in  —  stern, and Kelly amidships.
As we drifted closer  —  boat would rise on a wave while we sank in  — 
trough, till almost straight above me I could see  —  heads of  —  three
men craned overside and looking down.   — n,  —  next moment, we would
lift and soar upward while  — y sank far down beneath us.  It seemed
incredible that  —  next surge should not crush  —  _Ghost_ down upon  — 
tiny eggshell.

But, at  —  right moment, I passed  —  tackle to  —  Kanaka, while Wolf
Larsen did  —  same thing forward to Kerfoot.  Both tackles were hooked
in a trice, and  —  three men, deftly timing  —  roll, made a
simultaneous leap aboard  —  schooner.  As  —  _Ghost_ rolled her side
out of water,  —  boat was lifted snugly against her, and before  — 
return roll came, we had heaved it in over  —  side and turned it bottom
up on  —  deck.  I noticed blood spouting from Kerfoot’s left hand.  In
some way  —  third finger had been crushed to a pulp.  But he gave no
sign of pain, and with his single right hand helped us lash  —  boat in
its place.

“Stand by to let that jib over, you Oofty!” Wolf Larsen commanded,  — 
very second we had finished with  —  boat.  “Kelly, come aft and slack
off  —  main-sheet!  You, Kerfoot, go for’ard and see what’s become of
Cooky!  Mr. Van Weyden, run aloft again, and cut away any stray stuff on
your way!”

And having commanded, he went aft with his peculiar tigerish leaps to  — 
wheel.  While I toiled up  —  fore-shrouds  —  _Ghost_ slowly paid off.
This time, as we went into  —  trough of  —  sea and were swept,  — re
were no sails to carry away.  And, halfway to  —  crosstrees and
flattened against  —  rigging by  —  full force of  —  wind so that it
would have been impossible for me to have fallen,  —  _Ghost_ almost on
her beam-ends and  —  masts parallel with  —  water, I looked, not down,
but at almost right angles from  —  perpendicular, to  —  deck of  — 
_Ghost_.  But I saw, not  —  deck, but where  —  deck should have been,
for it was buried beneath a wild tumbling of water.  Out of this water I
could see  —  two masts rising, and that was all.   —  _Ghost_, for  — 
moment, was buried beneath  —  sea.  As she squared off more and more,
escaping from  —  side pressure, she righted herself and broke her deck,
like a whale’s back, through  —  ocean surface.

 — n we raced, and wildly, across  —  wild sea,  —  while I hung like a
fly in  —  crosstrees and searched for  —  o — r boats.  In half-an-hour
I sighted  —  second one, swamped and bottom up, to which were
desperately clinging Jock Horner, fat Louis, and Johnson.  This time I
remained aloft, and Wolf Larsen succeeded in heaving to without being
swept.  As before, we drifted down upon it.  Tackles were made fast and
lines flung to  —  men, who scrambled aboard like monkeys.   —  boat
itself was crushed and splintered against  —  schooner’s side as it came
inboard; but  —  wreck was securely lashed, for it could be patched and
made whole again.

Once more  —  _Ghost_ bore away before  —  storm, this time so submerging
herself that for some seconds I thought she would never reappear.  Even
 —  wheel, quite a deal higher than  —  waist, was covered and swept
again and again.  At such moments I felt strangely alone with God, alone
with him and watching  —  chaos of his wrath.  And  — n  —  wheel would
reappear, and Wolf Larsen’s broad shoulders, his hands gripping  — 
spokes and holding  —  schooner to  —  course of his will, himself an
earth-god, dominating  —  storm, flinging its descending waters from him
and riding it to his own ends.  And oh,  —  marvel of it!  —  marvel of
it!  That tiny men should live and brea —  and work, and drive so frail a
contrivance of wood and cloth through so tremendous an elemental strife.

As before,  —  _Ghost_ swung out of  —  trough, lifting her deck again
out of  —  sea, and dashed before  —  howling blast.  It was now
half-past five, and half-an-hour later, when  —  last of  —  day lost
itself in a dim and furious twilight, I sighted a third boat.  It was
bottom up, and  — re was no sign of its crew.  Wolf Larsen repeated his
manœuvre, holding off and  — n rounding up to windward and drifting down
upon it.  But this time he missed by forty feet,  —  boat passing astern.

“Number four boat!” Oofty-Oofty cried, his keen eyes reading its number
in  —  one second when it lifted clear of  —  foam, and upside down.

It was Henderson’s boat and with him had been lost Holyoak and Williams,
ano — r of  —  deep-water crowd.  Lost  — y indubitably were; but  — 
boat remained, and Wolf Larsen made one more reckless effort to recover
it.  I had come down to  —  deck, and I saw Horner and Kerfoot vainly
protest against  —  attempt.

“By God, I’ll not be robbed of my boat by any storm that ever blew out of
hell!” he shouted, and though we four stood with our heads toge — r that
we might hear, his voice seemed faint and far, as though removed from us
an immense distance.

“Mr. Van Weyden!” he cried, and I heard through  —  tumult as one might
hear a whisper.  “Stand by that jib with Johnson and Oofty!   —  rest of
you tail aft to  —  mainsheet!  Lively now! or I’ll sail you all into
Kingdom Come!  Understand?”

And when he put  —  wheel hard over and  —  _Ghost’s_ bow swung off,
 — re was nothing for  —  hunters to do but obey and make  —  best of a
risky chance.  How great  —  risk I realized when I was once more buried
beneath  —  pounding seas and clinging for life to  —  pinrail at  — 
foot of  —  foremast.  My fingers were torn loose, and I swept across to
 —  side and over  —  side into  —  sea.  I could not swim, but before I
could sink I was swept back again.  A strong hand gripped me, and when
 —  _Ghost_ finally emerged, I found that I owed my life to Johnson.  I
saw him looking anxiously about him, and noted that Kelly, who had come
forward at  —  last moment, was missing.

This time, having missed  —  boat, and not being in  —  same position as
in  —  previous instances, Wolf Larsen was compelled to resort to a
different manœuvre.  Running off before  —  wind with everything to
starboard, he came about, and returned close-hauled on  —  port tack.

“Grand!” Johnson shouted in my ear, as we successfully came through  — 
attendant deluge, and I knew he referred, not to Wolf Larsen’s
seamanship, but to  —  performance of  —  _Ghost_ herself.

It was now so dark that  — re was no sign of  —  boat; but Wolf Larsen
held back through  —  frightful turmoil as if guided by unerring
instinct.  This time, though we were continually half-buried,  — re was
no trough in which to be swept, and we drifted squarely down upon  — 
upturned boat, badly smashing it as it was heaved inboard.

Two hours of terrible work followed, in which all hands of us—two
hunters, three sailors, Wolf Larsen and I—reefed, first one and  — n  — 
o — r,  —  jib and mainsail.  Hove to under this short canvas, our decks
were comparatively free of water, while  —  _Ghost_ bobbed and ducked
amongst  —  combers like a cork.

I had burst open  —  ends of my fingers at  —  very first, and during  — 
reefing I had worked with tears of pain running down my cheeks.  And when
all was done, I gave up like a woman and rolled upon  —  deck in  — 
agony of exhaustion.

In  —  meantime Thomas Mugridge, like a drowned rat, was being dragged
out from under  —  forecastle head where he had cravenly ensconced
himself.  I saw him pulled aft to  —  cabin, and noted with a shock of
surprise that  —  galley had disappeared.  A clean space of deck showed
where it had stood.

In  —  cabin I found all hands assembled, sailors as well, and while
coffee was being cooked over  —  small stove we drank whisky and crunched
hard-tack.  Never in my life had food been so welcome.  And never had hot
coffee tasted so good.  So violently did  —  _Ghost_, pitch and toss and
tumble that it was impossible for even  —  sailors to move about without
holding on, and several times, after a cry of “Now she takes it!” we were
heaped upon  —  wall of  —  port cabins as though it had been  —  deck.

“To hell with a look-out,” I heard Wolf Larsen say when we had eaten and
drunk our fill.  “ — re’s nothing can be done on deck.  If anything’s
going to run us down we couldn’t get out of its way.  Turn in, all hands,
and get some sleep.”

 —  sailors slipped forward, setting  —  side-lights as  — y went, while
 —  two hunters remained to sleep in  —  cabin, it not being deemed
advisable to open  —  slide to  —  steerage companion-way.  Wolf Larsen
and I, between us, cut off Kerfoot’s crushed finger and sewed up  — 
stump.  Mugridge, who, during all  —  time he had been compelled to cook
and serve coffee and keep  —  fire going, had complained of internal
pains, now swore that he had a broken rib or two.  On examination we
found that he had three.  But his case was deferred to next day,
principally for  —  reason that I did not know anything about broken ribs
and would first have to read it up.

“I don’t think it was worth it,” I said to Wolf Larsen, “a broken boat
for Kelly’s life.”

“But Kelly didn’t amount to much,” was  —  reply.  “Good-night.”

After all that had passed, suffering intolerable anguish in my
finger-ends, and with three boats missing, to say nothing of  —  wild
capers  —  _Ghost_ was cutting, I should have thought it impossible to
sleep.  But my eyes must have closed  —  instant my head touched  — 
pillow, and in utter exhaustion I slept throughout  —  night,  —  while
 —  _Ghost_, lonely and undirected, fought her way through  —  storm.




CHAPTER 18


 —  next day, while  —  storm was blowing itself out, Wolf Larsen and I
crammed anatomy and surgery and set Mugridge’s ribs.   — n, when  — 
storm broke, Wolf Larsen cruised back and forth over that portion of  — 
ocean where we had encountered it, and somewhat more to  —  westward,
while  —  boats were being repaired and new sails made and bent.  Sealing
schooner after sealing schooner we sighted and boarded, most of which
were in search of lost boats, and most of which were carrying boats and
crews  — y had picked up and which did not belong to  — m.  For  —  thick
of  —  fleet had been to  —  westward of us, and  —  boats, scattered far
and wide, had headed in mad flight for  —  nearest refuge.

Two of our boats, with men all safe, we took off  —  _Cisco_, and, to
Wolf Larsen’s huge delight and my own grief, he culled Smoke, with Nilson
and Leach, from  —  _San Diego_.  So that, at  —  end of five days, we
found ourselves short but four men—Henderson, Holyoak, Williams, and
Kelly,—and were once more hunting on  —  flanks of  —  herd.

As we followed it north we began to encounter  —  dreaded sea-fogs.  Day
after day  —  boats lowered and were swallowed up almost ere  — y touched
 —  water, while we on board pumped  —  horn at regular intervals and
every fifteen minutes fired  —  bomb gun.  Boats were continually being
lost and found, it being  —  custom for a boat to hunt, on lay, with
whatever schooner picked it up, until such time it was recovered by its
own schooner.  But Wolf Larsen, as was to be expected, being a boat
short, took possession of  —  first stray one and compelled its men to
hunt with  —  _Ghost_, not permitting  — m to return to  — ir own
schooner when we sighted it.  I remember how he forced  —  hunter and his
two men below, a riffle at  — ir breasts, when  — ir captain passed by at
biscuit-toss and hailed us for information.

Thomas Mugridge, so strangely and pertinaciously clinging to life, was
soon limping about again and performing his double duties of cook and
cabin-boy.  Johnson and Leach were bullied and beaten as much as ever,
and  — y looked for  — ir lives to end with  —  end of  —  hunting
season; while  —  rest of  —  crew lived  —  lives of dogs and were
worked like dogs by  — ir pitiless master.  As for Wolf Larsen and
myself, we got along fairly well; though I could not quite rid myself of
 —  idea that right conduct, for me, lay in killing him.  He fascinated
me immeasurably, and I feared him immeasurably.  And yet, I could not
imagine him lying prone in death.   — re was an endurance, as of
perpetual youth, about him, which rose up and forbade  —  picture.  I
could see him only as living always, and dominating always, fighting and
destroying, himself surviving.

One diversion of his, when we were in  —  midst of  —  herd and  —  sea
was too rough to lower  —  boats, was to lower with two boat-pullers and
a steerer and go out himself.  He was a good shot, too, and brought many
a skin aboard under what  —  hunters termed impossible hunting
conditions.  It seemed  —  breath of his nostrils, this carrying his life
in his hands and struggling for it against tremendous odds.

I was learning more and more seamanship; and one clear day—a thing we
rarely encountered now—I had  —  satisfaction of running and handling  — 
_Ghost_ and picking up  —  boats myself.  Wolf Larsen had been smitten
with one of his headaches, and I stood at  —  wheel from morning until
evening, sailing across  —  ocean after  —  last lee boat, and heaving to
and picking it and  —  o — r five up without command or suggestion from
him.

Gales we encountered now and again, for it was a raw and stormy region,
and, in  —  middle of June, a typhoon most memorable to me and most
important because of  —  changes wrought through it upon my future.  We
must have been caught nearly at  —  centre of this circular storm, and
Wolf Larsen ran out of it and to  —  southward, first under a
double-reefed jib, and finally under bare poles.  Never had I imagined so
great a sea.   —  seas previously encountered were as ripples compared
with  — se, which ran a half-mile from crest to crest and which upreared,
I am confident, above our mas — ad.  So great was it that Wolf Larsen
himself did not dare heave to, though he was being driven far to  — 
southward and out of  —  seal herd.

We must have been well in  —  path of  —  trans-Pacific steamships when
 —  typhoon moderated, and here, to  —  surprise of  —  hunters, we found
ourselves in  —  midst of seals—a second herd, or sort of rear-guard,
 — y declared, and a most unusual thing.  But it was “Boats over!”  — 
boom-boom of guns, and  —  pitiful slaughter through  —  long day.

It was at this time that I was approached by Leach.  I had just finished
tallying  —  skins of  —  last boat aboard, when he came to my side, in
 —  darkness, and said in a low tone:

“Can you tell me, Mr. Van Weyden, how far we are off  —  coast, and what
 —  bearings of Yokohama are?”

My heart leaped with gladness, for I knew what he had in mind, and I gave
him  —  bearings—west-north-west, and five hundred miles away.

“Thank you, sir,” was all he said as he slipped back into  —  darkness.

Next morning No. 3 boat and Johnson and Leach were missing.   — 
water-breakers and grub-boxes from all  —  o — r boats were likewise
missing, as were  —  beds and sea bags of  —  two men.  Wolf Larsen was
furious.  He set sail and bore away into  —  west-north-west, two hunters
constantly at  —  mas — ads and sweeping  —  sea with glasses, himself
pacing  —  deck like an angry lion.  He knew too well my sympathy for  — 
runaways to send me aloft as look-out.

 —  wind was fair but fitful, and it was like looking for a needle in a
haystack to raise that tiny boat out of  —  blue immensity.  But he put
 —  _Ghost_ through her best paces so as to get between  —  deserters and
 —  land.  This accomplished, he cruised back and forth across what he
knew must be  — ir course.

On  —  morning of  —  third day, shortly after eight bells, a cry that
 —  boat was sighted came down from Smoke at  —  mas — ad.  All hands
lined  —  rail.  A snappy breeze was blowing from  —  west with  — 
promise of more wind behind it; and  — re, to leeward, in  —  troubled
silver of  —  rising sun, appeared and disappeared a black speck.

We squared away and ran for it.  My heart was as lead.  I felt myself
turning sick in anticipation; and as I looked at  —  gleam of triumph in
Wolf Larsen’s eyes, his form swam before me, and I felt almost
irresistibly impelled to fling myself upon him.  So unnerved was I by  — 
thought of impending violence to Leach and Johnson that my reason must
have left me.  I know that I slipped down into  —  steerage in a daze,
and that I was just beginning  —  ascent to  —  deck, a loaded shot-gun
in my hands, when I heard  —  startled cry:

“ — re’s five men in that boat!”

I supported myself in  —  companion-way, weak and trembling, while  — 
observation was being verified by  —  remarks of  —  rest of  —  men.
 — n my knees gave from under me and I sank down, myself again, but
overcome by shock at knowledge of what I had so nearly done.  Also, I was
very thankful as I put  —  gun away and slipped back on deck.

No one had remarked my absence.   —  boat was near enough for us to make
out that it was larger than any sealing boat and built on different
lines.  As we drew closer,  —  sail was taken in and  —  mast unstepped.
Oars were shipped, and its occupants waited for us to heave to and take
 — m aboard.

Smoke, who had descended to  —  deck and was now standing by my side,
began to chuckle in a significant way.  I looked at him inquiringly.

“Talk of a mess!” he giggled.

“What’s wrong?” I demanded.

Again he chuckled.  “Don’t you see  — re, in  —  stern-sheets, on  — 
bottom?  May I never shoot a seal again if that ain’t a woman!”

I looked closely, but was not sure until exclamations broke out on all
sides.   —  boat contained four men, and its fifth occupant was certainly
a woman.  We were agog with excitement, all except Wolf Larsen, who was
too evidently disappointed in that it was not his own boat with  —  two
victims of his malice.

We ran down  —  flying jib, hauled  —  jib-sheets to wind-ward and  — 
main-sheet flat, and came up into  —  wind.   —  oars struck  —  water,
and with a few strokes  —  boat was alongside.  I now caught my first
fair glimpse of  —  woman.  She was wrapped in a long ulster, for  — 
morning was raw; and I could see nothing but her face and a mass of light
brown hair escaping from under  —  seaman’s cap on her head.   —  eyes
were large and brown and lustrous,  —  mouth sweet and sensitive, and  — 
face itself a delicate oval, though sun and exposure to briny wind had
burnt  —  face scarlet.

She seemed to me like a being from ano — r world.  I was aware of a
hungry out-reaching for her, as of a starving man for bread.  But  — n, I
had not seen a woman for a very long time.  I know that I was lost in a
great wonder, almost a stupor,—this,  — n, was a woman?—so that I forgot
myself and my mate’s duties, and took no part in helping  —  new-comers

downstretched arms, she looked up into our curious faces and smiled
amusedly and sweetly, as only a woman can smile, and as I had seen no one
smile for so long that I had forgotten such smiles existed.

“Mr. Van Weyden!”

Wolf Larsen’s voice brought me sharply back to myself.

“Will you take  —  lady below and see to her comfort?  Make up that spare
port cabin.  Put Cooky to work on it.  And see what you can do for that
face.  It’s burned badly.”

He turned brusquely away from us and began to question  —  new men.   — 
boat was cast adrift, though one of  — m called it a “bloody shame” with
Yokohama so near.

I found myself strangely afraid of this woman I was escorting aft.  Also
I was awkward.  It seemed to me that I was realizing for  —  first time
what a delicate, fragile creature a woman is; and as I caught her arm to
help her down  —  companion stairs, I was startled by its smallness and
softness.  Indeed, she was a slender, delicate woman as women go, but to
me she was so e — really slender and delicate that I was quite prepared
for her arm to crumble in my grasp.  All this, in frankness, to show my
first impression, after long denial of women in general and of Maud
Brewster in particular.

“No need to go to any great trouble for me,” she protested, when I had
seated her in Wolf Larsen’s arm-chair, which I had dragged hastily from
his cabin.  “ —  men were looking for land at any moment this morning,
and  —  vessel should be in by night; don’t you think so?”

Her simple faith in  —  immediate future took me aback.  How could I
explain to her  —  situation,  —  strange man who stalked  —  sea like
Destiny, all that it had taken me months to learn?  But I answered
honestly:

“If it were any o — r captain except ours, I should say you would be
ashore in Yokohama to-morrow.  But our captain is a strange man, and I
beg of you to be prepared for anything—understand?—for anything.”

“I—I confess I hardly do understand,” she hesitated, a perturbed but not
frightened expression in her eyes.  “Or is it a misconception of mine
that shipwrecked people are always shown every consideration?  This is
such a little thing, you know.  We are so close to land.”

“Candidly, I do not know,” I strove to reassure her.  “I wished merely to
prepare you for  —  worst, if  —  worst is to come.  This man, this
captain, is a brute, a demon, and one can never tell what will be his
next fantastic act.”

I was growing excited, but she interrupted me with an “Oh, I see,” and
her voice sounded weary.  To think was patently an effort.  She was
clearly on  —  verge of physical collapse.

She asked no fur — r questions, and I vouchsafed no remark, devoting
myself to Wolf Larsen’s command, which was to make her comfortable.  I
bustled about in quite housewifely fashion, procuring soothing lotions
for her sunburn, raiding Wolf Larsen’s private stores for a bottle of
port I knew to be  — re, and directing Thomas Mugridge in  —  preparation
of  —  spare state-room.

 —  wind was freshening rapidly,  —  _Ghost_ heeling over more and more,
and by  —  time  —  state-room was ready she was dashing through  — 
water at a lively clip.  I had quite forgotten  —  existence of Leach and
Johnson, when suddenly, like a thunderclap, “Boat ho!” came down  —  open
companion-way.  It was Smoke’s unmistakable voice, crying from  — 
mas — ad.  I shot a glance at  —  woman, but she was leaning back in  — 
arm-chair, her eyes closed, unutterably tired.  I doubted that she had
heard, and I resolved to prevent her seeing  —  brutality I knew would
follow  —  capture of  —  deserters.  She was tired.  Very good.  She
should sleep.

 — re were swift commands on deck, a stamping of feet and a slapping of
reef-points as  —  _Ghost_ shot into  —  wind and about on  —  o — r
tack.  As she filled away and heeled,  —  arm-chair began to slide across
 —  cabin floor, and I sprang for it just in time to prevent  —  rescued
woman from being spilled out.

Her eyes were too heavy to suggest more than a hint of  —  sleepy
surprise that perplexed her as she looked up at me, and she half
stumbled, half tottered, as I led her to her cabin.  Mugridge grinned
insinuatingly in my face as I shoved him out and ordered him back to his
galley work; and he won his revenge by spreading glowing reports among
 —  hunters as to what an excellent “lydy’s-myde” I was proving myself to
be.

She leaned heavily against me, and I do believe that she had fallen
asleep again between  —  arm-chair and  —  state-room.  This I discovered
when she nearly fell into  —  bunk during a sudden lurch of  —  schooner.
She aroused, smiled drowsily, and was off to sleep again; and asleep I
left her, under a heavy pair of sailor’s blankets, her head resting on a
pillow I had appropriated from Wolf Larsen’s bunk.



CHAPTER 19


I came on deck to find  —  _Ghost_ heading up close on  —  port tack and
cutting in to windward of a familiar spritsail close-hauled on  —  same
tack ahead of us.  All hands were on deck, for  — y knew that something
was to happen when Leach and Johnson were dragged aboard.

It was four bells.  Louis came aft to relieve  —  wheel.   — re was a
dampness in  —  air, and I noticed he had on his oilskins.

“What are we going to have?” I asked him.

“A healthy young slip of a gale from  —  breath iv it, sir,” he answered,
“with a splatter iv rain just to wet our gills an’ no more.”

“Too bad we sighted  — m,” I said, as  —  _Ghost’s_ bow was flung off a
point by a large sea and  —  boat leaped for a moment past  —  jibs and
into our line of vision.

Louis gave a spoke and temporized.  “ — y’d never iv made  —  land, sir,
I’m thinkin’.”

“Think not?” I queried.

“No, sir.  Did you feel that?”  (A puff had caught  —  schooner, and he
was forced to put  —  wheel up rapidly to keep her out of  —  wind.)
“’Tis no egg-shell’ll float on this sea an hour come, an’ it’s a stroke
iv luck for  — m we’re here to pick ’em up.”

Wolf Larsen strode aft from amidships, where he had been talking with  — 
rescued men.   —  cat-like springiness in his tread was a little more
pronounced than usual, and his eyes were bright and snappy.

“Three oilers and a fourth engineer,” was his greeting.  “But we’ll make
sailors out of  — m, or boat-pullers at any rate.  Now, what of  — 
lady?”

I know not why, but I was aware of a twinge or pang like  —  cut of a
knife when he mentioned her.  I thought it a certain silly fastidiousness
on my part, but it persisted in spite of me, and I merely shrugged my
shoulders in answer.

Wolf Larsen pursed his lips in a long, quizzical whistle.

“What’s her name,  — n?” he demanded.

“I don’t know,” I replied.  “She is asleep.  She was very tired.  In
fact, I am waiting to hear  —  news from you.  What vessel was it?”

“Mail steamer,” he answered shortly.  “_ —  City of Tokio_, from ’Frisco,
bound for Yokohama.  Disabled in that typhoon.  Old tub.  Opened up top
and bottom like a sieve.   — y were adrift four days.  And you don’t know
who or what she is, eh?—maid, wife, or widow?  Well, well.”

He shook his head in a bantering way, and regarded me with laughing eyes.

“Are you—” I began.  It was on  —  verge of my tongue to ask if he were
going to take  —  castaways into Yokohama.

“Am I what?” he asked.

“What do you intend doing with Leach and Johnson?”

He shook his head.  “Really, Hump, I don’t know.  You see, with  — se
additions I’ve about all  —  crew I want.”

“And  — y’ve about all  —  escaping  — y want,” I said.  “Why not give
 — m a change of treatment?  Take  — m aboard, and deal gently with  — m.
Whatever  — y have done  — y have been hounded into doing.”

“By me?”

“By you,” I answered steadily.  “And I give you warning, Wolf Larsen,
that I may forget love of my own life in  —  desire to kill you if you go
too far in maltreating those poor wretches.”

“Bravo!” he cried.  “You do me proud, Hump!  You’ve found your legs with
a vengeance.  You’re quite an individual.  You were unfortunate in having
your life cast in easy places, but you’re developing, and I like you  — 
better for it.”

His voice and expression changed.  His face was serious.  “Do you believe
in promises?” he asked.  “Are  — y sacred things?”

“Of course,” I answered.

“ — n here’s a compact,” he went on, consummate actor.  “If I promise not
to lay my hands upon Leach will you promise, in turn, not to attempt to

“Oh, not that I’m afraid of you, not that I’m afraid of you,” he hastened

I could hardly believe my ears.  What was coming over  —  man?

“Is it a go?” he asked impatiently.

“A go,” I answered.

His hand went out to mine, and as I shook it heartily I could have sworn
I saw  —  mocking devil shine up for a moment in his eyes.

We strolled across  —  poop to  —  lee side.   —  boat was close at hand
now, and in desperate plight.  Johnson was steering, Leach bailing.  We

to change position.   —  boat lost headway, and, as we lifted on a huge
surge, toppled and fell into  —  trough.

It was at this moment that Leach and Johnson looked up into  —  faces of
 — ir shipmates, who lined  —  rail amidships.   — re was no greeting.
 — y were as dead men in  — ir comrades’ eyes, and between  — m was  — 
gulf that parts  —  living and  —  dead.

 —  next instant  — y were opposite  —  poop, where stood Wolf Larsen and

Johnson looked at me, and I could see that his face was worn and haggard.
I waved my hand to him, and he answered  —  greeting, but with a wave

I did not see into  —  eyes of Leach, for he was looking at Wolf Larsen,
 —  old and implacable snarl of hatred strong as ever on his face.

 — n  — y were gone astern.   —  spritsail filled with  —  wind,
suddenly, careening  —  frail open craft till it seemed it would surely

Wolf Larsen barked a short laugh in my ear and strode away to  —  wea — r

command and he went about on  —  starboard tack.

Back we held, two miles and more to windward of  —  struggling

 —  sealing boats are not made for windward work.   — ir hope lies in
keeping a wea — r position so that  — y may run before  —  wind for  — 
schooner when it breezes up.  But in all that wild waste  — re was no

Johnson was a splendid seaman, and he knew as much about small boats as
he did about ships.  At  —  end of an hour and a half he was nearly

“So you’ve changed your mind?” I heard Wolf Larsen mutter, half to

“Hard up with that helm!” he commanded Oofty-Oofty,  —  Kanaka, who had
in  —  meantime relieved Louis at  —  wheel.

Command followed command.  As  —  schooner paid off,  —  fore- and

Johnson squared away promptly and ran after us.   — re was nothing else

“’Tis  —  fear iv death at  —  hearts iv  — m,” Louis muttered in my ear,

“Oh, he’ll heave to in a little while and pick  — m up,” I answered

Louis looked at me shrewdly.  “Think so?” he asked.

“Surely,” I answered.  “Don’t you?”

“I think nothing but iv my own skin,  — se days,” was his answer.  “An’
’tis with wonder I’m filled as to  —  workin’ out iv things.  A pretty

“What do you mean?” I demanded; for, having sped his shaft, he was

“What do I mean?” he cried.  “And it’s you that asks me!  ’Tis not what I
mean, but what  —  Wolf ’ll mean.   —  Wolf, I said,  —  Wolf!”

“If trouble comes, will you stand by?” I asked impulsively, for he had
voiced my own fear.

“Stand by?  ’Tis old fat Louis I stand by, an’ trouble enough it’ll be.
We’re at  —  beginnin’ iv things, I’m tellin’ ye,  —  bare beginnin’ iv

“I had not thought you so great a coward,” I sneered.

He favoured me with a contemptuous stare.  “If I raised never a hand for
that poor fool,”—pointing astern to  —  tiny sail,—“d’ye think I’m
hungerin’ for a broken head for a woman I never laid me eyes upon before

I turned scornfully away and went aft.

“Better get in those topsails, Mr. Van Weyden,” Wolf Larsen said, as I
came on  —  poop.

I felt relief, at least as far as  —  two men were concerned.  It was
clear he did not wish to run too far away from  — m.  I picked up hope at

opened my mouth to issue  —  necessary commands, when eager men were

eagerness on  — ir part was noted by Wolf Larsen with a grim smile.

Still we increased our lead, and when  —  boat had dropped astern several

fixedly, betrayed a trouble in his face he was not quite able to hide.

 —  boat drew closer and closer, hurling along through  —  seething green
like a thing alive, lifting and sending and uptossing across  —

again and shoot skyward.  It seemed impossible that it could continue to

almost upon us.

“Hard up,  — re!” Wolf Larsen shouted, himself springing to  —  wheel and
whirling it over.

Again  —  _Ghost_ sprang away and raced before  —  wind, and for two
hours Johnson and Leach pursued us.  We hove to and ran away, hove to and
ran away, and ever astern  —  struggling patch of sail tossed skyward and
fell into  —  rushing valleys.  It was a quarter of a mile away when a
thick squall of rain veiled it from view.  It never emerged.   —  wind

 —  men remained grouped amidships.  No one had gone below, and no one
was speaking.  Nor were any looks being exchanged.  Each man seemed

realize just what had taken place.  Wolf Larsen gave  — m little time for
thought.  He at once put  —  _Ghost_ upon her course—a course which meant
 —  seal herd and not Yokohama harbour.  But  —  men were no longer eager
as  — y pulled and hauled, and I heard curses amongst  — m, which left
 — ir lips smo — red and as heavy and lifeless as were  — y.  Not so was
it with  —  hunters.  Smoke  —  irrepressible related a story, and  — y
descended into  —  steerage, bellowing with laughter.

As I passed to leeward of  —  galley on my way aft I was approached by
 —  engineer we had rescued.  His face was white, his lips were
trembling.

“Good God! sir, what kind of a craft is this?” he cried.

“You have eyes, you have seen,” I answered, almost brutally, what of  — 
pain and fear at my own heart.

“Your promise?” I said to Wolf Larsen.

“I was not thinking of taking  — m aboard when I made that promise,” he
answered.  “And anyway, you’ll agree I’ve not laid my hands upon  — m.”

“Far from it, far from it,” he laughed a moment later.

I made no reply.  I was incapable of speaking, my mind was too confused.
I must have time to think, I knew.  This woman, sleeping even now in  — 
spare cabin, was a responsibility, which I must consider, and  —  only
rational thought that flickered through my mind was that I must do

CHAPTER 20


 —  remainder of  —  day passed uneventfully.   —  young slip of a gale,
having wetted our gills, proceeded to moderate.   —  fourth engineer and
 —  three oilers, after a warm interview with Wolf Larsen, were furnished

 — y were awed by what  — y had already seen of Wolf Larsen’s character,

Miss Brewster—we had learned her name from  —  engineer—slept on and on.
At supper I requested  —  hunters to lower  — ir voices, so she was not
disturbed; and it was not till next morning that she made her appearance.
It had been my intention to have her meals served apart, but Wolf Larsen

But her coming to  —  table had something amusing in it.   —  hunters

Wolf Larsen had little to say at first, doing no more than reply when he

“And when shall we arrive at Yokohama?” she asked, turning to him and

 — re it was,  —  question flat.   —  jaws stopped working,  —  ears
ceased wobbling, and though eyes remained glued on plates, each man
listened greedily for  —  answer.

“In four months, possibly three if  —  season closes early,” Wolf Larsen

She caught her breath and stammered, “I—I thought—I was given to

“That is a question you must settle with Mr. Van Weyden  — re,” he

He regarded her smilingly.  Her eyes fell before his gaze, but she lifted

“What do you think?” she demanded.

“That it is unfortunate, especially if you have any engagements falling

I saw her eyes flash with indignation, and this time it was I who dropped

“Mr. Van Weyden speaks with  —  voice of authority,” Wolf Larsen laughed.

I nodded my head, and she, having recovered herself, waited expectantly.

“Not that he is much to speak of now,” Wolf Larsen went on, “but he has

Isn’t that so, Kerfoot?”

Kerfoot, thus directly addressed, was startled into dropping his knife on

“Developed himself by peeling potatoes and washing dishes.  Eh, Kerfoot?”

Again that worthy grunted.

“Look at him now.  True, he is not what you would term muscular, but

“I may have learned to stand on my own legs,” I retorted.  “But I have
yet to stamp upon o — rs with  — m.”

He looked at me insolently.  “Your education is only half completed,

“We are very hospitable upon  —  _Ghost_.  Mr. Van Weyden has discovered

“Even to  —  peeling of potatoes and  —  washing of dishes,” I answered,

“I beg of you not to receive false impressions of us from Mr. Van

is sometimes—how shall I say?—er—quarrelsome, and harsh measures are
necessary.  He is quite reasonable and fair in his calm moments, and as
he is calm now he will not deny that only yesterday he threatened my
life.”

I was well-nigh choking, and my eyes were certainly fiery.  He drew
attention to me.

“Look at him now.  He can scarcely control himself in your presence.  He
is not accustomed to  —  presence of ladies anyway.  I shall have to arm
myself before I dare go on deck with him.”

He shook his head sadly, murmuring, “Too bad, too bad,” while  —  hunters
burst into guffaws of laughter.

 —  deep-sea voices of  — se men, rumbling and bellowing in  —  confined
space, produced a wild effect.   —  whole setting was wild, and for  — 
first time, regarding this strange woman and realizing how incongruous

 — se men and  — ir mental processes, was one of  — m myself, living  —

rough clo — s,  —  coarse faces,  —  wild laughter, and  —  lurching

As I buttered a piece of bread my eyes chanced to rest upon my hand.   —

“ — re will be no passing vessels, except o — r sealing-schooners,” Wolf

“ —  sooner you get accustomed to it,  —  better,” he said.

“I’ll furnish you with cloth, needles, and thread,” he added.  “I hope it

She made a wry pucker with her mouth, as though to advertise her

“I suppose you’re like Mr. Van Weyden  — re, accustomed to having things

She regarded him with amazement unconcealed.

“I mean no offence, believe me.  People eat,  — refore  — y must procure
 —  wherewithal.   — se men here shoot seals in order to live; for  — 
same reason I sail this schooner; and Mr. Van Weyden, for  —  present at

She shrugged her shoulders.

“Do you feed yourself?  Or does some one else feed you?”

“I’m afraid some one else has fed me most of my life,” she laughed,

“And I suppose some one else makes your bed for you?”

“I _have_ made beds,” she replied.

“Very often?”

She shook her head with mock ruefulness.

“Do you know what  — y do to poor men in  —  States, who, like you, do
not work for  — ir living?”

“I am very ignorant,” she pleaded.  “What do  — y do to  —  poor men who
are like me?”

“ — y send  — m to jail.   —  crime of not earning a living, in  — ir
case, is called vagrancy.  If I were Mr. Van Weyden, who harps eternally

you do nothing to deserve living?”

“But as you are not Mr. Van Weyden, I don’t have to answer, do I?”

She beamed upon him through her terror-filled eyes, and  —  pathos of it

“Yes, I have,” she answered slowly, and I could have laughed aloud at his
crestfallen visage.  “I remember my fa — r giving me a dollar once, when
I was a little girl, for remaining absolutely quiet for five minutes.”

He smiled indulgently.

“But that was long ago,” she continued.  “And you would scarcely demand a
little girl of nine to earn her own living.”

“At present, however,” she said, after ano — r slight pause, “I earn
about eighteen hundred dollars a year.”

With one accord, all eyes left  —  plates and settled on her.  A woman
who earned eighteen hundred dollars a year was worth looking at.  Wolf
Larsen was undisguised in his admiration.

“Salary, or piece-work?” he asked.

“Piece-work,” she answered promptly.

“Eighteen hundred,” he calculated.  “That’s a hundred and fifty dollars a

Consider yourself on salary during  —  time you remain with us.”

She made no acknowledgment.  She was too unused as yet to  —  whims of
 —  man to accept  — m with equanimity.

“I forgot to inquire,” he went on suavely, “as to  —  nature of your
occupation.  What commodities do you turn out?  What tools and materials

“Paper and ink,” she laughed.  “And, oh! also a typewriter.”

“You are Maud Brewster,” I said slowly and with certainty, almost as
though I were charging her with a crime.

Her eyes lifted curiously to mine.  “How do you know?”

“Aren’t you?” I demanded.

She acknowledged her identity with a nod.  It was Wolf Larsen’s turn to
be puzzled.   —  name and its magic signified nothing to him.  I was

“I remember writing a review of a thin little volume—” I had begun
carelessly, when she interrupted me.

“You!” she cried.  “You are—”

She was now staring at me in wide-eyed wonder.

I nodded my identity, in turn.

“Humphrey Van Weyden,” she concluded;  — n added with a sigh of relief,
and unaware that she had glanced that relief at Wolf Larsen, “I am so
glad.”

“I remember  —  review,” she went on hastily, becoming aware of  — 
awkwardness of her remark; “that too, too flattering review.”

“Not at all,” I denied valiantly.  “You impeach my sober judgment and
make my canons of little worth.  Besides, all my bro — r critics were
with me.  Didn’t Lang include your ‘Kiss Endured’ among  —  four supreme
sonnets by women in  —  English language?”

“But you called me  —  American Mrs. Meynell!”

“Was it not true?” I demanded.

“No, not that,” she answered.  “I was hurt.”

“We can measure  —  unknown only by  —  known,” I replied, in my finest
academic manner.  “As a critic I was compelled to place you.  You have
now become a yardstick yourself.  Seven of your thin little volumes are

“You are very kind, I am sure,” she murmured; and  —  very
conventionality of her tones and words, with  —  host of associations it

“And you are Maud Brewster,” I said solemnly, gazing across at her.

“And you are Humphrey Van Weyden,” she said, gazing back at me with equal
solemnity and awe.  “How unusual!  I don’t understand.  We surely are not
to expect some wildly romantic sea-story from your sober pen.”

“No, I am not ga — ring material, I assure you,” was my answer.  “I have
nei — r aptitude nor inclination for fiction.”

“Tell me, why have you always buried yourself in California?” she next
asked.  “It has not been kind of you.  We of  —  East have seen to very
little of you—too little, indeed, of  —  Dean of American Letters,  — 
Second.”

I bowed to, and disclaimed,  —  compliment.  “I nearly met you, once, in
Philadelphia, some Browning affair or o — r—you were to lecture, you
know.  My train was four hours late.”

And  — n we quite forgot where we were, leaving Wolf Larsen stranded and
silent in  —  midst of our flood of gossip.   —  hunters left  —  table
and went on deck, and still we talked.  Wolf Larsen alone remained.
Suddenly I became aware of him, leaning back from  —  table and listening
curiously to our alien speech of a world he did not know.

I broke short off in  —  middle of a sentence.   —  present, with all its
perils and anxieties, rushed upon me with stunning force.  It smote Miss
Brewster likewise, a vague and nameless terror rushing into her eyes as
she regarded Wolf Larsen.

He rose to his feet and laughed awkwardly.   —  sound of it was metallic.

“Oh, don’t mind me,” he said, with a self-depreciatory wave of his hand.
“I don’t count.  Go on, go on, I pray you.”

But  —  gates of speech were closed, and we, too, rose from  —  table and
laughed awkwardly.




CHAPTER 21


 —  chagrin Wolf Larsen felt from being ignored by Maud Brewster and me
in  —  conversation at table had to express itself in some fashion, and
it fell to Thomas Mugridge to be  —  victim.  He had not mended his ways
nor his shirt, though  —  latter he contended he had changed.   —

“I’ve given you warning, Cooky,” Wolf Larsen said, “and now you’ve got to
take your medicine.”

Mugridge’s face turned white under its sooty veneer, and when Wolf Larsen

and concoctions of  —  vilest order.  Conditions favoured  —

three miles an hour, and  —  sea was fairly calm.  But Mugridge had
little stomach for a dip in it.  Possibly he had seen men towed before.
Besides,  —  water was frightfully cold, and his was anything but a
rugged constitution.

As usual,  —  watches below and  —  hunters turned out for what promised
sport.  Mugridge seemed to be in rabid fear of  —  water, and he

Holding his weight by his arms, and in mid-air doubling his body at  —

squarely in  —  pit of  —  stomach, groaned involuntarily, and doubled up
and sank backward to  —  deck.

Hand-clapping and roars of laughter from  —  hunters greeted  —  exploit,

Parsons took  —  wheel, and  —  pursuit continued.  Round and round  —

Half-a-dozen sailors swarmed to  —  crosstrees after him, where  — y

It was a perilous undertaking, for, at a height of over a hundred feet

 —  aërial battle was over, and Thomas Mugridge, whining and gibbering,

It was a pitiful spectacle.  Though he could not drown, and was

He made no reply.  His eyes, fixed intently on  —  sea astern, suddenly

“Shark ho, sir!” he cried.

She gave no sign that she had heard, though  —  expression of her eyes

By now I had developed such skill at surgery that Wolf Larsen, with a few

CHAPTER 22


I knew what it was as she came toward me.  For ten minutes I had watched
her talking earnestly with  —  engineer, and now, with a sign for
silence, I drew her out of earshot of  —  helmsman.  Her face was white

for she had come to search Humphrey Van Weyden’s soul, and Humphrey Van
Weyden had nothing of which to be particularly proud since his advent on
 —  _Ghost_.

We walked to  —  break of  —  poop, where she turned and faced me.  I
glanced around to see that no one was within hearing distance.

“What is it?” I asked gently; but  —  expression of determination on her
face did not relax.

“I can readily understand,” she began, “that this morning’s affair was
largely an accident; but I have been talking with Mr. Haskins.  He tells

were guilty of  —  deed, or at least a party to it.

“ —  information is quite correct,” I answered.  “ —  two men were
murdered.”

“And you permitted it!” she cried.

“I was unable to prevent it, is a better way of phrasing it,” I replied,
still gently.

“But you tried to prevent it?”   — re was an emphasis on  —  “tried,” and
a pleading little note in her voice.

“Oh, but you didn’t,” she hurried on, divining my answer.  “But why
didn’t you?”

I shrugged my shoulders.  “You must remember, Miss Brewster, that you are
a new inhabitant of this little world, and that you do not yet understand

conceptions of humanity, manhood, conduct, and such things; but here you
will find  — m misconceptions.  I have found it so,” I added, with an
involuntary sigh.

She shook her head incredulously.

“What would you advise,  — n?” I asked.  “That I should take a knife, or
a gun, or an axe, and kill this man?”

She half started back.

“No, not that!”

“ — n what should I do?  Kill myself?”

“You speak in purely materialistic terms,” she objected.  “ — re is such
a thing as moral courage, and moral courage is never without effect.”

“Ah,” I smiled, “you advise me to kill nei — r him nor myself, but to let

little moral courage I may possess.

“You must understand, Miss Brewster, and understand clearly, that this
man is a monster.  He is without conscience.  Nothing is sacred to him,
nothing is too terrible for him to do.  It was due to his whim that I was
detained aboard in  —  first place.  It is due to his whim that I am
still alive.  I do nothing, can do nothing, because I am a slave to this
monster, as you are now a slave to him; because I desire to live, as you
will desire to live; because I cannot fight and overcome him, just as you
will not be able to fight and overcome him.”

She waited for me to go on.

“What remains?  Mine is  —  role of  —  weak.  I remain silent and suffer
ignominy, as you will remain silent and suffer ignominy.  And it is well.
It is  —  best we can do if we wish to live.   —  battle is not always to
 —  strong.  We have not  —  strength with which to fight this man; we
must dissimulate, and win, if win we can, by craft.  If you will be
advised by me, this is what you will do.  I know my position is perilous,
and I may say frankly that yours is even more perilous.  We must stand
toge — r, without appearing to do so, in secret alliance.  I shall not be
able to side with you openly, and, no matter what indignities may be put
upon me, you are to remain likewise silent.  We must provoke no scenes
with this man, nor cross his will.  And we must keep smiling faces and be
friendly with him no matter how repulsive it may be.”

She brushed her hand across her forehead in a puzzled way, saying, “Still
I do not understand.”

“You must do as I say,” I interrupted authoritatively, for I saw Wolf
Larsen’s gaze wandering toward us from where he paced up and down with
Latimer amidships.  “Do as I say, and ere long you will find I am right.”

“What shall I do,  — n?” she asked, detecting  —  anxious glance I had
shot at  —  object of our conversation, and impressed, I flatter myself,

“Dispense with all  —  moral courage you can,” I said briskly.  “Don’t
arouse this man’s animosity.  Be quite friendly with him, talk with him,

will make it easier for you to act your part.”

“I am to lie,” she said in steady, rebellious tones, “by speech and
action to lie.”

Wolf Larsen had separated from Latimer and was coming toward us.  I was
desperate.

“Please, please understand me,” I said hurriedly, lowering my voice.
“All your experience of men and things is worthless here.  You must begin
over again.  I know,—I can see it—you have, among o — r ways, been used

easily control a lion, while he would make a mock of you.  He would—I
have always been proud of  —  fact that I discovered him,” I said,

“And it was a newspaper poem,” she said glibly.

“It did happen to see  —  light in a newspaper,” I replied, “but not
because  —  magazine editors had been denied a glimpse at it.”

“We were talking of Harris,” I said to Wolf Larsen.

“Oh, yes,” he acknowledged.  “I remember  —  ‘Forge.’  Filled with pretty
sentiments and an almighty faith in human illusions.  By  —  way, Mr. Van
Weyden, you’d better look in on Cooky.  He’s complaining and restless.”

Thus was I bluntly dismissed from  —  poop, only to find Mugridge
sleeping soundly from  —  morphine I had given him.  I made no haste to

me.  She was following my advice.  And yet I was conscious of a slight
shock or hurt in that she was able to do  —  thing I had begged her to do
and which she had notably disliked.




CHAPTER 23


Brave winds, blowing fair, swiftly drove  —  _Ghost_ northward into  — 
seal herd.  We encountered it well up to  —  forty-fourth parallel, in a
raw and stormy sea across which  —  wind harried  —  fog-banks in eternal
flight.  For days at a time we could never see  —  sun nor take an
observation;  — n  —  wind would sweep  —  face of  —  ocean clean,  — 
waves would ripple and flash, and we would learn where we were.  A day of
clear wea — r might follow, or three days or four, and  — n  —  fog would
settle down upon us, seemingly thicker than ever.

 —  hunting was perilous; yet  —  boats, lowered day after day, were
swallowed up in  —  grey obscurity, and were seen no more till nightfall,
and often not till long after, when  — y would creep in like sea-wraiths,
one by one, out of  —  grey.  Wainwright— —  hunter whom Wolf Larsen had
stolen with boat and men—took advantage of  —  veiled sea and escaped.
He disappeared one morning in  —  encircling fog with his two men, and we
never saw  — m again, though it was not many days when we learned that
 — y had passed from schooner to schooner until  — y finally regained
 — ir own.

This was  —  thing I had set my mind upon doing, but  —  opportunity
never offered.  It was not in  —  mate’s province to go out in  —  boats,
and though I manœuvred cunningly for it, Wolf Larsen never granted me  — 
privilege.  Had he done so, I should have managed somehow to carry Miss
Brewster away with me.  As it was,  —  situation was approaching a stage
which I was afraid to consider.  I involuntarily shunned  —  thought of
it, and yet  —  thought continually arose in my mind like a haunting
spectre.

I had read sea-romances in my time, wherein figured, as a matter of
course,  —  lone woman in  —  midst of a shipload of men; but I learned,
now, that I had never comprehended  —  deeper significance of such a
situation— —  thing  —  writers harped upon and exploited so thoroughly.
And here it was, now, and I was face to face with it.  That it should be
as vital as possible, it required no more than that  —  woman should be
Maud Brewster, who now charmed me in person as she had long charmed me
through her work.

No one more out of environment could be imagined.  She was a delicate,
e — real creature, swaying and willowy, light and graceful of movement.
It never seemed to me that she walked, or, at least, walked after  — 
ordinary manner of mortals.  Hers was an extreme li — someness, and she
moved with a certain indefinable airiness, approaching one as down might
float or as a bird on noiseless wings.

She was like a bit of Dresden china, and I was continually impressed with
what I may call her fragility.  As at  —  time I caught her arm when
helping her below, so at any time I was quite prepared, should stress or
rough handling befall her, to see her crumble away.  I have never seen
body and spirit in such perfect accord.  Describe her verse, as  — 
critics have described it, as sublimated and spiritual, and you have
described her body.  It seemed to partake of her soul, to have analogous
attributes, and to link it to life with  —  slenderest of chains.
Indeed, she trod  —  earth lightly, and in her constitution  — re was
little of  —  robust clay.

She was in striking contrast to Wolf Larsen.  Each was nothing that  — 
o — r was, everything that  —  o — r was not.  I noted  — m walking  — 
deck toge — r one morning, and I likened  — m to  —  extreme ends of  — 
human ladder of evolution— —  one  —  culmination of all savagery,  — 
o — r  —  finished product of  —  finest civilization.  True, Wolf Larsen
possessed intellect to an unusual degree, but it was directed solely to
 —  exercise of his savage instincts and made him but  —  more formidable
a savage.  He was splendidly muscled, a heavy man, and though he strode
with  —  certitude and directness of  —  physical man,  — re was nothing
heavy about his stride.   —  jungle and  —  wilderness lurked in  — 
uplift and downput of his feet.  He was cat-footed, and li — , and
strong, always strong.  I likened him to some great tiger, a beast of
prowess and prey.  He looked it, and  —  piercing glitter that arose at
times in his eyes was  —  same piercing glitter I had observed in  — 
eyes of caged leopards and o — r preying creatures of  —  wild.

But this day, as I noted  — m pacing up and down, I saw that it was she
who terminated  —  walk.   — y came up to where I was standing by  — 
entrance to  —  companion-way.  Though she betrayed it by no outward

idle remark, looking at me, and laughed lightly enough; but I saw her
eyes return to his, involuntarily, as though fascinated;  — n  — y fell,
but not swiftly enough to veil  —  rush of terror that filled  — m.

It was in his eyes that I saw  —  cause of her perturbation.  Ordinarily
grey and cold and harsh,  — y were now warm and soft and golden, and all
a-dance with tiny lights that dimmed and faded, or welled up till  — 
full orbs were flooded with a glowing radiance.  Perhaps it was to this
that  —  golden colour was due; but golden his eyes were, enticing and
masterful, at  —  same time luring and compelling, and speaking a demand
and clamour of  —  blood which no woman, much less Maud Brewster, could
misunderstand.

Her own terror rushed upon me, and in that moment of fear— —  most
terrible fear a man can experience—I knew that in inexpressible ways she
was dear to me.   —  knowledge that I loved her rushed upon me with  — 
terror, and with both emotions gripping at my heart and causing my blood
at  —  same time to chill and to leap riotously, I felt myself drawn by a

 —  golden colour and  —  dancing lights were gone.  Cold and grey and
glittering  — y were as he bowed brusquely and turned away.

“I am afraid,” she whispered, with a shiver.  “I am so afraid.”

I, too, was afraid, and what of my discovery of how much she meant to me
my mind was in a turmoil; but, I succeeded in answering quite calmly:

“All will come right, Miss Brewster.  Trust me, it will come right.”

She answered with a grateful little smile that sent my heart pounding,
and started to descend  —  companion-stairs.

For a long while I remained standing where she had left me.   — re was
imperative need to adjust myself, to consider  —  significance of  — 
changed aspect of things.  It had come, at last, love had come, when I
least expected it and under  —  most forbidding conditions.  Of course,
my philosophy had always recognized  —  inevitableness of  —  love-call
sooner or later; but long years of bookish silence had made me
inattentive and unprepared.

And now it had come!  Maud Brewster!  My memory flashed back to that
first thin little volume on my desk, and I saw before me, as though in

had welcomed each of  — m!  Each year one had come from  —  press, and to
me each was  —  advent of  —  year.   — y had voiced a kindred intellect
and spirit, and as such I had received  — m into a camaraderie of  — 
mind; but now  — ir place was in my heart.

My heart?  A revulsion of feeling came over me.  I seemed to stand
outside myself and to look at myself incredulously.  Maud Brewster!
Humphrey Van Weyden, “ —  cold-blooded fish,”  —  “emotionless monster,”
 —  “analytical demon,” of Charley Furuseth’s christening, in love!  And
 — n, without rhyme or reason, all sceptical, my mind flew back to a

And  — n I said, “Twenty-seven years old and still free and fancy free?”
But how did I know she was fancy free?  And  —  pang of new-born jealousy
put all incredulity to flight.   — re was no doubt about it.  I was
jealous;  — refore I loved.  And  —  woman I loved was Maud Brewster.

I, Humphrey Van Weyden, was in love!  And again  —  doubt assailed me.
Not that I was afraid of it, however, or reluctant to meet it.  On  — 
contrary, idealist that I was to  —  most pronounced degree, my
philosophy had always recognized and guerdoned love as  —  greatest thing
in  —  world,  —  aim and  —  summit of being,  —  most exquisite pitch

come I could not believe.  I could not be so fortunate.  It was too good,
too good to be true.  Symons’s lines came into my head:

   “I wandered all  — se years among
   A world of women, seeking you.”

And  — n I had ceased seeking.  It was not for me, this greatest thing in
 —  world, I had decided.  Furuseth was right; I was abnormal, an
“emotionless monster,” a strange bookish creature, capable of pleasuring

women all my days, my appreciation of  — m had been æs — tic and nothing
more.  I had actually, at times, considered myself outside  —  pale, a

unheralded, it had come.  In what could have been no less than an

along  —  deck, murmuring to myself those beautiful lines of Mrs.
Browning:

   “I lived with visions for my company
   Instead of men and women years ago,
   And found  — m gentle mates, nor thought to know
   A sweeter music than  — y played to me.”

But  —  sweeter music was playing in my ears, and I was blind and
oblivious to all about me.   —  sharp voice of Wolf Larsen aroused me.

“What  —  hell are you up to?” he was demanding.

I had strayed forward where  —  sailors were painting, and I came to
myself to find my advancing foot on  —  verge of overturning a paint-pot.

“Sleep-walking, sunstroke,—what?” he barked.

“No; indigestion,” I retorted, and continued my walk as if nothing
untoward had occurred.




CHAPTER 24


Among  —  most vivid memories of my life are those of  —  events on  — 
_Ghost_ which occurred during  —  forty hours succeeding  —  discovery of
my love for Maud Brewster.  I, who had lived my life in quiet places,
only to enter at  —  age of thirty-five upon a course of  —  most
irrational adventure I could have imagined, never had more incident and
excitement crammed into any forty hours of my experience.  Nor can I
quite close my ears to a small voice of pride which tells me I did not do
so badly, all things considered.

To begin with, at  —  midday dinner, Wolf Larsen informed  —  hunters
that  — y were to eat  — nceforth in  —  steerage.  It was an
unprecedented thing on sealing-schooners, where it is  —  custom for  — 
hunters to rank, unofficially as officers.  He gave no reason, but his
motive was obvious enough.  Horner and Smoke had been displaying a
gallantry toward Maud Brewster, ludicrous in itself and inoffensive to
her, but to him evidently distasteful.

 —  announcement was received with black silence, though  —  o — r four
hunters glanced significantly at  —  two who had been  —  cause of  — ir

“Anything to say?”  —  o — r demanded aggressively.

It was a challenge, but Smoke refused to accept it.

“About what?” he asked, so innocently that Wolf Larsen was disconcerted,
while  —  o — rs smiled.

“Oh, nothing,” Wolf Larsen said lamely.  “I just thought you might want
to register a kick.”

“About what?” asked  —  imperturbable Smoke.

Smoke’s mates were now smiling broadly.  His captain could have killed
him, and I doubt not that blood would have flowed had not Maud Brewster
been present.  For that matter, it was her presence which enabled Smoke
to act as he did.  He was too discreet and cautious a man to incur Wolf
Larsen’s anger at a time when that anger could be expressed in terms
stronger than words.  I was in fear that a struggle might take place, but
a cry from  —  helmsman made it easy for  —  situation to save itself.

“Smoke ho!”  —  cry came down  —  open companion-way.

“How’s it bear?” Wolf Larsen called up.

“Dead astern, sir.”

“Maybe it’s a Russian,” suggested Latimer.

His words brought anxiety into  —  faces of  —  o — r hunters.  A Russian
could mean but one thing—a cruiser.   —  hunters, never more than roughly
aware of  —  position of  —  ship, never — less knew that we were close
to  —  boundaries of  —  forbidden sea, while Wolf Larsen’s record as a
poacher was notorious.  All eyes centred upon him.

“We’re dead safe,” he assured  — m with a laugh.  “No salt mines this
time, Smoke.  But I’ll tell you what—I’ll lay odds of five to one it’s
 —  _Macedonia_.”

No one accepted his offer, and he went on: “In which event, I’ll lay ten
to one  — re’s trouble breezing up.”

“No, thank you,” Latimer spoke up.  “I don’t object to losing my money,
but I like to get a run for it anyway.   — re never was a time when  — re
wasn’t trouble when you and that bro — r of yours got toge — r, and I’ll
lay twenty to one on that.”

A general smile followed, in which Wolf Larsen joined, and  —  dinner
went on smoothly, thanks to me, for he treated me abominably  —  rest of

We left  —  table to go on deck, for a steamer was a welcome break in  —

sailed since daylight across a sea barren of seals, and were now running
into  —  herd.

 —  smoke was still miles astern, but overhauling us rapidly, when we
lowered our boats.   — y spread out and struck a nor — rly course across
 —  ocean.  Now and again we saw a sail lower, heard  —  reports of  —

Under  —  approaching smoke  —  hull and upper-works of a steamer were

“Where is  —  trouble you were so sure was breezing up, Captain Larsen?”
she asked gaily.

He glanced at her, a moment’s amusement softening his features.

“What did you expect?  That  — y’d come aboard and cut our throats?”

“Something like that,” she confessed.  “You understand, seal-hunters are
so new and strange to me that I am quite ready to expect anything.”

He nodded his head.  “Quite right, quite right.  Your error is that you
failed to expect  —  worst.”

“Why, what can be worse than cutting our throats?” she asked, with pretty

“Cutting our purses,” he answered.  “Man is so made  — se days that his
capacity for living is determined by  —  money he possesses.”

“’Who steals my purse steals trash,’” she quoted.

“Who steals my purse steals my right to live,” was  —  reply, “old saws
to  —  contrary.  For he steals my bread and meat and bed, and in so

 — ir purses pretty speedily.”

“But I fail to see that this steamer has any designs on your purse.”

“Wait and you will see,” he answered grimly.

We did not have long to wait.  Having passed several miles beyond our

line of fourteen boats, like a huge broom, swept  —  herd before it.

Our boats hunted across  —  two or three miles of water between  — m and
 —  point where  —  _Macedonia’s_ had been dropped, and  — n headed for

at me as he rested from hauling taut  —  lashings of his boat.

“Listen to  — m, and find if it is hard to discover  —  most vital thing
in  — ir souls,” said Wolf Larsen.  “Faith? and love? and high ideals?
 —  good?  —  beautiful?  —  true?”

“ — ir innate sense of right has been violated,” Maud Brewster said,
joining  —  conversation.

She was standing a dozen feet away, one hand resting on  —  main-shrouds

She was positively bewitching, and, withal, sweetly spirituelle, if not

“A sentimentalist,” he sneered, “like Mr. Van Weyden.  Those men are
cursing because  — ir desires have been outraged.  That is all.  What

“’You hardly behave as if your purse had been touched,” she said,

“ — n it so happens that I am behaving differently, for my purse and my
soul have both been touched.  At  —  current price of skins in  —  London

have been had not  —  _Macedonia_ hogged it,  —  _Ghost_ has lost about
fifteen hundred dollars’ worth of skins.”

“You speak so calmly—” she began.

“But I do not feel calm; I could kill  —  man who robbed me,” he
interrupted.  “Yes, yes, I know, and that man my bro — r—more sentiment!
Bah!”

His face underwent a sudden change.  His voice was less harsh and wholly
sincere as he said:

“You must be happy, you sentimentalists, really and truly happy at
dreaming and finding things good, and, because you find some of  — m
good, feeling good yourself.  Now, tell me, you two, do you find me

“You are good to look upon—in a way,” I qualified.

“ — re are in you all powers for good,” was Maud Brewster’s answer.

“ — re you are!” he cried at her, half angrily.  “Your words are empty to
me.   — re is nothing clear and sharp and definite about  —  thought you

In point of fact, it is not a thought.  It is a feeling, a sentiment, a

As he went on his voice again grew soft, and a confiding note came into

 — y’re wrong, all wrong, of course, and contrary to reason; but in  —

lives  —  most, and your dreams and unrealities are less disturbing to
you and more gratifying than are my facts to me.”

He shook his head slowly, pondering.

“I often doubt, I often doubt,  —  worthwhileness of reason.  Dreams must
be more substantial and satisfying.  Emotional delight is more filling
and lasting than intellectual delight; and, besides, you pay for your
moments of intellectual delight by having  —  blues.  Emotional delight
is followed by no more than jaded senses which speedily recuperate.  I
envy you, I envy you.”

He stopped abruptly, and  — n on his lips formed one of his strange
quizzical smiles, as he added:

“It’s from my brain I envy you, take notice, and not from my heart.  My
reason dictates it.   —  envy is an intellectual product.  I am like a
sober man looking upon drunken men, and, greatly weary, wishing he, too,
were drunk.”

“Or like a wise man looking upon fools and wishing he, too, were a fool,”
I laughed.

“Quite so,” he said.  “You are a blessed, bankrupt pair of fools.  You
have no facts in your pocketbook.”

“Yet we spend as freely as you,” was Maud Brewster’s contribution.

“More freely, because it costs you nothing.”

“And because we draw upon eternity,” she retorted.

“Whe — r you do or think you do, it’s  —  same thing.  You spend what you
haven’t got, and in return you get greater value from spending what you
haven’t got than I get from spending what I have got, and what I have
sweated to get.”

“Why don’t you change  —  basis of your coinage,  — n?” she queried
teasingly.

He looked at her quickly, half-hopefully, and  — n said, all regretfully:
“Too late.  I’d like to, perhaps, but I can’t.  My pocketbook is stuffed
with  —  old coinage, and it’s a stubborn thing.  I can never bring
myself to recognize anything else as valid.”

He ceased speaking, and his gaze wandered absently past her and became
lost in  —  placid sea.   —  old primal melancholy was strong upon him.
He was quivering to it.  He had reasoned himself into a spell of  — 
blues, and within few hours one could look for  —  devil within him to be
up and stirring.  I remembered Charley Furuseth, and knew this man’s
sadness as  —  penalty which  —  materialist ever pays for his
materialism.




CHAPTER 25


“You’ve been on deck, Mr. Van Weyden,” Wolf Larsen said,  —  following
morning at  —  breakfast-table, “How do things look?”

“Clear enough,” I answered, glancing at  —  sunshine which streamed down
 —  open companion-way.  “Fair westerly breeze, with a promise of
stiffening, if Louis predicts correctly.”

He nodded his head in a pleased way.  “Any signs of fog?”

“Thick banks in  —  north and north-west.”

He nodded his head again, evincing even greater satisfaction than before.

“What of  —  _Macedonia_?”

“Not sighted,” I answered.

I could have sworn his face fell at  —  intelligence, but why he should
be disappointed I could not conceive.

I was soon to learn.  “Smoke ho!” came  —  hail from on deck, and his
face brightened.

“Good!” he exclaimed, and left  —  table at once to go on deck and into
 —  steerage, where  —  hunters were taking  —  first breakfast of  — ir

From  —  sounds on deck I knew that  —  sailors had been routed out and
