“What’s up?” I asked Wolf Larsen, unable longer to keep my curiosity in
check.

“Never mind what’s up,” he answered gruffly.  “You won’t be a thousand
years in finding out, and in  —  meantime just pray for plenty of wind.”

“Oh, well, I don’t mind telling you,” he said  —  next moment.  “I’m
going to give that bro — r of mine a taste of his own medicine.  In
short, I’m going to play  —  hog myself, and not for one day, but for  — 
rest of  —  season,—if we’re in luck.”

“And if we’re not?” I queried.

“Not to be considered,” he laughed.  “We simply must be in luck, or it’s
all up with us.”

He had  —  wheel at  —  time, and I went forward to my hospital in  — 
forecastle, where lay  —  two crippled men, Nilson and Thomas Mugridge.
Nilson was as cheerful as could be expected, for his broken leg was
knitting nicely; but  —  Cockney was desperately melancholy, and I was
aware of a great sympathy for  —  unfortunate creature.  And  —  marvel
of it was that still he lived and clung to life.   —  brutal years had
reduced his meagre body to splintered wreckage, and yet  —  spark of life
within burned brightly as ever.

“With an artificial foot—and  — y make excellent ones—you will be
stumping ships’ galleys to  —  end of time,” I assured him jovially.

But his answer was serious, nay, solemn.  “I don’t know about wot you
s’y, Mr. Van W’yden, but I do know I’ll never rest ’appy till I see that
’ell-’ound bloody well dead.  ’E cawn’t live as long as me.  ’E’s got no
right to live, an’ as  —  Good Word puts it, ‘’E shall shorely die,’ an’
I s’y, ‘Amen, an’ damn soon at that.’”

When I returned on deck I found Wolf Larsen steering mainly with one
hand, while with  —  o — r hand he held  —  marine glasses and studied
 —  situation of  —  boats, paying particular attention to  —  position
of  —  _Macedonia_.   —  only change noticeable in our boats was that
 — y had hauled close on  —  wind and were heading several points west of
north.  Still, I could not see  —  expediency of  —  manœuvre, for  — 
free sea was still intercepted by  —  _Macedonia’s_ five wea — r boats,
which, in turn, had hauled close on  —  wind.  Thus  — y slowly diverged
toward  —  west, drawing far — r away from  —  remainder of  —  boats in
 — ir line.  Our boats were rowing as well as sailing.  Even  —  hunters
were pulling, and with three pairs of oars in  —  water  — y rapidly
overhauled what I may appropriately term  —  enemy.

 —  smoke of  —  _Macedonia_ had dwindled to a dim blot on  — 
north-eastern horizon.  Of  —  steamer herself nothing was to be seen.
We had been loafing along, till now, our sails shaking half  —  time and
spilling  —  wind; and twice, for short periods, we had been hove to.
But  — re was no more loafing.  Sheets were trimmed, and Wolf Larsen
proceeded to put  —  _Ghost_ through her paces.  We ran past our line of
boats and bore down upon  —  first wea — r boat of  —  o — r line.

“Down that flying jib, Mr. Van Weyden,” Wolf Larsen commanded.  “And
stand by to back over  —  jibs.”

I ran forward and had  —  downhaul of  —  flying jib all in and fast as
we slipped by  —  boat a hundred feet to leeward.   —  three men in it
gazed at us suspiciously.   — y had been hogging  —  sea, and  — y knew
Wolf Larsen, by reputation at any rate.  I noted that  —  hunter, a huge
Scandinavian sitting in  —  bow, held his rifle, ready to hand, across
his knees.  It should have been in its proper place in  —  rack.  When
 — y came opposite our stern, Wolf Larsen greeted  — m with a wave of  — 
hand, and cried:

“Come on board and have a ’gam’!”

“To gam,” among  —  sealing-schooners, is a substitute for  —  verbs “to
visit,” “to gossip.”  It expresses  —  garrulity of  —  sea, and is a
pleasant break in  —  monotony of  —  life.

 —  _Ghost_ swung around into  —  wind, and I finished my work forward in
time to run aft and lend a hand with  —  mainsheet.

“You will please stay on deck, Miss Brewster,” Wolf Larsen said, as he
started forward to meet his guest.  “And you too, Mr. Van Weyden.”

 —  boat had lowered its sail and run alongside.   —  hunter, golden
bearded like a sea-king, came over  —  rail and dropped on deck.  But his
hugeness could not quite overcome his apprehensiveness.  Doubt and
distrust showed strongly in his face.  It was a transparent face, for all
of its hairy shield, and advertised instant relief when he glanced from
Wolf Larsen to me, noted that  — re was only  —  pair of us, and  — n
glanced over his own two men who had joined him.  Surely he had little
reason to be afraid.  He towered like a Goliath above Wolf Larsen.  He
must have measured six feet eight or nine inches in stature, and I
subsequently learned his weight—240 pounds.  And  — re was no fat about
him.  It was all bone and muscle.

A return of apprehension was apparent when, at  —  top of  — 
companion-way, Wolf Larsen invited him below.  But he reassured himself
with a glance down at his host—a big man himself but dwarfed by  — 
propinquity of  —  giant.  So all hesitancy vanished, and  —  pair
descended into  —  cabin.  In  —  meantime, his two men, as was  —  wont
of visiting sailors, had gone forward into  —  forecastle to do some
visiting  — mselves.

Suddenly, from  —  cabin came a great, choking bellow, followed by all
 —  sounds of a furious struggle.  It was  —  leopard and  —  lion, and
 —  lion made all  —  noise.  Wolf Larsen was  —  leopard.

“You see  —  sacredness of our hospitality,” I said bitterly to Maud
Brewster.

She nodded her head that she heard, and I noted in her face  —  signs of
 —  same sickness at sight or sound of violent struggle from which I had
suffered so severely during my first weeks on  —  _Ghost_.

“Wouldn’t it be better if you went forward, say by  —  steerage
companion-way, until it is over?” I suggested.

She shook her head and gazed at me pitifully.  She was not frightened,
but appalled, ra — r, at  —  human animality of it.

“You will understand,” I took advantage of  —  opportunity to say,
“whatever part I take in what is going on and what is to come, that I am
compelled to take it—if you and I are ever to get out of this scrape with
our lives.”

“It is not nice—for me,” I added.

“I understand,” she said, in a weak, far-away voice, and her eyes showed
me that she did understand.

 —  sounds from below soon died away.   — n Wolf Larsen came alone on
deck.   — re was a slight flush under his bronze, but o — rwise he bore
no signs of  —  battle.

“Send those two men aft, Mr. Van Weyden,” he said.

I obeyed, and a minute or two later  — y stood before him.  “Hoist in
your boat,” he said to  — m.  “Your hunter’s decided to stay aboard
awhile and doesn’t want it pounding alongside.”

“Hoist in your boat, I said,” he repeated, this time in sharper tones as
 — y hesitated to do his bidding.

“Who knows? you may have to sail with me for a time,” he said, quite
softly, with a silken threat that belied  —  softness, as  — y moved
slowly to comply, “and we might as well start with a friendly
understanding.  Lively now!  Death Larsen makes you jump better than
that, and you know it!”

 — ir movements perceptibly quickened under his coaching, and as  —  boat
swung inboard I was sent forward to let go  —  jibs.  Wolf Larsen, at  — 
wheel, directed  —  _Ghost_ after  —  _Macedonia’s_ second wea — r boat.

Under way, and with nothing for  —  time being to do, I turned my
attention to  —  situation of  —  boats.   —  _Macedonia’s_ third wea — r
boat was being attacked by two of ours,  —  fourth by our remaining
three; and  —  fifth, turn about, was taking a hand in  —  defence of its
nearest mate.   —  fight had opened at long distance, and  —  rifles were
cracking steadily.  A quick, snappy sea was being kicked up by  —  wind,
a condition which prevented fine shooting; and now and again, as we drew
closer, we could see  —  bullets zip-zipping from wave to wave.

 —  boat we were pursuing had squared away and was running before  — 
wind to escape us, and, in  —  course of its flight, to take part in
repulsing our general boat attack.

Attending to sheets and tacks now left me little time to see what was
taking place, but I happened to be on  —  poop when Wolf Larsen ordered
 —  two strange sailors forward and into  —  forecastle.   — y went
sullenly, but  — y went.  He next ordered Miss Brewster below, and smiled
at  —  instant horror that leapt into her eyes.

“You’ll find nothing gruesome down  — re,” he said, “only an unhurt man
securely made fast to  —  ring-bolts.  Bullets are liable to come aboard,
and I don’t want you killed, you know.”

Even as he spoke, a bullet was deflected by a brass-capped spoke of  — 
wheel between his hands and screeched off through  —  air to windward.

“You see,” he said to her; and  — n to me, “Mr. Van Weyden, will you take
 —  wheel?”

Maud Brewster had stepped inside  —  companion-way so that only her head
was exposed.  Wolf Larsen had procured a rifle and was throwing a
cartridge into  —  barrel.  I begged her with my eyes to go below, but
she smiled and said:

“We may be feeble land-creatures without legs, but we can show Captain
Larsen that we are at least as brave as he.”

He gave her a quick look of admiration.

“I like you a hundred per cent. better for that,” he said.  “Books, and
brains, and bravery.  You are well-rounded, a blue-stocking fit to be  — 
wife of a pirate chief.  Ahem, we’ll discuss that later,” he smiled, as a
bullet struck solidly into  —  cabin wall.

I saw his eyes flash golden as he spoke, and I saw  —  terror mount in
her own.

“We are braver,” I hastened to say.  “At least, speaking for myself, I
know I am braver than Captain Larsen.”

It was I who was now favoured by a quick look.  He was wondering if I
were making fun of him.  I put three or four spokes over to counteract a
sheer toward  —  wind on  —  part of  —  _Ghost_, and  — n steadied her.
Wolf Larsen was still waiting an explanation, and I pointed down to my
knees.

“You will observe  — re,” I said, “a slight trembling.  It is because I
am afraid,  —  flesh is afraid; and I am afraid in my mind because I do
not wish to die.  But my spirit masters  —  trembling flesh and  — 
qualms of  —  mind.  I am more than brave.  I am courageous.  Your flesh
is not afraid.  You are not afraid.  On  —  one hand, it costs you
nothing to encounter danger; on  —  o — r hand, it even gives you
delight.  You enjoy it.  You may be unafraid, Mr. Larsen, but you must
grant that  —  bravery is mine.”

“You’re right,” he acknowledged at once.  “I never thought of it in that
way before.  But is  —  opposite true?  If you are braver than I, am I
more cowardly than you?”

We both laughed at  —  absurdity, and he dropped down to  —  deck and
rested his rifle across  —  rail.   —  bullets we had received had
travelled nearly a mile, but by now we had cut that distance in half.  He
fired three careful shots.   —  first struck fifty feet to windward of
 —  boat,  —  second alongside; and at  —  third  —  boat-steerer let
loose his steering-oar and crumpled up in  —  bottom of  —  boat.

“I guess that’ll fix  — m,” Wolf Larsen said, rising to his feet.  “I
couldn’t afford to let  —  hunter have it, and  — re is a chance  — 
boat-puller doesn’t know how to steer.  In which case,  —  hunter cannot
steer and shoot at  —  same time.”

His reasoning was justified, for  —  boat rushed at once into  —  wind
and  —  hunter sprang aft to take  —  boat-steerer’s place.   — re was no
more shooting, though  —  rifles were still cracking merrily from  — 
o — r boats.

 —  hunter had managed to get  —  boat before  —  wind again, but we ran
down upon it, going at least two feet to its one.  A hundred yards away,
I saw  —  boat-puller pass a rifle to  —  hunter.  Wolf Larsen went
amidships and took  —  coil of  —  throat-halyards from its pin.   — n he
peered over  —  rail with levelled rifle.  Twice I saw  —  hunter let go
 —  steering-oar with one hand, reach for his rifle, and hesitate.  We
were now alongside and foaming past.

“Here, you!” Wolf Larsen cried suddenly to  —  boat-puller.  “Take a
turn!”

At  —  same time he flung  —  coil of rope.  It struck fairly, nearly
knocking  —  man over, but he did not obey.  Instead, he looked to his
hunter for orders.   —  hunter, in turn, was in a quandary.  His rifle
was between his knees, but if he let go  —  steering-oar in order to
shoot,  —  boat would sweep around and collide with  —  schooner.  Also
he saw Wolf Larsen’s rifle bearing upon him and knew he would be shot ere
he could get his rifle into play.

“Take a turn,” he said quietly to  —  man.

 —  boat-puller obeyed, taking a turn around  —  little forward thwart
and paying  —  line as it jerked taut.   —  boat sheered out with a rush,
and  —  hunter steadied it to a parallel course some twenty feet from  — 
side of  —  _Ghost_.

“Now, get that sail down and come alongside!” Wolf Larsen ordered.

He never let go his rifle, even passing down  —  tackles with one hand.
When  — y were fast, bow and stern, and  —  two uninjured men prepared to
come aboard,  —  hunter picked up his rifle as if to place it in a secure
position.

“Drop it!” Wolf Larsen cried, and  —  hunter dropped it as though it were
hot and had burned him.

Once aboard,  —  two prisoners hoisted in  —  boat and under Wolf
Larsen’s direction carried  —  wounded boat-steerer down into  — 
forecastle.

“If our five boats do as well as you and I have done, we’ll have a pretty
full crew,” Wolf Larsen said to me.

“ —  man you shot—he is—I hope?” Maud Brewster quavered.

“In  —  shoulder,” he answered.  “Nothing serious, Mr. Van Weyden will
pull him around as good as ever in three or four weeks.”

“But he won’t pull those chaps around, from  —  look of it,” he added,
pointing at  —  _Macedonia’s_ third boat, for which I had been steering
and which was now nearly abreast of us.  “That’s Horner’s and Smoke’s
work.  I told  — m we wanted live men, not carcasses.  But  —  joy of
shooting to hit is a most compelling thing, when once you’ve learned how
to shoot.  Ever experienced it, Mr. Van Weyden?”

I shook my head and regarded  — ir work.  It had indeed been bloody, for
 — y had drawn off and joined our o — r three boats in  —  attack on  — 
remaining two of  —  enemy.   —  deserted boat was in  —  trough of  — 
sea, rolling drunkenly across each comber, its loose spritsail out at
right angles to it and fluttering and flapping in  —  wind.   —  hunter
and boat-puller were both lying awkwardly in  —  bottom, but  — 
boat-steerer lay across  —  gunwale, half in and half out, his arms
trailing in  —  water and his head rolling from side to side.

“Don’t look, Miss Brewster, please don’t look,” I had begged of her, and
I was glad that she had minded me and been spared  —  sight.

“Head right into  —  bunch, Mr. Van Weyden,” was Wolf Larsen’s command.

As we drew nearer,  —  firing ceased, and we saw that  —  fight was over.
 —  remaining two boats had been captured by our five, and  —  seven were
grouped toge — r, waiting to be picked up.

“Look at that!” I cried involuntarily, pointing to  —  north-east.

 —  blot of smoke which indicated  —  _Macedonia’s_ position had
reappeared.

“Yes, I’ve been watching it,” was Wolf Larsen’s calm reply.  He measured
 —  distance away to  —  fog-bank, and for an instant paused to feel  — 
weight of  —  wind on his cheek.  “We’ll make it, I think; but you can
depend upon it that blessed bro — r of mine has twigged our little game
and is just a-humping for us.  Ah, look at that!”

 —  blot of smoke had suddenly grown larger, and it was very black.

“I’ll beat you out, though, bro — r mine,” he chuckled.  “I’ll beat you
out, and I hope you no worse than that you rack your old engines into
scrap.”

When we hove to, a hasty though orderly confusion reigned.   —  boats
came aboard from every side at once.  As fast as  —  prisoners came over
 —  rail  — y were marshalled forward to  —  forecastle by our hunters,
while our sailors hoisted in  —  boats, pell-mell, dropping  — m anywhere
upon  —  deck and not stopping to lash  — m.  We were already under way,
all sails set and drawing, and  —  sheets being slacked off for a wind
abeam, as  —  last boat lifted clear of  —  water and swung in  — 
tackles.

 — re was need for haste.   —  _Macedonia_, belching  —  blackest of
smoke from her funnel, was charging down upon us from out of  — 
north-east.  Neglecting  —  boats that remained to her, she had altered
her course so as to anticipate ours.  She was not running straight for
us, but ahead of us.  Our courses were converging like  —  sides of an
angle,  —  vertex of which was at  —  edge of  —  fog-bank.  It was
 — re, or not at all, that  —  _Macedonia_ could hope to catch us.   — 
hope for  —  _Ghost_ lay in that she should pass that point before  — 
_Macedonia_ arrived at it.

Wolf Larsen was steering, his eyes glistening and snapping as  — y dwelt
upon and leaped from detail to detail of  —  chase.  Now he studied  — 
sea to windward for signs of  —  wind slackening or freshening, now  — 
_Macedonia_; and again, his eyes roved over every sail, and he gave
commands to slack a sheet here a trifle, to come in on one  — re a
trifle, till he was drawing out of  —  _Ghost_  —  last bit of speed she
possessed.  All feuds and grudges were forgotten, and I was surprised at
 —  alacrity with which  —  men who had so long endured his brutality
sprang to execute his orders.  Strange to say,  —  unfortunate Johnson
came into my mind as we lifted and surged and heeled along, and I was
aware of a regret that he was not alive and present; he had so loved  — 
_Ghost_ and delighted in her sailing powers.

“Better get your rifles, you fellows,” Wolf Larsen called to our hunters;
and  —  five men lined  —  lee rail, guns in hand, and waited.

 —  _Macedonia_ was now but a mile away,  —  black smoke pouring from her
funnel at a right angle, so madly she raced, pounding through  —  sea at
a seventeen-knot gait—“’Sky-hooting through  —  brine,” as Wolf Larsen
quoted while gazing at her.  We were not making more than nine knots, but
 —  fog-bank was very near.

A puff of smoke broke from  —  _Macedonia’s_ deck, we heard a heavy
report, and a round hole took form in  —  stretched canvas of our
mainsail.   — y were shooting at us with one of  —  small cannon which
rumour had said  — y carried on board.  Our men, clustering amidships,
waved  — ir hats and raised a derisive cheer.  Again  — re was a puff of
smoke and a loud report, this time  —  cannon-ball striking not more than
twenty feet astern and glancing twice from sea to sea to windward ere it
sank.

But  — re was no rifle-firing for  —  reason that all  — ir hunters were
out in  —  boats or our prisoners.  When  —  two vessels were half-a-mile
apart, a third shot made ano — r hole in our mainsail.   — n we entered
 —  fog.  It was about us, veiling and hiding us in its dense wet gauze.

 —  sudden transition was startling.   —  moment before we had been
leaping through  —  sunshine,  —  clear sky above us,  —  sea breaking
and rolling wide to  —  horizon, and a ship, vomiting smoke and fire and
iron missiles, rushing madly upon us.  And at once, as in an instant’s
leap,  —  sun was blotted out,  — re was no sky, even our mas — ads were
lost to view, and our horizon was such as tear-blinded eyes may see.   — 
grey mist drove by us like a rain.  Every woollen filament of our
garments, every hair of our heads and faces, was jewelled with a crystal
globule.   —  shrouds were wet with moisture; it dripped from our rigging
overhead; and on  —  underside of our booms drops of water took shape in
long swaying lines, which were detached and flung to  —  deck in mimic
showers at each surge of  —  schooner.  I was aware of a pent, stifled
feeling.  As  —  sounds of  —  ship thrusting herself through  —  waves
were hurled back upon us by  —  fog, so were one’s thoughts.   —  mind
recoiled from contemplation of a world beyond this wet veil which wrapped
us around.  This was  —  world,  —  universe itself, its bounds so near
one felt impelled to reach out both arms and push  — m back.  It was
impossible, that  —  rest could be beyond  — se walls of grey.   —  rest
was a dream, no more than  —  memory of a dream.

It was weird, strangely weird.  I looked at Maud Brewster and knew that
she was similarly affected.   — n I looked at Wolf Larsen, but  — re was
nothing subjective about his state of consciousness.  His whole concern
was with  —  immediate, objective present.  He still held  —  wheel, and
I felt that he was timing Time, reckoning  —  passage of  —  minutes with
each forward lunge and leeward roll of  —  _Ghost_.

“Go for’ard and hard alee without any noise,” he said to me in a low
voice.  “Clew up  —  topsails first.  Set men at all  —  sheets.  Let
 — re be no rattling of blocks, no sound of voices.  No noise,
understand, no noise.”

When all was ready,  —  word “hard-a-lee” was passed forward to me from
man to man; and  —  _Ghost_ heeled about on  —  port tack with
practically no noise at all.  And what little  — re was,— —  slapping of
a few reef-points and  —  creaking of a sheave in a block or two,—was
ghostly under  —  hollow echoing pall in which we were swa — d.

We had scarcely filled away, it seemed, when  —  fog thinned abruptly and
we were again in  —  sunshine,  —  wide-stretching sea breaking before us
to  —  sky-line.  But  —  ocean was bare.  No wrathful _Macedonia_ broke
its surface nor blackened  —  sky with her smoke.

Wolf Larsen at once squared away and ran down along  —  rim of  — 
fog-bank.  His trick was obvious.  He had entered  —  fog to windward of
 —  steamer, and while  —  steamer had blindly driven on into  —  fog in
 —  chance of catching him, he had come about and out of his shelter and
was now running down to re-enter to leeward.  Successful in this,  —  old
simile of  —  needle in  —  haystack would be mild indeed compared with
his bro — r’s chance of finding him.  He did not run long.  Jibing  — 
fore- and main-sails and setting  —  topsails again, we headed back into
 —  bank.  As we entered I could have sworn I saw a vague bulk emerging
to windward.  I looked quickly at Wolf Larsen.  Already we were ourselves
buried in  —  fog, but he nodded his head.  He, too, had seen it— — 
_Macedonia_, guessing his manœuvre and failing by a moment in
anticipating it.   — re was no doubt that we had escaped unseen.

“He can’t keep this up,” Wolf Larsen said.  “He’ll have to go back for
 —  rest of his boats.  Send a man to  —  wheel, Mr. Van Weyden, keep
this course for  —  present, and you might as well set  —  watches, for
we won’t do any lingering to-night.”

“I’d give five hundred dollars, though,” he added, “just to be aboard  — 
_Macedonia_ for five minutes, listening to my bro — r curse.”

“And now, Mr. Van Weyden,” he said to me when he had been relieved from
 —  wheel, “we must make  — se new-comers welcome.  Serve out plenty of
whisky to  —  hunters and see that a few bottles slip for’ard.  I’ll
wager every man Jack of  — m is over  —  side to-morrow, hunting for Wolf
Larsen as contentedly as ever  — y hunted for Death Larsen.”

“But won’t  — y escape as Wainwright did?” I asked.

He laughed shrewdly.  “Not as long as our old hunters have anything to
say about it.  I’m dividing amongst  — m a dollar a skin for all  — 
skins shot by our new hunters.  At least half of  — ir enthusiasm to-day
was due to that.  Oh, no,  — re won’t be any escaping if  — y have
anything to say about it.  And now you’d better get for’ard to your
hospital duties.   — re must be a full ward waiting for you.”




CHAPTER 26


Wolf Larsen took  —  distribution of  —  whisky off my hands, and  — 
bottles began to make  — ir appearance while I worked over  —  fresh
batch of wounded men in  —  forecastle.  I had seen whisky drunk, such as
whisky-and-soda by  —  men of  —  clubs, but never as  — se men drank it,
from pannikins and mugs, and from  —  bottles—great brimming drinks, each
one of which was in itself a debauch.  But  — y did not stop at one or
two.   — y drank and drank, and ever  —  bottles slipped forward and  — y
drank more.

Everybody drank;  —  wounded drank; Oofty-Oofty, who helped me, drank.
Only Louis refrained, no more than cautiously wetting his lips with  — 
liquor, though he joined in  —  revels with an abandon equal to that of
most of  — m.  It was a saturnalia.  In loud voices  — y shouted over  — 
day’s fighting, wrangled about details, or waxed affectionate and made
friends with  —  men whom  — y had fought.  Prisoners and captors
hiccoughed on one ano — r’s shoulders, and swore mighty oaths of respect
and esteem.   — y wept over  —  miseries of  —  past and over  — 
miseries yet to come under  —  iron rule of Wolf Larsen.  And all cursed
him and told terrible tales of his brutality.

It was a strange and frightful spectacle— —  small, bunk-lined space,  — 
floor and walls leaping and lurching,  —  dim light,  —  swaying shadows
leng — ning and fore-shortening monstrously,  —  thick air heavy with
smoke and  —  smell of bodies and iodoform, and  —  inflamed faces of  — 
men—half-men, I should call  — m.  I noted Oofty-Oofty, holding  —  end
of a bandage and looking upon  —  scene, his velvety and luminous eyes
glistening in  —  light like a deer’s eyes, and yet I knew  —  barbaric
devil that lurked in his breast and belied all  —  softness and
tenderness, almost womanly, of his face and form.  And I noticed  — 
boyish face of Harrison,—a good face once, but now a demon’s,—convulsed
with passion as he told  —  new-comers of  —  hell-ship  — y were in and
shrieked curses upon  —  head of Wolf Larsen.

Wolf Larsen it was, always Wolf Larsen, enslaver and tormentor of men, a
male Circe and  — se his swine, suffering brutes that grovelled before
him and revolted only in drunkenness and in secrecy.  And was I, too, one
of his swine? I thought.  And Maud Brewster?  No!  I ground my teeth in
my anger and determination till  —  man I was attending winced under my
hand and Oofty-Oofty looked at me with curiosity.  I felt endowed with a
sudden strength.  What of my new-found love, I was a giant.  I feared
nothing.  I would work my will through it all, in spite of Wolf Larsen
and of my own thirty-five bookish years.  All would be well.  I would
make it well.  And so, exalted, upborne by a sense of power, I turned my
back on  —  howling inferno and climbed to  —  deck, where  —  fog
drifted ghostly through  —  night and  —  air was sweet and pure and
quiet.

 —  steerage, where were two wounded hunters, was a repetition of  — 
forecastle, except that Wolf Larsen was not being cursed; and it was with
a great relief that I again emerged on deck and went aft to  —  cabin.
Supper was ready, and Wolf Larsen and Maud were waiting for me.

While all his ship was getting drunk as fast as it could, he remained
sober.  Not a drop of liquor passed his lips.  He did not dare it under
 —  circumstances, for he had only Louis and me to depend upon, and Louis
was even now at  —  wheel.  We were sailing on through  —  fog without a
look-out and without lights.  That Wolf Larsen had turned  —  liquor
loose among his men surprised me, but he evidently knew  — ir psychology
and  —  best method of cementing in cordiality, what had begun in
bloodshed.

His victory over Death Larsen seemed to have had a remarkable effect upon
him.   —  previous evening he had reasoned himself into  —  blues, and I
had been waiting momentarily for one of his characteristic outbursts.
Yet nothing had occurred, and he was now in splendid trim.  Possibly his
success in capturing so many hunters and boats had counteracted  — 
customary reaction.  At any rate,  —  blues were gone, and  —  blue
devils had not put in an appearance.  So I thought at  —  time; but, ah
me, little I knew him or knew that even  — n, perhaps, he was meditating
an outbreak more terrible than any I had seen.

As I say, he discovered himself in splendid trim when I entered  — 
cabin.  He had had no headaches for weeks, his eyes were clear blue as
 —  sky, his bronze was beautiful with perfect health; life swelled
through his veins in full and magnificent flood.  While waiting for me he
had engaged Maud in animated discussion.  Temptation was  —  topic  — y
had hit upon, and from  —  few words I heard I made out that he was
contending that temptation was temptation only when a man was seduced by
it and fell.

“For look you,” he was saying, “as I see it, a man does things because of
desire.  He has many desires.  He may desire to escape pain, or to enjoy
pleasure.  But whatever he does, he does because he desires to do it.”

“But suppose he desires to do two opposite things, nei — r of which will
permit him to do  —  o — r?” Maud interrupted.

“ —  very thing I was coming to,” he said.

“And between  — se two desires is just where  —  soul of  —  man is
manifest,” she went on.  “If it is a good soul, it will desire and do  — 
good action, and  —  contrary if it is a bad soul.  It is  —  soul that
decides.”

“Bosh and nonsense!” he exclaimed impatiently.  “It is  —  desire that
decides.  Here is a man who wants to, say, get drunk.  Also, he doesn’t
want to get drunk.  What does he do?  How does he do it?  He is a puppet.
He is  —  creature of his desires, and of  —  two desires he obeys  — 
strongest one, that is all.  His soul hasn’t anything to do with it.  How
can he be tempted to get drunk and refuse to get drunk?  If  —  desire to
remain sober prevails, it is because it is  —  strongest desire.
Temptation plays no part, unless—” he paused while grasping  —  new
thought which had come into his mind—“unless he is tempted to remain
sober.

“Ha! ha!” he laughed.  “What do you think of that, Mr. Van Weyden?”

“That both of you are hair-splitting,” I said.  “ —  man’s soul is his
desires.  Or, if you will,  —  sum of his desires is his soul.   — rein
you are both wrong.  You lay  —  stress upon  —  desire apart from  — 
soul, Miss Brewster lays  —  stress on  —  soul apart from  —  desire,
and in point of fact soul and desire are  —  same thing.

“However,” I continued, “Miss Brewster is right in contending that
temptation is temptation whe — r  —  man yield or overcome.  Fire is
fanned by  —  wind until it leaps up fiercely.  So is desire like fire.
It is fanned, as by a wind, by sight of  —  thing desired, or by a new
and luring description or comprehension of  —  thing desired.   — re lies
 —  temptation.  It is  —  wind that fans  —  desire until it leaps up to
mastery.  That’s temptation.  It may not fan sufficiently to make  — 
desire overmastering, but in so far as it fans at all, that far is it
temptation.  And, as you say, it may tempt for good as well as for evil.”

I felt proud of myself as we sat down to  —  table.  My words had been
decisive.  At least  — y had put an end to  —  discussion.

But Wolf Larsen seemed voluble, prone to speech as I had never seen him
before.  It was as though he were bursting with pent energy which must
find an outlet somehow.  Almost immediately he launched into a discussion
on love.  As usual, his was  —  sheer materialistic side, and Maud’s was
 —  idealistic.  For myself, beyond a word or so of suggestion or
correction now and again, I took no part.

He was brilliant, but so was Maud, and for some time I lost  —  thread of
 —  conversation through studying her face as she talked.  It was a face
that rarely displayed colour, but to-night it was flushed and vivacious.
Her wit was playing keenly, and she was enjoying  —  tilt as much as Wolf
Larsen, and he was enjoying it hugely.  For some reason, though I know
not why in  —  argument, so utterly had I lost it in  —  contemplation of
one stray brown lock of Maud’s hair, he quoted from Iseult at Tintagel,
where she says:

   “Blessed am I beyond women even herein,
   That beyond all born women is my sin,
   And perfect my transgression.”

As he had read pessimism into Omar, so now he read triumph, stinging
triumph and exultation, into Swinburne’s lines.  And he read rightly, and
he read well.  He had hardly ceased reading when Louis put his head into
 —  companion-way and whispered down:

“Be easy, will ye?   —  fog’s lifted, an’ ’tis  —  port light iv a
steamer that’s crossin’ our bow this blessed minute.”

Wolf Larsen sprang on deck, and so swiftly that by  —  time we followed
him he had pulled  —  steerage-slide over  —  drunken clamour and was on
his way forward to close  —  forecastle-scuttle.   —  fog, though it
remained, had lifted high, where it obscured  —  stars and made  —  night
quite black.  Directly ahead of us I could see a bright red light and a
white light, and I could hear  —  pulsing of a steamer’s engines.  Beyond
a doubt it was  —  _Macedonia_.

Wolf Larsen had returned to  —  poop, and we stood in a silent group,
watching  —  lights rapidly cross our bow.

“Lucky for me he doesn’t carry a searchlight,” Wolf Larsen said.

“What if I should cry out loudly?” I queried in a whisper.

“It would be all up,” he answered.  “But have you thought upon what would
immediately happen?”

Before I had time to express any desire to know, he had me by  —  throat
with his gorilla grip, and by a faint quiver of  —  muscles—a hint, as it
were—he suggested to me  —  twist that would surely have broken my neck.
 —  next moment he had released me and we were gazing at  — 
_Macedonia’s_ lights.

“What if I should cry out?” Maud asked.

“I like you too well to hurt you,” he said softly—nay,  — re was a
tenderness and a caress in his voice that made me wince.

“But don’t do it, just  —  same, for I’d promptly break Mr. Van Weyden’s
neck.”

“ — n she has my permission to cry out,” I said defiantly.

“I hardly think you’ll care to sacrifice  —  Dean of American Letters  — 
Second,” he sneered.

We spoke no more, though we had become too used to one ano — r for  — 
silence to be awkward; and when  —  red light and  —  white had
disappeared we returned to  —  cabin to finish  —  interrupted supper.

Again  — y fell to quoting, and Maud gave Dowson’s “Impenitentia Ultima.”
She rendered it beautifully, but I watched not her, but Wolf Larsen.  I
was fascinated by  —  fascinated look he bent upon Maud.  He was quite
out of himself, and I noticed  —  unconscious movement of his lips as he
shaped word for word as fast as she uttered  — m.  He interrupted her
when she gave  —  lines:

   “And her eyes should be my light while  —  sun went out behind me,
   And  —  viols in her voice be  —  last sound in my ear.”

“ — re are viols in your voice,” he said bluntly, and his eyes flashed
 — ir golden light.

I could have shouted with joy at her control.  She finished  — 
concluding stanza without faltering and  — n slowly guided  — 
conversation into less perilous channels.  And all  —  while I sat in a
half-daze,  —  drunken riot of  —  steerage breaking through  — 
bulkhead,  —  man I feared and  —  woman I loved talking on and on.   — 
table was not cleared.   —  man who had taken Mugridge’s place had
evidently joined his comrades in  —  forecastle.

If ever Wolf Larsen attained  —  summit of living, he attained it  — n.
From time to time I forsook my own thoughts to follow him, and I followed
in amaze, mastered for  —  moment by his remarkable intellect, under  — 
spell of his passion, for he was preaching  —  passion of revolt.  It was
inevitable that Milton’s Lucifer should be instanced, and  —  keenness
with which Wolf Larsen analysed and depicted  —  character was a
revelation of his stifled genius.  It reminded me of Taine, yet I knew
 —  man had never heard of that brilliant though dangerous thinker.

“He led a lost cause, and he was not afraid of God’s thunderbolts,” Wolf
Larsen was saying.  “Hurled into hell, he was unbeaten.  A third of God’s
angels he had led with him, and straightway he incited man to rebel
against God, and gained for himself and hell  —  major portion of all  — 
generations of man.  Why was he beaten out of heaven?  Because he was
less brave than God? less proud? less aspiring?  No!  A thousand times
no!  God was more powerful, as he said, Whom thunder hath made greater.
But Lucifer was a free spirit.  To serve was to suffocate.  He preferred
suffering in freedom to all  —  happiness of a comfortable servility.  He
did not care to serve God.  He cared to serve nothing.  He was no
figure-head.  He stood on his own legs.  He was an individual.”

“ —  first Anarchist,” Maud laughed, rising and preparing to withdraw to
her state-room.

“ — n it is good to be an anarchist!” he cried.  He, too, had risen, and
he stood facing her, where she had paused at  —  door of her room, as he
went on:

               “‘Here at least
   We shall be free;  —  Almighty hath not built
   Here for his envy; will not drive us hence;
   Here we may reign secure; and in my choice
   To reign is worth ambition, though in hell:
   Better to reign in hell than serve in heaven.”

It was  —  defiant cry of a mighty spirit.   —  cabin still rang with his
voice, as he stood  — re, swaying, his bronzed face shining, his head up
and dominant, and his eyes, golden and masculine, intensely masculine and
insistently soft, flashing upon Maud at  —  door.

Again that unnamable and unmistakable terror was in her eyes, and she
said, almost in a whisper, “You are Lucifer.”

 —  door closed and she was gone.  He stood staring after her for a
minute,  — n returned to himself and to me.

“I’ll relieve Louis at  —  wheel,” he said shortly, “and call upon you to
relieve at midnight.  Better turn in now and get some sleep.”

He pulled on a pair of mittens, put on his cap, and ascended  — 
companion-stairs, while I followed his suggestion by going to bed.  For
some unknown reason, prompted mysteriously, I did not undress, but lay
down fully clo — d.  For a time I listened to  —  clamour in  —  steerage
and marvelled upon  —  love which had come to me; but my sleep on  — 
_Ghost_ had become most healthful and natural, and soon  —  songs and
cries died away, my eyes closed, and my consciousness sank down into  — 
half-death of slumber.

                                * * * * *

I knew not what had aroused me, but I found myself out of my bunk, on my
feet, wide awake, my soul vibrating to  —  warning of danger as it might
have thrilled to a trumpet call.  I threw open  —  door.   —  cabin light
was burning low.  I saw Maud, my Maud, straining and struggling and
crushed in  —  embrace of Wolf Larsen’s arms.  I could see  —  vain beat
and flutter of her as she strove, pressing her face against his breast,
to escape from him.  All this I saw on  —  very instant of seeing and as
I sprang forward.

I struck him with my fist, on  —  face, as he raised his head, but it was
a puny blow.  He roared in a ferocious, animal-like way, and gave me a
shove with his hand.  It was only a shove, a flirt of  —  wrist, yet so
tremendous was his strength that I was hurled backward as from a
catapult.  I struck  —  door of  —  state-room which had formerly been
Mugridge’s, splintering and smashing  —  panels with  —  impact of my
body.  I struggled to my feet, with difficulty dragging myself clear of
 —  wrecked door, unaware of any hurt whatever.  I was conscious only of
an overmastering rage.  I think I, too, cried aloud, as I drew  —  knife
at my hip and sprang forward a second time.

But something had happened.   — y were reeling apart.  I was close upon
him, my knife uplifted, but I withheld  —  blow.  I was puzzled by  — 
strangeness of it.  Maud was leaning against  —  wall, one hand out for
support; but he was staggering, his left hand pressed against his
forehead and covering his eyes, and with  —  right he was groping about
him in a dazed sort of way.  It struck against  —  wall, and his body
seemed to express a muscular and physical relief at  —  contact, as
though he had found his bearings, his location in space as well as
something against which to lean.

 — n I saw red again.  All my wrongs and humiliations flashed upon me
with a dazzling brightness, all that I had suffered and o — rs had
suffered at his hands, all  —  enormity of  —  man’s very existence.  I
sprang upon him, blindly, insanely, and drove  —  knife into his
shoulder.  I knew,  — n, that it was no more than a flesh wound,—I had
felt  —  steel grate on his shoulder-blade,—and I raised  —  knife to
strike at a more vital part.

But Maud had seen my first blow, and she cried, “Don’t!  Please don’t!”

I dropped my arm for a moment, and a moment only.  Again  —  knife was
raised, and Wolf Larsen would have surely died had she not stepped
between.  Her arms were around me, her hair was brushing my face.  My
pulse rushed up in an unwonted manner, yet my rage mounted with it.  She
looked me bravely in  —  eyes.

“For my sake,” she begged.

“I would kill him for your sake!” I cried, trying to free my arm without
hurting her.

“Hush!” she said, and laid her fingers lightly on my lips.  I could have
kissed  — m, had I dared, even  — n, in my rage,  —  touch of  — m was so
sweet, so very sweet.  “Please, please,” she pleaded, and she disarmed me
by  —  words, as I was to discover  — y would ever disarm me.

I stepped back, separating from her, and replaced  —  knife in its
sheath.  I looked at Wolf Larsen.  He still pressed his left hand against
his forehead.  It covered his eyes.  His head was bowed.  He seemed to
have grown limp.  His body was sagging at  —  hips, his great shoulders
were drooping and shrinking forward.

“Van, Weyden!” he called hoarsely, and with a note of fright in his
voice.  “Oh, Van Weyden! where are you?”

I looked at Maud.  She did not speak, but nodded her head.

“Here I am,” I answered, stepping to his side.  “What is  —  matter?”

“Help me to a seat,” he said, in  —  same hoarse, frightened voice.

“I am a sick man; a very sick man, Hump,” he said, as he left my
sustaining grip and sank into a chair.

His head dropped forward on  —  table and was buried in his hands.  From
time to time it rocked back and forward as with pain.  Once, when he half
raised it, I saw  —  sweat standing in heavy drops on his forehead about
 —  roots of his hair.

“I am a sick man, a very sick man,” he repeated again, and yet once
again.

“What is  —  matter?” I asked, resting my hand on his shoulder.  “What
can I do for you?”

But he shook my hand off with an irritated movement, and for a long time
I stood by his side in silence.  Maud was looking on, her face awed and
frightened.  What had happened to him we could not imagine.

“Hump,” he said at last, “I must get into my bunk.  Lend me a hand.  I’ll
be all right in a little while.  It’s those damn headaches, I believe.  I
was afraid of  — m.  I had a feeling—no, I don’t know what I’m talking
about.  Help me into my bunk.”

But when I got him into his bunk he again buried his face in his hands,
covering his eyes, and as I turned to go I could hear him murmuring, “I
am a sick man, a very sick man.”

Maud looked at me inquiringly as I emerged.  I shook my head, saying:

“Something has happened to him.  What, I don’t know.  He is helpless, and
frightened, I imagine, for  —  first time in his life.  It must have
occurred before he received  —  knife-thrust, which made only a
superficial wound.  You must have seen what happened.”

She shook her head.  “I saw nothing.  It is just as mysterious to me.  He
suddenly released me and staggered away.  But what shall we do?  What
shall I do?”

“If you will wait, please, until I come back,” I answered.

I went on deck.  Louis was at  —  wheel.

“You may go for’ard and turn in,” I said, taking it from him.

He was quick to obey, and I found myself alone on  —  deck of  — 
_Ghost_.  As quietly as was possible, I clewed up  —  topsails, lowered
 —  flying jib and staysail, backed  —  jib over, and flattened  — 
mainsail.   — n I went below to Maud.  I placed my finger on my lips for
silence, and entered Wolf Larsen’s room.  He was in  —  same position in
which I had left him, and his head was rocking—almost writhing—from side
to side.

“Anything I can do for you?” I asked.

He made no reply at first, but on my repeating  —  question he answered,
“No, no; I’m all right.  Leave me alone till morning.”

But as I turned to go I noted that his head had resumed its rocking
motion.  Maud was waiting patiently for me, and I took notice, with a
thrill of joy, of  —  queenly poise of her head and her glorious, calm
eyes.  Calm and sure  — y were as her spirit itself.

“Will you trust yourself to me for a journey of six hundred miles or so?”
I asked.

“You mean—?” she asked, and I knew she had guessed aright.

“Yes, I mean just that,” I replied.  “ — re is nothing left for us but
 —  open boat.”

“For me, you mean,” she said.  “You are certainly as safe here as you
have been.”

“No,  — re is nothing left for us but  —  open boat,” I iterated stoutly.
“Will you please dress as warmly as you can, at once, and make into a
bundle whatever you wish to bring with you.”

“And make all haste,” I added, as she turned toward her state-room.

 —  lazarette was directly beneath  —  cabin, and, opening  —  trap-door
in  —  floor and carrying a candle with me, I dropped down and began
overhauling  —  ship’s stores.  I selected mainly from  —  canned goods,
and by  —  time I was ready, willing hands were extended from above to
receive what I passed up.

We worked in silence.  I helped myself also to blankets, mittens,
oilskins, caps, and such things, from  —  slop-chest.  It was no light
adventure, this trusting ourselves in a small boat to so raw and stormy a
sea, and it was imperative that we should guard ourselves against  — 
cold and wet.

We worked feverishly at carrying our plunder on deck and depositing it
amidships, so feverishly that Maud, whose strength was hardly a positive
quantity, had to give over, exhausted, and sit on  —  steps at  —  break
of  —  poop.  This did not serve to recover her, and she lay on her back,
on  —  hard deck, arms stretched out, and whole body relaxed.  It was a
trick I remembered of my sister, and I knew she would soon be herself
again.  I knew, also, that weapons would not come in amiss, and I
re-entered Wolf Larsen’s state-room to get his rifle and shot-gun.  I
spoke to him, but he made no answer, though his head was still rocking
from side to side and he was not asleep.

“Good-bye, Lucifer,” I whispered to myself as I softly closed  —  door.

Next to obtain was a stock of ammunition,—an easy matter, though I had to
enter  —  steerage companion-way to do it.  Here  —  hunters stored  — 
ammunition-boxes  — y carried in  —  boats, and here, but a few feet from
 — ir noisy revels, I took possession of two boxes.

Next, to lower a boat.  Not so simple a task for one man.  Having cast
off  —  lashings, I hoisted first on  —  forward tackle,  — n on  —  aft,
till  —  boat cleared  —  rail, when I lowered away, one tackle and  — n
 —  o — r, for a couple of feet, till it hung snugly, above  —  water,
against  —  schooner’s side.  I made certain that it contained  —  proper
equipment of oars, rowlocks, and sail.  Water was a consideration, and I
robbed every boat aboard of its breaker.  As  — re were nine boats all
told, it meant that we should have plenty of water, and ballast as well,
though  — re was  —  chance that  —  boat would be overloaded, what of
 —  generous supply of o — r things I was taking.

While Maud was passing me  —  provisions and I was storing  — m in  — 
boat, a sailor came on deck from  —  forecastle.  He stood by  —  wea — r
rail for a time (we were lowering over  —  lee rail), and  — n sauntered
slowly amidships, where he again paused and stood facing  —  wind, with
his back toward us.  I could hear my heart beating as I crouched low in
 —  boat.  Maud had sunk down upon  —  deck and was, I knew, lying
motionless, her body in  —  shadow of  —  bulwark.  But  —  man never
turned, and, after stretching his arms above his head and yawning
audibly, he retraced his steps to  —  forecastle scuttle and disappeared.

A few minutes sufficed to finish  —  loading, and I lowered  —  boat into
 —  water.  As I helped Maud over  —  rail and felt her form close to
mine, it was all I could do to keep from crying out, “I love you!  I love
you!”  Truly Humphrey Van Weyden was at last in love, I thought, as her
fingers clung to mine while I lowered her down to  —  boat.  I held on to
 —  rail with one hand and supported her weight with  —  o — r, and I was
proud at  —  moment of  —  feat.  It was a strength I had not possessed a
few months before, on  —  day I said good-bye to Charley Furuseth and
started for San Francisco on  —  ill-fated _Martinez_.

As  —  boat ascended on a sea, her feet touched and I released her hands.
I cast off  —  tackles and leaped after her.  I had never rowed in my
life, but I put out  —  oars and at  —  expense of much effort got  — 
boat clear of  —  _Ghost_.   — n I experimented with  —  sail.  I had
seen  —  boat-steerers and hunters set  — ir spritsails many times, yet
this was my first attempt.  What took  — m possibly two minutes took me
twenty, but in  —  end I succeeded in setting and trimming it, and with
 —  steering-oar in my hands hauled on  —  wind.

“ — re lies Japan,” I remarked, “straight before us.”

“Humphrey Van Weyden,” she said, “you are a brave man.”

“Nay,” I answered, “it is you who are a brave woman.”

We turned our heads, swayed by a common impulse to see  —  last of  — 
_Ghost_.  Her low hull lifted and rolled to windward on a sea; her canvas
loomed darkly in  —  night; her lashed wheel creaked as  —  rudder
kicked;  — n sight and sound of her faded away, and we were alone on  — 
dark sea.




CHAPTER 27


Day broke, grey and chill.   —  boat was close-hauled on a fresh breeze
and  —  compass indicated that we were just making  —  course which would
bring us to Japan.  Though stoutly mittened, my fingers were cold, and
 — y pained from  —  grip on  —  steering-oar.  My feet were stinging
from  —  bite of  —  frost, and I hoped fervently that  —  sun would
shine.

Before me, in  —  bottom of  —  boat, lay Maud.  She, at least, was warm,
for under her and over her were thick blankets.   —  top one I had drawn
over her face to shelter it from  —  night, so I could see nothing but
 —  vague shape of her, and her light-brown hair, escaped from  — 
covering and jewelled with moisture from  —  air.

Long I looked at her, dwelling upon that one visible bit of her as only a
man would who deemed it  —  most precious thing in  —  world.  So
insistent was my gaze that at last she stirred under  —  blankets,  — 
top fold was thrown back and she smiled out on me, her eyes yet heavy
with sleep.

“Good-morning, Mr. Van Weyden,” she said.  “Have you sighted land yet?”

“No,” I answered, “but we are approaching it at a rate of six miles an
hour.”

She made a _mouè_ of disappointment.

“But that is equivalent to one hundred and forty-four miles in
twenty-four hours,” I added reassuringly.

Her face brightened.  “And how far have we to go?”

“Siberia lies off  — re,” I said, pointing to  —  west.  “But to  — 
south-west, some six hundred miles, is Japan.  If this wind should hold,
we’ll make it in five days.”

“And if it storms?   —  boat could not live?”

She had a way of looking one in  —  eyes and demanding  —  truth, and
thus she looked at me as she asked  —  question.

“It would have to storm very hard,” I temporized.

“And if it storms very hard?”

I nodded my head.  “But we may be picked up any moment by a
sealing-schooner.   — y are plentifully distributed over this part of  — 
ocean.”

“Why, you are chilled through!” she cried.  “Look!  You are shivering.
Don’t deny it; you are.  And here I have been lying warm as toast.”

“I don’t see that it would help matters if you, too, sat up and were
chilled,” I laughed.

“It will, though, when I learn to steer, which I certainly shall.”

She sat up and began making her simple toilet.  She shook down her hair,
and it fell about her in a brown cloud, hiding her face and shoulders.
Dear, damp brown hair!  I wanted to kiss it, to ripple it through my
fingers, to bury my face in it.  I gazed entranced, till  —  boat ran
into  —  wind and  —  flapping sail warned me I was not attending to my
duties.  Idealist and romanticist that I was and always had been in spite
of my analytical nature, yet I had failed till now in grasping much of
 —  physical characteristics of love.   —  love of man and woman, I had
always held, was a sublimated something related to spirit, a spiritual
bond that linked and drew  — ir souls toge — r.   —  bonds of  —  flesh
had little part in my cosmos of love.  But I was learning  —  sweet
lesson for myself that  —  soul transmuted itself, expressed itself,
through  —  flesh; that  —  sight and sense and touch of  —  loved one’s
hair was as much breath and voice and essence of  —  spirit as  —  light
that shone from  —  eyes and  —  thoughts that fell from  —  lips.  After
all, pure spirit was unknowable, a thing to be sensed and divined only;
nor could it express itself in terms of itself.  Jehovah was
anthropomorphic because he could address himself to  —  Jews only in
terms of  — ir understanding; so he was conceived as in  — ir own image,
as a cloud, a pillar of fire, a tangible, physical something which  — 
mind of  —  Israelites could grasp.

And so I gazed upon Maud’s light-brown hair, and loved it, and learned
more of love than all  —  poets and singers had taught me with all  — ir
songs and sonnets.  She flung it back with a sudden adroit movement, and
her face emerged, smiling.

“Why don’t women wear  — ir hair down always?” I asked.  “It is so much
more beautiful.”

“If it didn’t tangle so dreadfully,” she laughed.  “ — re!  I’ve lost one
of my precious hair-pins!”

I neglected  —  boat and had  —  sail spilling  —  wind again and again,
such was my delight in following her every movement as she searched
through  —  blankets for  —  pin.  I was surprised, and joyfully, that
she was so much  —  woman, and  —  display of each trait and mannerism
that was characteristically feminine gave me keener joy.  For I had been
elevating her too highly in my concepts of her, removing her too far from
 —  plane of  —  human, and too far from me.  I had been making of her a
creature goddess-like and unapproachable.  So I hailed with delight  — 
little traits that proclaimed her only woman after all, such as  —  toss
of  —  head which flung back  —  cloud of hair, and  —  search for  — 
pin.  She was woman, my kind, on my plane, and  —  delightful intimacy of
kind, of man and woman, was possible, as well as  —  reverence and awe in
which I knew I should always hold her.

She found  —  pin with an adorable little cry, and I turned my attention
more fully to my steering.  I proceeded to experiment, lashing and
wedging  —  steering-oar until  —  boat held on fairly well by  —  wind
without my assistance.  Occasionally it came up too close, or fell off
too freely; but it always recovered itself and in  —  main behaved
satisfactorily.

“And now we shall have breakfast,” I said.  “But first you must be more
warmly clad.”

I got out a heavy shirt, new from  —  slop-chest and made from blanket
goods.  I knew  —  kind, so thick and so close of texture that it could
resist  —  rain and not be soaked through after hours of wetting.  When
she had slipped this on over her head, I exchanged  —  boy’s cap she wore
for a man’s cap, large enough to cover her hair, and, when  —  flap was
turned down, to completely cover her neck and ears.   —  effect was
charming.  Her face was of  —  sort that cannot but look well under all
circumstances.  Nothing could destroy its exquisite oval, its well-nigh
classic lines, its delicately stencilled brows, its large brown eyes,
clear-seeing and calm, gloriously calm.

A puff, slightly stronger than usual, struck us just  — n.   —  boat was
caught as it obliquely crossed  —  crest of a wave.  It went over
suddenly, burying its gunwale level with  —  sea and shipping a bucketful
or so of water.  I was opening a can of tongue at  —  moment, and I
sprang to  —  sheet and cast it off just in time.   —  sail flapped and
fluttered, and  —  boat paid off.  A few minutes of regulating sufficed
to put it on its course again, when I returned to  —  preparation of
breakfast.

“It does very well, it seems, though I am not versed in things nautical,”
she said, nodding her head with grave approval at my steering
contrivance.

“But it will serve only when we are sailing by  —  wind,” I explained.
“When running more freely, with  —  wind astern abeam, or on  —  quarter,
it will be necessary for me to steer.”

“I must say I don’t understand your technicalities,” she said, “but I do
your conclusion, and I don’t like it.  You cannot steer night and day and
for ever.  So I shall expect, after breakfast, to receive my first
lesson.  And  — n you shall lie down and sleep.  We’ll stand watches just
as  — y do on ships.”

“I don’t see how I am to teach you,” I made protest.  “I am just learning
for myself.  You little thought when you trusted yourself to me that I
had had no experience whatever with small boats.  This is  —  first time
I have ever been in one.”

“ — n we’ll learn toge — r, sir.  And since you’ve had a night’s start
you shall teach me what you have learned.  And now, breakfast.  My! this
air does give one an appetite!”

“No coffee,” I said regretfully, passing her buttered sea-biscuits and a
slice of canned tongue.  “And  — re will be no tea, no soups, nothing
hot, till we have made land somewhere, somehow.”

After  —  simple breakfast, capped with a cup of cold water, Maud took
her lesson in steering.  In teaching her I learned quite a deal myself,
though I was applying  —  knowledge already acquired by sailing  — 
_Ghost_ and by watching  —  boat-steerers sail  —  small boats.  She was
an apt pupil, and soon learned to keep  —  course, to luff in  —  puffs
and to cast off  —  sheet in an emergency.

Having grown tired, apparently, of  —  task, she relinquished  —  oar to
me.  I had folded up  —  blankets, but she now proceeded to spread  — m
out on  —  bottom.  When all was arranged snugly, she said:

“Now, sir, to bed.  And you shall sleep until luncheon.  Till
dinner-time,” she corrected, remembering  —  arrangement on  —  _Ghost_.

What could I do?  She insisted, and said, “Please, please,” whereupon I
turned  —  oar over to her and obeyed.  I experienced a positive sensuous
delight as I crawled into  —  bed she had made with her hands.   —  calm
and control which were so much a part of her seemed to have been
communicated to  —  blankets, so that I was aware of a soft dreaminess
and content, and of an oval face and brown eyes framed in a fisherman’s
cap and tossing against a background now of grey cloud, now of grey sea,
and  — n I was aware that I had been asleep.

I looked at my watch.  It was one o’clock.  I had slept seven hours!  And
she had been steering seven hours!  When I took  —  steering-oar I had
first to unbend her cramped fingers.  Her modicum of strength had been
exhausted, and she was unable even to move from her position.  I was
compelled to let go  —  sheet while I helped her to  —  nest of blankets
and chafed her hands and arms.

“I am so tired,” she said, with a quick intake of  —  breath and a sigh,
drooping her head wearily.

But she straightened it  —  next moment.  “Now don’t scold, don’t you
dare scold,” she cried with mock defiance.

“I hope my face does not appear angry,” I answered seriously; “for I
assure you I am not in  —  least angry.”

“N-no,” she considered.  “It looks only reproachful.”

“ — n it is an honest face, for it looks what I feel.  You were not fair
to yourself, nor to me.  How can I ever trust you again?”

She looked penitent.  “I’ll be good,” she said, as a naughty child might
say it.  “I promise—”

“To obey as a sailor would obey his captain?”

“Yes,” she answered.  “It was stupid of me, I know.”

“ — n you must promise something else,” I ventured.

“Readily.”

“That you will not say, ‘Please, please,’ too often; for when you do you
are sure to override my authority.”

She laughed with amused appreciation.  She, too, had noticed  —  power of
 —  repeated “please.”

“It is a good word—” I began.

“But I must not overwork it,” she broke in.

But she laughed weakly, and her head drooped again.  I left  —  oar long
enough to tuck  —  blankets about her feet and to pull a single fold
across her face.  Alas! she was not strong.  I looked with misgiving
toward  —  south-west and thought of  —  six hundred miles of hardship
before us—ay, if it were no worse than hardship.  On this sea a storm
might blow up at any moment and destroy us.  And yet I was unafraid.  I
was without confidence in  —  future, extremely doubtful, and yet I felt
no underlying fear.  It must come right, it must come right, I repeated
to myself, over and over again.

 —  wind freshened in  —  afternoon, raising a stiffer sea and trying  — 
boat and me severely.  But  —  supply of food and  —  nine breakers of
water enabled  —  boat to stand up to  —  sea and wind, and I held on as
long as I dared.   — n I removed  —  sprit, tightly hauling down  —  peak
of  —  sail, and we raced along under what sailors call a leg-of-mutton.

Late in  —  afternoon I sighted a steamer’s smoke on  —  horizon to
leeward, and I knew it ei — r for a Russian cruiser, or, more likely,  — 
_Macedonia_ still seeking  —  _Ghost_.   —  sun had not shone all day,
and it had been bitter cold.  As night drew on,  —  clouds darkened and
 —  wind freshened, so that when Maud and I ate supper it was with our
mittens on and with me still steering and eating morsels between puffs.

By  —  time it was dark, wind and sea had become too strong for  —  boat,
and I reluctantly took in  —  sail and set about making a drag or
sea-anchor.  I had learned of  —  device from  —  talk of  —  hunters,
and it was a simple thing to manufacture.  Furling  —  sail and lashing
it securely about  —  mast, boom, sprit, and two pairs of spare oars, I
threw it overboard.  A line connected it with  —  bow, and as it floated
low in  —  water, practically unexposed to  —  wind, it drifted less
rapidly than  —  boat.  In consequence it held  —  boat bow on to  —  sea
and wind— —  safest position in which to escape being swamped when  — 
sea is breaking into whitecaps.

“And now?” Maud asked cheerfully, when  —  task was accomplished and I
pulled on my mittens.

“And now we are no longer travelling toward Japan,” I answered.  “Our
drift is to  —  south-east, or south-south-east, at  —  rate of at least
two miles an hour.”

“That will be only twenty-four miles,” she urged, “if  —  wind remains
high all night.”

“Yes, and only one hundred and forty miles if it continues for three days
and nights.”

“But it won’t continue,” she said with easy confidence.  “It will turn
around and blow fair.”

“ —  sea is  —  great faithless one.”

“But  —  wind!” she retorted.  “I have heard you grow eloquent over  — 
brave trade-wind.”

“I wish I had thought to bring Wolf Larsen’s chronometer and sextant,” I
said, still gloomily.  “Sailing one direction, drifting ano — r
direction, to say nothing of  —  set of  —  current in some third
direction, makes a resultant which dead reckoning can never calculate.
Before long we won’t know where we are by five hundred miles.”

 — n I begged her pardon and promised I should not be disheartened any
more.  At her solicitation I let her take  —  watch till midnight,—it was
 — n nine o’clock, but I wrapped her in blankets and put an oilskin about
her before I lay down.  I slept only cat-naps.   —  boat was leaping and
pounding as it fell over  —  crests, I could hear  —  seas rushing past,
and spray was continually being thrown aboard.  And still, it was not a
bad night, I mused—nothing to  —  nights I had been through on  — 
_Ghost_; nothing, perhaps, to  —  nights we should go through in this
cockle-shell.  Its planking was three-quarters of an inch thick.  Between
us and  —  bottom of  —  sea was less than an inch of wood.

And yet, I aver it, and I aver it again, I was unafraid.   —  death which
Wolf Larsen and even Thomas Mugridge had made me fear, I no longer
feared.   —  coming of Maud Brewster into my life seemed to have
transformed me.  After all, I thought, it is better and finer to love
than to be loved, if it makes something in life so worth while that one
is not loath to die for it.  I forget my own life in  —  love of ano — r
life; and yet, such is  —  paradox, I never wanted so much to live as
right now when I place  —  least value upon my own life.  I never had so
much reason for living, was my concluding thought; and after that, until
I dozed, I contented myself with trying to pierce  —  darkness to where I
knew Maud crouched low in  —  stern-sheets, watchful of  —  foaming sea
and ready to call me on an instant’s notice.




CHAPTER 28


 — re is no need of going into an extended recital of our suffering in
 —  small boat during  —  many days we were driven and drifted, here and
 — re, willy-nilly, across  —  ocean.   —  high wind blew from  — 
north-west for twenty-four hours, when it fell calm, and in  —  night
sprang up from  —  south-west.  This was dead in our teeth, but I took in
 —  sea-anchor and set sail, hauling a course on  —  wind which took us
in a south-south-easterly direction.  It was an even choice between this
and  —  west-north-westerly course which  —  wind permitted; but  —  warm
airs of  —  south fanned my desire for a warmer sea and swayed my
decision.

In three hours—it was midnight, I well remember, and as dark as I had
ever seen it on  —  sea— —  wind, still blowing out of  —  south-west,
rose furiously, and once again I was compelled to set  —  sea-anchor.

Day broke and found me wan-eyed and  —  ocean lashed white,  —  boat
pitching, almost on end, to its drag.  We were in imminent danger of
being swamped by  —  whitecaps.  As it was, spray and spume came aboard
in such quantities that I bailed without cessation.   —  blankets were
soaking.  Everything was wet except Maud, and she, in oilskins, rubber
boots, and sou’wester, was dry, all but her face and hands and a stray
wisp of hair.  She relieved me at  —  bailing-hole from time to time, and
bravely she threw out  —  water and faced  —  storm.  All things are
relative.  It was no more than a stiff blow, but to us, fighting for life
in our frail craft, it was indeed a storm.

Cold and cheerless,  —  wind beating on our faces,  —  white seas roaring
by, we struggled through  —  day.  Night came, but nei — r of us slept.
Day came, and still  —  wind beat on our faces and  —  white seas roared
past.  By  —  second night Maud was falling asleep from exhaustion.  I
covered her with oilskins and a tarpaulin.  She was comparatively dry,
but she was numb with  —  cold.  I feared greatly that she might die in
 —  night; but day broke, cold and cheerless, with  —  same clouded sky
and beating wind and roaring seas.

I had had no sleep for forty-eight hours.  I was wet and chilled to  — 
marrow, till I felt more dead than alive.  My body was stiff from
exertion as well as from cold, and my aching muscles gave me  —  severest
torture whenever I used  — m, and I used  — m continually.  And all  — 
time we were being driven off into  —  north-east, directly away from
Japan and toward bleak Bering Sea.

And still we lived, and  —  boat lived, and  —  wind blew unabated.  In
fact, toward nightfall of  —  third day it increased a trifle and
something more.   —  boat’s bow plunged under a crest, and we came
through quarter-full of water.  I bailed like a madman.   —  liability of
shipping ano — r such sea was enormously increased by  —  water that
weighed  —  boat down and robbed it of its buoyancy.  And ano — r such
sea meant  —  end.  When I had  —  boat empty again I was forced to take
away  —  tarpaulin which covered Maud, in order that I might lash it down
across  —  bow.  It was well I did, for it covered  —  boat fully a third
of  —  way aft, and three times, in  —  next several hours, it flung off
 —  bulk of  —  down-rushing water when  —  bow shoved under  —  seas.

Maud’s condition was pitiable.  She sat crouched in  —  bottom of  — 
boat, her lips blue, her face grey and plainly showing  —  pain she
suffered.  But ever her eyes looked bravely at me, and ever her lips
uttered brave words.

 —  worst of  —  storm must have blown that night, though little I
noticed it.  I had succumbed and slept where I sat in  —  stern-sheets.
 —  morning of  —  fourth day found  —  wind diminished to a gentle
whisper,  —  sea dying down and  —  sun shining upon us.  Oh,  —  blessed
sun!  How we ba — d our poor bodies in its delicious warmth, reviving
like bugs and crawling things after a storm.  We smiled again, said
amusing things, and waxed optimistic over our situation.  Yet it was, if
anything, worse than ever.  We were far — r from Japan than  —  night we
left  —  _Ghost_.  Nor could I more than roughly guess our latitude and
longitude.  At a calculation of a two-mile drift per hour, during  — 
seventy and odd hours of  —  storm, we had been driven at least one
hundred and fifty miles to  —  north-east.  But was such calculated drift
correct?  For all I knew, it might have been four miles per hour instead
of two.  In which case we were ano — r hundred and fifty miles to  — 
bad.

Where we were I did not know, though  — re was quite a likelihood that we
were in  —  vicinity of  —  _Ghost_.   — re were seals about us, and I
was prepared to sight a sealing-schooner at any time.  We did sight one,
in  —  afternoon, when  —  north-west breeze had sprung up freshly once
more.  But  —  strange schooner lost itself on  —  sky-line and we alone
occupied  —  circle of  —  sea.

Came days of fog, when even Maud’s spirit drooped and  — re were no merry
words upon her lips; days of calm, when we floated on  —  lonely
immensity of sea, oppressed by its greatness and yet marvelling at  — 
miracle of tiny life, for we still lived and struggled to live; days of
sleet and wind and snow-squalls, when nothing could keep us warm; or days
of drizzling rain, when we filled our water-breakers from  —  drip of  — 
wet sail.

And ever I loved Maud with an increasing love.  She was so many-sided, so
many-mooded—“protean-mooded” I called her.  But I called her this, and
o — r and dearer things, in my thoughts only.  Though  —  declaration of
my love urged and trembled on my tongue a thousand times, I knew that it
was no time for such a declaration.  If for no o — r reason, it was no
time, when one was protecting and trying to save a woman, to ask that
woman for her love.  Delicate as was  —  situation, not alone in this but
in o — r ways, I flattered myself that I was able to deal delicately with
it; and also I flattered myself that by look or sign I gave no
advertisement of  —  love I felt for her.  We were like good comrades,
and we grew better comrades as  —  days went by.

One thing about her which surprised me was her lack of timidity and fear.
 —  terrible sea,  —  frail boat,  —  storms,  —  suffering,  — 
strangeness and isolation of  —  situation,—all that should have
frightened a robust woman,—seemed to make no impression upon her who had
known life only in its most sheltered and consummately artificial
aspects, and who was herself all fire and dew and mist, sublimated
spirit, all that was soft and tender and clinging in woman.  And yet I am
wrong.  She _was_ timid and afraid, but she possessed courage.   —  flesh
and  —  qualms of  —  flesh she was heir to, but  —  flesh bore heavily
only on  —  flesh.  And she was spirit, first and always spirit,
e — realized essence of life, calm as her calm eyes, and sure of
permanence in  —  changing order of  —  universe.

Came days of storm, days and nights of storm, when  —  ocean menaced us
with its roaring whiteness, and  —  wind smote our struggling boat with a
Titan’s buffets.  And ever we were flung off, far — r and far — r, to  — 
north-east.  It was in such a storm, and  —  worst that we had
experienced, that I cast a weary glance to leeward, not in quest of
anything, but more from  —  weariness of facing  —  elemental strife, and
in mute appeal, almost, to  —  wrathful powers to cease and let us be.
What I saw I could not at first believe.  Days and nights of
sleeplessness and anxiety had doubtless turned my head.  I looked back at
Maud, to identify myself, as it were, in time and space.   —  sight of
her dear wet cheeks, her flying hair, and her brave brown eyes convinced
me that my vision was still healthy.  Again I turned my face to leeward,
and again I saw  —  jutting promontory, black and high and naked,  — 
raging surf that broke about its base and beat its front high up with
spouting fountains,  —  black and forbidden coast-line running toward  — 
south-east and fringed with a tremendous scarf of white.

“Maud,” I said.  “Maud.”

She turned her head and beheld  —  sight.

“It cannot be Alaska!” she cried.

“Alas, no,” I answered, and asked, “Can you swim?”

She shook her head.

“Nei — r can I,” I said.  “So we must get ashore without swimming, in
some opening between  —  rocks through which we can drive  —  boat and
clamber out.  But we must be quick, most quick—and sure.”

I spoke with a confidence she knew I did not feel, for she looked at me
with that unfaltering gaze of hers and said:

“I have not thanked you yet for all you have done for me but—”

She hesitated, as if in doubt how best to word her gratitude.

“Well?” I said, brutally, for I was not quite pleased with her thanking
me.

“You might help me,” she smiled.

“To acknowledge your obligations before you die?  Not at all.  We are not
going to die.  We shall land on that island, and we shall be snug and
sheltered before  —  day is done.”

I spoke stoutly, but I did not believe a word.  Nor was I prompted to lie
through fear.  I felt no fear, though I was sure of death in that boiling
surge amongst  —  rocks which was rapidly growing nearer.  It was
impossible to hoist sail and claw off that shore.   —  wind would
instantly capsize  —  boat;  —  seas would swamp it  —  moment it fell
into  —  trough; and, besides,  —  sail, lashed to  —  spare oars,
dragged in  —  sea ahead of us.

As I say, I was not afraid to meet my own death,  — re, a few hundred
yards to leeward; but I was appalled at  —  thought that Maud must die.
My cursed imagination saw her beaten and mangled against  —  rocks, and
it was too terrible.  I strove to compel myself to think we would make
 —  landing safely, and so I spoke, not what I believed, but what I
preferred to believe.

I recoiled before contemplation of that frightful death, and for a moment
I entertained  —  wild idea of seizing Maud in my arms and leaping
overboard.   — n I resolved to wait, and at  —  last moment, when we
entered on  —  final stretch, to take her in my arms and proclaim my
love, and, with her in my embrace, to make  —  desperate struggle and
die.

Instinctively we drew closer toge — r in  —  bottom of  —  boat.  I felt
her mittened hand come out to mine.  And thus, without speech, we waited
 —  end.  We were not far off  —  line  —  wind made with  —  western
edge of  —  promontory, and I watched in  —  hope that some set of  — 
current or send of  —  sea would drift us past before we reached  — 
surf.

“We shall go clear,” I said, with a confidence which I knew deceived
nei — r of us.

“By God, we _will_ go clear!” I cried, five minutes later.

 —  oath left my lips in my excitement— —  first, I do believe, in my
life, unless “trouble it,” an expletive of my youth, be accounted an
oath.

“I beg your pardon,” I said.

“You have convinced me of your sincerity,” she said, with a faint smile.
“I do know, now, that we shall go clear.”

I had seen a distant headland past  —  extreme edge of  —  promontory,
and as we looked we could see grow  —  intervening coastline of what was
evidently a deep cove.  At  —  same time  — re broke upon our ears a
continuous and mighty bellowing.  It partook of  —  magnitude and volume
of distant thunder, and it came to us directly from leeward, rising above
 —  crash of  —  surf and travelling directly in  —  teeth of  —  storm.
As we passed  —  point  —  whole cove burst upon our view, a half-moon of
white sandy beach upon which broke a huge surf, and which was covered
with myriads of seals.  It was from  — m that  —  great bellowing went
up.

“A rookery!” I cried.  “Now are we indeed saved.   — re must be men and
cruisers to protect  — m from  —  seal-hunters.  Possibly  — re is a
station ashore.”

But as I studied  —  surf which beat upon  —  beach, I said, “Still bad,
but not so bad.  And now, if  —  gods be truly kind, we shall drift by
that next headland and come upon a perfectly sheltered beach, where we
may land without wetting our feet.”

And  —  gods were kind.   —  first and second headlands were directly in
line with  —  south-west wind; but once around  —  second,—and we went
perilously near,—we picked up  —  third headland, still in line with  — 
wind and with  —  o — r two.  But  —  cove that intervened!  It
penetrated deep into  —  land, and  —  tide, setting in, drifted us under
 —  shelter of  —  point.  Here  —  sea was calm, save for a heavy but
smooth ground-swell, and I took in  —  sea-anchor and began to row.  From
 —  point  —  shore curved away, more and more to  —  south and west,
until at last it disclosed a cove within  —  cove, a little land-locked
harbour,  —  water level as a pond, broken only by tiny ripples where
vagrant breaths and wisps of  —  storm hurtled down from over  — 
frowning wall of rock that backed  —  beach a hundred feet inshore.

Here were no seals whatever.   —  boat’s stern touched  —  hard shingle.
I sprang out, extending my hand to Maud.   —  next moment she was beside
me.  As my fingers released hers, she clutched for my arm hastily.  At
 —  same moment I swayed, as about to fall to  —  sand.  This was  — 
startling effect of  —  cessation of motion.  We had been so long upon
 —  moving, rocking sea that  —  stable land was a shock to us.  We
expected  —  beach to lift up this way and that, and  —  rocky walls to

non-occurrence quite overcame our equilibrium.

“I really must sit down,” Maud said, with a nervous laugh and a dizzy
gesture, and forthwith she sat down on  —  sand.

I attended to making  —  boat secure and joined her.  Thus we landed on
Endeavour Island, as we came to it, land-sick from long custom of  — 
sea.




CHAPTER 29


“Fool!” I cried aloud in my vexation.

I had unloaded  —  boat and carried its contents high up on  —  beach,
where I had set about making a camp.   — re was driftwood, though not
much, on  —  beach, and  —  sight of a coffee tin I had taken from  — 
_Ghost’s_ larder had given me  —  idea of a fire.

But Maud said, “Tut, tut,” in gentle reproval, and  — n asked why I was a
bli — ring idiot.

“No matches,” I groaned.  “Not a match did I bring.  And now we shall

I confess, I wanted  —  coffee badly; and I learned, not long afterward,

I had looked upon it as a simple task, what of  —  oars, mast, boom, and

“It would be better if you remained.  You have had enough of hardship.

Something suspiciously akin to moistness dimmed her beautiful eyes before

“I should prefer going with you,” she said in a low voice, in which  — re

“I might be able to help you a—” her voice broke,—“a little.  And if

“Oh, I intend being very careful,” I answered.  “And I shall not go so

She turned and looked me in  —  eyes.  Her gaze was unfaltering, but

“Please, please,” she said, oh, so softly.

I stiffened myself to refuse, and shook my head.  Still she waited and

 —  wind died down in  —  afternoon, and we were prepared to start  —

Morning broke dull and grey, but calm, and I was awake early and had  —
