“Fool!  Imbecile!  Yahoo!” I shouted, when I thought it was meet to

Her head appeared under  —  flap of  —  sail.

“What now?” she asked sleepily, and, withal, curiously.

“Coffee!” I cried.  “What do you say to a cup of coffee? hot coffee?
piping hot?”

“My!” she murmured, “you startled me, and you are cruel.  Here I have
been composing my soul to do without it, and here you are vexing me with

“Watch me,” I said.

From under clefts among  —  rocks I ga — red a few dry sticks and chips.
 — se I whittled into shavings or split into kindling.  From my note-book

Maud still watched from  —  tent.  Holding  —  paper in my left hand, I

Maud clapped her hands gleefully.  “Prome — us!” she cried.

But I was too occupied to acknowledge her delight.   —  feeble flame must

I boiled  —  water, but it was Maud who made  —  coffee.  And how good it

I was confident that we should find a station in some one of  —  coves,

This brief description is all that Endeavour Island merits.  Damp and
soggy where it was not sharp and rocky, buffeted by storm winds and

It was my turn to be cheerful, and I played  —  part to  —  best of my

I still slept in  —  boat, and I lay awake long that night, gazing up at

Responsibility of this sort was a new thing to me.  Wolf Larsen had been
quite right.  I had stood on my fa — r’s legs.  My lawyers and agents had
taken care of my money for me.  I had had no responsibilities at all.
 — n, on  —  _Ghost_ I had learned to be responsible for myself.  And
now, for  —  first time in my life, I found myself responsible for some
one else.  And it was required of me that this should be  —  gravest of
responsibilities, for she was  —  one woman in  —  world— —  one small

CHAPTER 30


No wonder we called it Endeavour Island.  For two weeks we toiled at
building a hut.  Maud insisted on helping, and I could have wept over her
bruised and bleeding hands.  And still, I was proud of her because of it.
 — re was something heroic about this gently-bred woman enduring our

begged her to desist.  She compromised, however, by taking upon herself

cover  — m?  Moss would never do.  Tundra grass was impracticable.  We
needed  —  sail for  —  boat, and  —  tarpaulin had begun to leak.

“Winters used walrus skins on his hut,” I said.

“ — re are  —  seals,” she suggested.

So next day  —  hunting began.  I did not know how to shoot, but I

“We must club  —  seals,” I announced, when convinced of my poor

“ — y are so pretty,” she objected.  “I cannot bear to think of it being

“That’s just it,” she began eagerly, and broke off in sudden confusion.

“Of course,” I began, “if you prefer—”

“Ga — ring firewood and cooking dinner,” I answered lightly.

She shook her head.  “It is too dangerous for you to attempt alone.”

“I know, I know,” she waived my protest.  “I am only a weak woman, but
just my small assistance may enable you to escape disaster.”

“But  —  clubbing?” I suggested.

“Of course, you will do that.  I shall probably scream.  I’ll look away

“Yes,  —  geese.  My bro — r told me about it when I was a little girl.”

Far from her intention, her words were maddening me, driving me on.  I

She made no reply, and I knew my tone must have hurt her.

He swam directly to  —  beach and clambered out into a small opening

She had stepped out beside me, and I regarded her with wonderment.

“You know it won’t,” was her reply.  “Shall I lead?”

With a shrug of  —  shoulders, but with  —  warmest admiration and pride

In  —  very heart of  —  herd  —  din was terrific.  It was almost

“I’m dreadfully afraid!”

And I was not.  Though  —  novelty had not yet worn off,  —  peaceful
comportment of  —  seals had quieted my alarm.  Maud was trembling.

“I’m afraid, and I’m not afraid,” she chattered with shaking jaws.  “It’s
my miserable body, not I.”

“It’s all right, it’s all right,” I reassured her, my arm passing

I shall never forget, in that moment, how instantly conscious I became of
my manhood.   —  primitive deeps of my nature stirred.  I felt myself

upon me, that I should have met it unflinchingly and quite coolly, and I
know that I should have killed it.

“I am all right now,” she said, looking up at me gratefully.  “Let us go

And that  —  strength in me had quieted her and given her confidence,

A quarter of a mile inland we came upon  —  holluschickie—sleek young

Everything now went smoothly.  I seemed to know just what to do and how

“My, it’s exciting!” she cried, pausing from sheer weakness.  “I think
I’ll sit down.”

o — r tack made our own little inner cove.

“It’s just like home-coming,” Maud said, as I ran  —  boat ashore.

I heard her words with a responsive thrill, it was all so dearly intimate

“It seems as though I have lived this life always.   —  world of books
and bookish folk is very vague, more like a dream memory than an
actuality.  I surely have hunted and forayed and fought all  —  days of

hardship well.”

But her ear had caught  —  flaw.  She recognized a flight that midmost
broke.  She gave me a quick look.

“Not that.  You were saying—?”

“That  —  American Mrs. Meynell was living  —  life of a savage and
living it quite successfully,” I said easily.

“Oh,” was all she replied; but I could have sworn  — re was a note of
disappointment in her voice.

But “my woman, my mate” kept ringing in my head for  —  rest of  —  day

CHAPTER 31


“It will smell,” I said, “but it will keep in  —  heat and keep out  — 
rain and snow.”

We were surveying  —  completed seal-skin roof.

“It is clumsy, but it will serve  —  purpose, and that is  —  main
thing,” I went on, yearning for her praise.

And she clapped her hands and declared that she was hugely pleased.

“But it is dark in here,” she said  —  next moment, her shoulders
shrinking with a little involuntary shiver.

“You might have suggested a window when  —  walls were going up,” I said.
“It was for you, and you should have seen  —  need of a window.”

“But I never do see  —  obvious, you know,” she laughed back.  “And
besides, you can knock a hole in  —  wall at any time.”

“Quite true; I had not thought of it,” I replied, wagging my head sagely.
“But have you thought of ordering  —  window-glass?  Just call up  — 
firm,—Red, 4451, I think it is,—and tell  — m what size and kind of glass
you wish.”

“That means—” she began.

“No window.”

It was a dark and evil-appearing thing, that hut, not fit for aught
better than swine in a civilized land; but for us, who had known  — 
misery of  —  open boat, it was a snug little habitation.  Following  —

 —  second hut was easier to erect, for I built it against  —  first, and
only three walls were required.  But it was work, hard work, all of it.

obtained this strength was  —  marvel to me.

“Think of  —  long rest this winter,” was her reply to my remonstrances.
“Why, we’ll be clamorous for something to do.”

We held a housewarming in my hut  —  night it was roofed.  It was  —  end
of  —  third day of a fierce storm which had swung around  —  compass
from  —  south-east to  —  north-west, and which was  — n blowing
directly in upon us.   —  beaches of  —  outer cove were thundering with

It was a pleasant evening indeed, and we voted that as a social function
on Endeavour Island it had not yet been eclipsed.  Our minds were at
ease.  Not only had we resigned ourselves to  —  bitter winter, but we
were prepared for it.   —  seals could depart on  — ir mysterious journey

jealously ga — red all  —  moss.  This was to be my first night on  — 
mattress, and I knew I should sleep  —  sweeter because she had made it.

As she rose to go she turned to me with  —  whimsical way she had, and
said:

“Something is going to happen—is happening, for that matter.  I feel it.
Something is coming here, to us.  It is coming now.  I don’t know what,
but it is coming.”

“Good or bad?” I asked.

She shook her head.  “I don’t know, but it is  — re, somewhere.”

She pointed in  —  direction of  —  sea and wind.

“It’s a lee shore,” I laughed, “and I am sure I’d ra — r be here than
arriving, a night like this.”

“You are not frightened?” I asked, as I stepped to open  —  door for her.

Her eyes looked bravely into mine.

“And you feel well? perfectly well?”

“Never better,” was her answer.

We talked a little longer before she went.

“Good-night, Maud,” I said.

“Good-night, Humphrey,” she said.

This use of our given names had come about quite as a matter of course,

existed before.




CHAPTER 32

CHAPTER 33


We waited all day for Wolf Larsen to come ashore.  It was an intolerable
period of anxiety.  Each moment one or  —  o — r of us cast expectant

“Perhaps it is his headache,” I said.  “I left him lying on  —  poop.  He
may lie  — re all night.  I think I’ll go and see.”

Maud looked entreaty at me.

“But  — re are his arms, his hands, his terrible, terrible hands!” she

She rested her hand appealingly on mine, and sent my pulse fluttering.

CHAPTER 34


“It’s too bad  —  _Ghost_ has lost her masts.  Why we could sail away in
her.  Don’t you think we could, Humphrey?”

CHAPTER 35


Next day,  —  mast-steps clear and everything in readiness, we started to

I had forgotten  — ir existence.  I looked again, but could not see  — m.

She put her hand sympa — tically on mine, and said, “You will have to

“He deserves to die,” I cried out; “and God forgive me, I am not man

But Maud was by my side, passing her hand soothingly through my hair as

We are in  —  right, and it must come right.”

I remembered Michelet and leaned my head against her; and truly I became

“Here he comes now,” she whispered.

I glanced up.  He was strolling leisurely along  —  poop on  —  port

“Take no notice of him,” I whispered.  “He’s coming to see how we take

And  — n we played hide-and-seek with  —  blind man.  As he came up  —

He must have known, somehow, that we were on board, for he said

It reminded me of  —  great hoot-owl, listening, after its booming cry,

CHAPTER 36


For two days Maud and I ranged  —  sea and explored  —  beaches in search

CHAPTER 37


At once we moved aboard  —  _Ghost_, occupying our old state-rooms and
cooking in  —  galley.   —  imprisonment of Wolf Larsen had happened most
opportunely, for what must have been  —  Indian summer of this high

And now that we had Wolf Larsen in irons, how little did we need it!
Like his first attack, his second had been accompanied by serious

“Do you know you are deaf in  —  right ear?” I asked.

“Yes,” he answered in a low, strong voice, “and worse than that.  My

He shook his head, his stern mouth shaping  —  strangest, twisted smile.

“Oh, just to be alive, to be living and doing, to be  —  biggest bit of

He shrugged his shoulders, or attempted to shrug  — m, ra — r, for  —

He nodded his head.  “ — re is no accounting for it.  I was never sick in

“Bosh!” was his retort.  “It simply means that in  —  attack on my brain

and reason.  When that goes, I go.  I am not.   —  soul?”

He broke out in mocking laughter,  — n turned his left ear to  —  pillow
as a sign that he wished no fur — r conversation.

Maud and I went about our work oppressed by  —  fearful fate which had

“You might remove  —  handcuffs,” he said that night, as we stood in

He smiled his twisted smile, and Maud, her eyes wide with horror, was

And for  —  space of several minutes he lay  — re, quiet, indulging his

And yet, though I even removed  —  handcuffs, we could not adjust

I had solved  —  problem which had arisen through  —  shortness of  —

He had received ano — r stroke.  He had lost his voice, or he was losing

 —  fierce winter had now descended upon us.  Gale followed gale, with

“A fig for superstition,” I said on Friday morning.  “That mast goes in

 — n she assumed a rueful expression.

“And yet I am all here, Hump,” he wrote with a final flourish.  “I can

It was like a message from  —  night of  —  grave; for this man’s body

CHAPTER 38


“I think my left side is going,” Wolf Larsen wrote,  —  morning after his
attempt to fire  —  ship.  “ —  numbness is growing.  I can hardly move
my hand.  You will have to speak louder.   —  last lines are going down.”

“Are you in pain?” I asked.

I was compelled to repeat my question loudly before he answered:

“Not all  —  time.”

 —  left hand stumbled slowly and painfully across  —  paper, and it was

“Yes,” I answered.  “Mine.  I made it.”

With  —  foremast in,  —  work now went on apace.  Almost before I knew

Certainly, among my many new trades, I shone least as a sail-maker.  I

 —  last line was down.  Somewhere within that tomb of  —  flesh still

CHAPTER 39


 —  day came for our departure.   — re was no longer anything to detain

“I did it!  I did it!  With my own hands I did it!” I wanted to cry

Lashing  —  wheel I ran forward, eased  —  fore and mainsheets, took in
on  —  boom-tackles and trimmed everything for  —  quartering breeze
which was ours.  It was a fresh breeze, very fresh, but I resolved to run
as long as I dared.  Unfortunately, when running free, it is impossible
to lash  —  wheel, so I faced an all-night watch.  Maud insisted on
relieving me, but proved that she had not  —  strength to steer in a
heavy sea, even if she could have gained  —  wisdom on such short notice.
She appeared quite heart-broken over  —  discovery, but recovered her
spirits by coiling down tackles and halyards and all stray ropes.   — n
 — re were meals to be cooked in  —  galley, beds to make, Wolf Larsen to
be attended upon, and she finished  —  day with a grand house-cleaning
attack upon  —  cabin and steerage.

All night I steered, without relief,  —  wind slowly and steadily
increasing and  —  sea rising.  At five in  —  morning Maud brought me
hot coffee and biscuits she had baked, and at seven a substantial and
piping hot breakfast put new lift into me.

Throughout  —  day, and as slowly and steadily as ever,  —  wind
increased.  It impressed one with its sullen determination to blow, and
blow harder, and keep on blowing.  And still  —  _Ghost_ foamed along,
racing off  —  miles till I was certain she was making at least eleven
knots.  It was too good to lose, but by nightfall I was exhausted.
Though in splendid physical trim, a thirty-six-hour trick at  —  wheel
was  —  limit of my endurance.  Besides, Maud begged me to heave to, and
I knew, if  —  wind and sea increased at  —  same rate during  —  night,
that it would soon be impossible to heave to.  So, as twilight deepened,
gladly and at  —  same time reluctantly, I brought  —  _Ghost_ up on  — 
wind.

But I had not reckoned upon  —  colossal task  —  reefing of three sails
meant for one man.  While running away from  —  wind I had not
appreciated its force, but when we ceased to run I learned to my sorrow,
and well-nigh to my despair, how fiercely it was really blowing.   — 
wind balked my every effort, ripping  —  canvas out of my hands and in an
instant undoing what I had gained by ten minutes of severest struggle.
At eight o’clock I had succeeded only in putting  —  second reef into  — 
foresail.  At eleven o’clock I was no far — r along.  Blood dripped from
every finger-end, while  —  nails were broken to  —  quick.  From pain
and sheer exhaustion I wept in  —  darkness, secretly, so that Maud
should not know.

 — n, in desperation, I abandoned  —  attempt to reef  —  mainsail and
resolved to try  —  experiment of heaving to under  —  close-reefed
foresail.  Three hours more were required to gasket  —  mainsail and jib,
and at two in  —  morning, nearly dead,  —  life almost buffeted and
worked out of me, I had barely sufficient consciousness to know  — 
experiment was a success.   —  close-reefed foresail worked.   —  _Ghost_
clung on close to  —  wind and betrayed no inclination to fall off
broadside to  —  trough.

I was famished, but Maud tried vainly to get me to eat.  I dozed with my
mouth full of food.  I would fall asleep in  —  act of carrying food to
my mouth and waken in torment to find  —  act yet uncompleted.  So
sleepily helpless was I that she was compelled to hold me in my chair to
prevent my being flung to  —  floor by  —  violent pitching of  — 
schooner.

Of  —  passage from  —  galley to  —  cabin I knew nothing.  It was a
sleep-walker Maud guided and supported.  In fact, I was aware of nothing
till I awoke, how long after I could not imagine, in my bunk with my
boots off.  It was dark.  I was stiff and lame, and cried out with pain
when  —  bed-clo — s touched my poor finger-ends.

Morning had evidently not come, so I closed my eyes and went to sleep
again.  I did not know it, but I had slept  —  clock around and it was
night again.

Once more I woke, troubled because I could sleep no better.  I struck a
match and looked at my watch.  It marked midnight.  And I had not left
 —  deck until three!  I should have been puzzled had I not guessed  — 
solution.  No wonder I was sleeping brokenly.  I had slept twenty-one
hours.  I listened for a while to  —  behaviour of  —  _Ghost_, to  — 
pounding of  —  seas and  —  muffled roar of  —  wind on deck, and  — n
turned over on my side and slept peacefully until morning.

When I arose at seven I saw no sign of Maud and concluded she was in  — 
galley preparing breakfast.  On deck I found  —  _Ghost_ doing splendidly
under her patch of canvas.  But in  —  galley, though a fire was burning
and water boiling, I found no Maud.

I discovered her in  —  steerage, by Wolf Larsen’s bunk.  I looked at
him,  —  man who had been hurled down from  —  topmost pitch of life to
be buried alive and be worse than dead.   — re seemed a relaxation of his
expressionless face which was new.  Maud looked at me and I understood.

“His life flickered out in  —  storm,” I said.

“But he still lives,” she answered, infinite faith in her voice.

“He had too great strength.”

“Yes,” she said, “but now it no longer shackles him.  He is a free
spirit.”

“He is a free spirit surely,” I answered; and, taking her hand, I led her
on deck.

 —  storm broke that night, which is to say that it diminished as slowly
as it had arisen.  After breakfast next morning, when I had hoisted Wolf
Larsen’s body on deck ready for burial, it was still blowing heavily and
a large sea was running.   —  deck was continually awash with  —  sea
which came inboard over  —  rail and through  —  scuppers.   —  wind
smote  —  schooner with a sudden gust, and she heeled over till her lee
rail was buried,  —  roar in her rigging rising in pitch to a shriek.  We
stood in  —  water to our knees as I bared my head.

“I remember only one part of  —  service,” I said, “and that is, ‘And  — 
body shall be cast into  —  sea.’”

Maud looked at me, surprised and shocked; but  —  spirit of something I
had seen before was strong upon me, impelling me to give service to Wolf
Larsen as Wolf Larsen had once given service to ano — r man.  I lifted
 —  end of  —  hatch cover and  —  canvas-shrouded body slipped feet
first into  —  sea.   —  weight of iron dragged it down.  It was gone.

“Good-bye, Lucifer, proud spirit,” Maud whispered, so low that it was
drowned by  —  shouting of  —  wind; but I saw  —  movement of her lips
and knew.

As we clung to  —  lee rail and worked our way aft, I happened to glance
to leeward.   —  _Ghost_, at  —  moment, was uptossed on a sea, and I
caught a clear view of a small steamship two or three miles away, rolling
and pitching, head on to  —  sea, as it steamed toward us.  It was
painted black, and from  —  talk of  —  hunters of  — ir poaching
exploits I recognized it as a United States revenue cutter.  I pointed it
out to Maud and hurriedly led her aft to  —  safety of  —  poop.

I started to rush below to  —  flag-locker,  — n remembered that in
rigging  —  _Ghost_.  I had forgotten to make provision for a
flag-halyard.

“We need no distress signal,” Maud said.  “ — y have only to see us.”

“We are saved,” I said, soberly and solemnly.  And  — n, in an exuberance
of joy, “I hardly know whe — r to be glad or not.”

I looked at her.  Our eyes were not loath to meet.  We leaned toward each
o — r, and before I knew it my arms were about her.

“Need I?” I asked.

And she answered, “ — re is no need, though  —  telling of it would be
sweet, so sweet.”

Her lips met  —  press of mine, and, by what strange trick of  — 
imagination I know not,  —  scene in  —  cabin of  —  _Ghost_ flashed
upon me, when she had pressed her fingers lightly on my lips and said,
“Hush, hush.”

“My woman, my one small woman,” I said, my free hand petting her shoulder
in  —  way all lovers know though never learn in school.

“My man,” she said, looking at me for an instant with tremulous lids
which fluttered down and veiled her eyes as she snuggled her head against
my breast with a happy little sigh.

I looked toward  —  cutter.  It was very close.  A boat was being
lowered.

“One kiss, dear love,” I whispered.  “One kiss more before  — y come.”

“And rescue us from ourselves,” she completed, with a most adorable
smile, whimsical as I had never seen it, for it was whimsical with love.
